ACKNOWLEDGEMENTS<br /><br />
TEDA would like to sincerely thank the entire team that<br /><br />

<br /><br />
Executive Summary<br /><br />
Project Background<br /><br />
Sierra Minerals Holdings Ltd. (SMHL) is a joint venture known as VIMETCO Group that owns license to<br /><br />
mine bauxite in the south eastern Province of Sierra Leone. The lease area is located in five chiefdoms<br /><br />
within Bo, Moyamba and Bonthe Districts.<br /><br />
For SMHL to continue with its operations in the Concession area, the Environment Protection Agency<br /><br />
(EPA/SL) is requesting for an Environmental and Social Impact Assessment (ESIA) before a License can be<br /><br />
renewed. The ESIA should outline the existing environmental and social conditions within the mine site<br /><br />
and the surrounding communities identify potential impacts of the project and propose mitigation<br /><br />
measures that SMHL should undertake during its project operations.<br /><br />
Tropical Environmental Design Associates (TEDA) was retained by SMHL to prepare this ESIA with the<br /><br />
following Terms of Reference (TOR).<br /><br />
1. Describe and assess the prevailing baseline environmental and socio-economic conditions of the<br /><br />
study area.<br /><br />
2. Produce project overview including: management, Infrastructure and methods of production.<br /><br />
3. Describe the national and international regulatory framework applicable to bauxite mining in<br /><br />
Sierra Leone.<br /><br />
4.<br /><br />
<br /><br />
Determine project alternative scenarios.<br /><br />
<br /><br />
5. Identify, predict and determine the intensity of the impacts on the receptors using the<br /><br />
information obtained from objective 1.<br /><br />
6. Propose mitigation measures that would be used to minimize these impacts.<br /><br />
7. Produce a summary of an Environmental Management Plan (EMP) based on the information<br /><br />
obtained and that contained in the EMP and,<br /><br />
8. Conduct a Public Consultation and Disclosure meetings with all stakeholders including the<br /><br />
affected communities at chiefdom, district and regional levels. These will be held to solicit<br /><br />
feedback from all stakeholders’ concerns, and if possible propose further mitigation measures.<br /><br />
<br /><br />
Brief Description of the Project<br /><br />
SMHL produces about 1.4 million metric tonnes of bauxite annually from five main deposits at Gbonge,<br /><br />
Gondama, Jenega, Konta, and Wunde. It has a complex washing facility at Gondama with a capacity of<br /><br />
300t/hr. and a recovery of more than 65 percent. The bauxite mined is shipped to Constanta in Romania<br /><br />
for ALUM Tulcea the primary alumina manufacturing company that produces 600,000 tpa.<br /><br />
The proven washed bauxite reserves in the SML lease area are 12.5 million metric tonnes with an overall<br /><br />
grade of 51% Al203, 48% available alumina (av. Al2O3) and 3.0 % Si02 but total reserves should be at least<br /><br />
30 million metric tonnes. This reserve estimate will support production for approximately 20 years at an<br /><br />
annual production rate of 1.7 million metric tonnes. The bauxite reserves are dispersed 20% around the<br /><br />
washing plant and 80%, 14-18 km haulage distance from the washing plant.<br /><br />
The exploration program involves line cutting, pitting, drilling, sampling and assaying. The entire lease<br /><br />
area was prospected and classified by an earlier company, Sieromco into measured, indicated or<br /><br />
inferred resources. Based on this available information, SMHL has primarily been engaged in validating<br /><br />
these reported resources in four deposits (Konta, Wunde, Jenega and Gbonge) by generating its current<br /><br />
reserve base and the extension of SMHL mine life.<br /><br />
Infrastructure and Machinery<br /><br />
Summarily, the existing facilities and owner assets include:<br /><br />
<br /><br />
<br /><br />
Senior staff Camp – 14 double houses and lodging facilities with 7 rooms.<br /><br />
<br /><br />
<br /><br />
<br /><br />
Two administrative buildings, 2 canteens, mechanical workshop area, laboratory, store,<br /><br />
warehouse and a fuel station at the main plant site at Gondama.<br /><br />
<br /><br />
<br /><br />
<br /><br />
Harbour facility with dryer, 9 barges, 4 tug boats, fuel storage tanks and conveyors at Nitti.<br /><br />
<br /><br />
<br /><br />
<br /><br />
Five power Generating plants.<br /><br />
<br /><br />
<br /><br />
<br /><br />
Tailing storage facilities,<br /><br />
<br /><br />
<br /><br />
<br /><br />
Stockpile facilities (ROM & Washed).<br /><br />
<br /><br />
<br /><br />
<br /><br />
A fleet of vehicles including 30 MAN trucks and 32 Volvo trucks.<br /><br />
<br /><br />
<br /><br />
<br /><br />
A fleet of mining machinery including<br /><br />
<br /><br />
excavators, dozers, frontal loader, graders,<br /><br />
<br /><br />
compactors and a wide range of support equipments.etc.<br /><br />
<br /><br />
<br /><br />
Project Access (Land and Sea).<br /><br />
<br /><br />
Water resources within/proximity to SMHL lease are abundant. Due to the large quantities of water<br /><br />
required by bauxite wash process, water is sourced at the Taia River and pumped to a 5000 m 3 capacity<br /><br />
reservoir which in turn runs to the plant site by gravity flow.<br /><br />
<br /><br />
SMHL has a highly skilled and experienced workforce with an organizational management capable of<br /><br />
running the project at management and supervisory level. More than 95% of the workforce is Sierra<br /><br />
Leonean and majority of the employees are skilled with technical expertise in handling mining related<br /><br />
jobs.<br /><br />
Regulatory Framework<br /><br />
In accordance with the national and international regulatory framework for mining and due diligence<br /><br />
requirements of the lending institutions, the submission and acceptance of this ESIA is a mandatory<br /><br />
precursor for the renewal of license for this project. These regulatory framework and policies were used<br /><br />
in the preparation of this report.<br /><br />
Baseline Conditions<br /><br />
The baseline data used in the preparation of this report was based on field visits carried out between<br /><br />
September and November 2012. They complemented those previously collected from other sources.<br /><br />
Stakeholder consultations were undertaken to determine the additional data required for the evaluation<br /><br />
of the baseline conditions and potential impacts of the project. Following the stakeholder consultations,<br /><br />
a field survey was undertaken with the purpose of obtaining an overview of the entire study area that<br /><br />
would further complement the information gathered during the consultative meetings.<br /><br />
Environmental baseline conditions studied include: topography, climate, drainage, geology, water<br /><br />
resources, flora, fauna, and socio-economic data including demography, health and sanitation,<br /><br />
education, water supply, agricultural practices and land tenure.<br /><br />
Project Impacts and Mitigation<br /><br />
Project Benefits<br /><br />
<br /><br />
The exploitation of mineral resources such as bauxite is benefitting SMHL and its allied companies<br /><br />
financially. Apart from this direct benefit to the company, an array of other direct and indirect benefits<br /><br />
that the project could have on the mining community and the country at large are as follows:<br /><br />
Economic Benefits: In 2010 SMHL Vimetco acquired the bauxite mines from Titanium Resources Group<br /><br />
(TRG) with a market capital of $189 million. The injection of capital expenditure in foreign exchange has<br /><br />
boosted the economy of Sierra Leone in terms of foreign exchange earnings. The project is also<br /><br />
generating economic benefits to the GOSL in terms of taxes, duties and royalties as specified in the<br /><br />
mining code.<br /><br />
<br /><br />
Job Creation: The project directly and indirectly is injecting money into the local economy through<br /><br />
salaries to workers and supplies outsourced locally which thereby increases the purchasing power of the<br /><br />
local population. This is indirectly benefitting the small business groups in the community and the local<br /><br />
tax collection system.<br /><br />
Roads Infrastructure: Since operations started, SMHL has reconstructed and resurfaced all roads in the<br /><br />
lease area and it has continued to invest in maintenance of local roads with other neighboring<br /><br />
companies in the project area. This is facilitating a speedy movement of goods, agricultural produce and<br /><br />
people within and outside the concession area.<br /><br />
Community Development Initiatives: Since November 2012, the company has been financing community<br /><br />
development initiatives within the purview of SMHL Foundation in the five chiefdoms and in the<br /><br />
following priority areas of interventions:<br /><br />
<br /><br />
<br /><br />
Continued rehabilitation of major roads and haul roads in the concession area and Joint venture<br /><br />
rehabilitation of other major roads in the Moyamba District;<br /><br />
<br /><br />
<br /><br />
<br /><br />
Construction of Maternity and Child Health Post, hand dug wells fitted with pumps and classroom buildings.<br /><br />
<br /><br />
<br /><br />
<br /><br />
Provision of building materials to households affected by fire accidents in the community;<br /><br />
<br /><br />
<br /><br />
<br /><br />
Provision of services and supplies to the Serabu Hospital and Mokanji Police Station; and<br /><br />
<br /><br />
<br /><br />
<br /><br />
Offer of scholarships and tuition to students resident in the mining community.<br /><br />
<br /><br />
Notwithstanding the above positive impacts, SMHL operations continue to have various negative<br /><br />
biophysical and socio-economic impacts in its operations. These were predicted using established<br /><br />
procedures, standards and by means of professional and subjective judgments. Most of these impacts<br /><br />
were rated from insignificant to moderate. With no mitigation measures applied, most of these are<br /><br />
either minor or moderate. The table below presents a summary of the projects’ impacts and mitigation.<br /><br />
<br /><br />
When all the impacts are considered the two most prominent are those that are prevalent on surface<br /><br />
water, including the hydrology and natural drainage systems in the natural environment and the<br /><br />
disconnect existing between the communities expectations and apprehensions and the realistic gains<br /><br />
which may accrue through a more equitable transfer mechanism. Although there will be a number of<br /><br />
social impacts of high significance, the majority of the impacts on social conditions are of moderate<br /><br />
<br /><br />
significance. These social impacts must be compared with the predicted benefits of the mining activities,<br /><br />
as indicated in Section 1. The positive economic impact of the mines is highly significant, and the<br /><br />
revenue from taxation that will accrue to government provides resources that can be used to<br /><br />
significantly improve the condition of infrastructure in the South east region of the country. The<br /><br />
economic benefit to the local economy is also significant, since the project will not only support several<br /><br />
hundred employees directly, but it will also support significantly more of their dependents. It is<br /><br />
estimated that, on average, each employee will support eight dependents or family members. This<br /><br />
should result in a general improvement in standards of living in the study area and provides an<br /><br />
alternative livelihood to the current subsistence farming.<br /><br />

<br /><br />
Noise<br /><br />
<br /><br />
Future facilities with high noise impacts should be located well away from<br /><br />
noise sensitive areas.<br /><br />
Mine faces should be used as noise barriers in the immediate vicinity of<br /><br />
villages such as Gbonge, Jenega and Konta.<br /><br />
Protective hearing devices should be used when employees are at work<br /><br />
especially at or near earth-moving equipment, wash plants and power<br /><br />
houses.<br /><br />
<br /><br />
Surface Water<br /><br />
<br /><br />
Stockpiles and overburden should not be placed at nearby drainage ways.<br /><br />
Waste oil, fuel and other lubricants should be stored in strong<br /><br />
containments.<br /><br />
Tailings Management Plan should be properly implemented.<br /><br />
As much as possible, leakages into earth fill dams should be avoided.<br /><br />
Efforts should be made in the wet season to ensure that rain water is<br /><br />
diverted from the TSF<br /><br />

<br /><br />
Flora and Fauna<br /><br />
<br /><br />
Prompt mine reclamation activities in the mined out areas should be<br /><br />
enhanced.<br /><br />
If vulnerable or endangered species exist within the mine sites, measures<br /><br />
should be taken to create alternative sites.<br /><br />

<br /><br />
Analyses of Alternatives<br /><br />
Analyses of alternatives constitute part of this ESIA. The purpose is to improve on project design,<br /><br />
construction and operation decision making based on feasible project alternatives as prescribed by the<br /><br />
International Finance Corporation (IFC, 2006). It provides the basis for identifying optimal project<br /><br />
alternatives that are consistent with the goals of the mining company, funding organization, the<br /><br />
government, the mining community and the policy and regulatory framework established for the mining<br /><br />
industry.<br /><br />
Project alternatives that were considered for SMHL project include:<br /><br />
<br /><br />
<br /><br />
No Action alternative;<br /><br />
<br /><br />
<br /><br />
<br /><br />
Alternative for location of staff accommodation;<br /><br />
<br /><br />
<br /><br />
<br /><br />
Bauxite processing location;<br /><br />
<br /><br />
<br /><br />
<br /><br />
Mine closure.<br /><br />
<br /><br />
The adoption of the no-action alternative would mean that the project would remain in its current<br /><br />
condition with no mine-related development activity by SMHL. This scenario would mean that the<br /><br />
Environmental Management Plan and the community Development Action Plan (CDAP) that are<br /><br />
prepared would not be implemented. As such, the anticipated project benefits such as employment,<br /><br />
foreign exchange earnings, infrastructure development and community development initiatives would<br /><br />
not improve in the concession area and the country as a whole. This will result in the continuation of in<br /><br />
migration into the area and the resultant poor state of local economy, infrastructure and other public<br /><br />
services.<br /><br />
Following the depletion of the Mokanji deposits in 1982, Sieromco relocated its operations to Gondama.<br /><br />
The accommodation provided for SMHL staff are located 20 – 40 miles away from the plant site. The<br /><br />
mining activities at the Gondama site do not require total off-site accommodation especially for<br /><br />
supervision work during the second and third shifts. The option therefore is the relocation or expansion<br /><br />
of staff accommodation closer to the mine site. Proximity to the mine site would lead to the following<br /><br />
benefits:<br /><br />
<br /><br />
<br /><br />
Reduction in travel time to work;<br /><br />
<br /><br />
<br /><br />
<br /><br />
Increased work supervision at all times;<br /><br />
<br /><br />
<br /><br />
<br /><br />
Proximity to the only Medical Hospital in the mining area;<br /><br />
<br /><br />
<br /><br />
<br /><br />
Decrease in the running cost of vehicles;<br /><br />
<br /><br />
<br /><br />
<br /><br />
Extension of overtime work for certain staff categories;<br /><br />
<br /><br />
<br /><br />
<br /><br />
Increase in the opportunity for the expansion of the Gbonge deposits and future expansion of<br /><br />
shipping facility closer to the four bauxite deposits at Mattru.<br /><br />
<br /><br />
The existing processing plant is in a closer proximity to the five major deposits. For operational<br /><br />
considerations, the Tailings Storage Facility (TSF) is located as close as possible to the plant site to<br /><br />
reduce cost of transportation of the slurry. The potential for the existing facility to near its storage<br /><br />
capacity is high considering mine life of SMHL operations. Consideration should be given for an<br /><br />
alternative storage facility in the near future.<br /><br />
<br /><br />
Tailings Disposal and Storage will involve a construction of mine pits in the Gbonge area into tailings<br /><br />
storage containment. The slurries would be deposited in the pits as mining continues. Filling would<br /><br />
continue for less than a year and after a decanting period, revegetation would start followed by a full<br /><br />
reclamation work based on the mine reclamation plan.<br /><br />
The major disadvantages of this alternative would be related to short term mine plan as this operation<br /><br />
will concurrently go on with other mining operations.<br /><br />
<br /><br />
Construction of a new storage facility would require concurrent construction work and mining<br /><br />
operations with little obstruction. Construction work would however be more expensive as materials<br /><br />
such as sand and gravel would have to be transported from a distance outside the mining area.<br /><br />
Preferred alternative for the two options would be controlled by key factors including costs, time of<br /><br />
construction, and availability of equipments, weather conditions and the expected life of the mine<br /><br />
operations.<br /><br />
Mine reclamation and closure activities at SMHL operations are implemented to establish a beneficial<br /><br />
post-mining land use, in the case of this project it will consist primarily of agricultural lands. This option<br /><br />
requires millions of dollars in earth moving activities, and it cannot be considered as the best option for<br /><br />
the open pits. The preferred options would be to identify those pits (especially those around Gbonge) to<br /><br />
fill with water in the wet season for use by the community at the end the mine life. Backfilling of these<br /><br />
pits with tailings slurries would be another feasible option considering the life of the existing tailings<br /><br />
facilities at the plant site.<br /><br />
<br /><br />
Public Consultation and Disclosure<br /><br />
<br /><br />
This section defines the Sierra Leone requirements for conducting stakeholder consultation and<br /><br />
disclosure activities during the ESIA process.<br /><br />
Public Consultation and disclosure meetings for this ESIA were held in respect of the renewal of license<br /><br />
at chiefdom, district and regional levels between January 25th and 28th 2013.<br /><br />
1. Serabu on the 25th January 2013 – Chiefdom level<br /><br />
<br /><br />
2. Moyamba District on the 26th January 2013 – District level<br /><br />
<br /><br />
3. Bo Town on the 28th January 2013 – Regional level<br /><br />
<br /><br />
The meetings were meant to allow all stakeholders stay informed on the current updates on SMHL<br /><br />
operations, allow all stakeholders voice their opinions, grievances and provide meaningful suggestions<br /><br />
for sustained operations of the project.<br /><br />
<br /><br />
The key objectives of the public consultation and disclosure as set-out by the Sierra Leone<br /><br />
Environmental Protection Agency (SLEPA) as part of the international requirement for public hearings to<br /><br />
be undertaken for the approval/renewal and issuance of an Environmental Licence are;<br /><br />
<br /><br />
<br /><br />
<br /><br />
Inform stakeholders (individuals, communities, local interest groups, Government officers, local<br /><br />
NGOs, Community Based Organizations, affected and interested parties) of the important<br /><br />
aspects of the SMHL-Vimetco Bauxite Project;<br /><br />
<br /><br />
<br /><br />
<br /><br />
Provide an opportunity to consult with local communities, stakeholders, civil activists etc to<br /><br />
voice out their concerns and perceptions regarding the project;<br /><br />
<br /><br />
<br /><br />
<br /><br />
Consult comprehensively as far as possible by disseminating information about the nature of the<br /><br />
project, identify, and discuss the public concerns( potential project’s impacts) about the project;<br /><br />
<br /><br />
<br /><br />
<br /><br />
Obtain feedback on perceptions and concerns by means of a public exposure of the ESIA draft<br /><br />
document;<br /><br />
<br /><br />
<br /><br />
<br /><br />
Solicit public comments and encourage input on preferred alternatives, mitigation measures and<br /><br />
similar aspects on project implementation.<br /><br />
<br /><br />
<br /><br />
<br /><br />
Respond to all stakeholders as far as possible and address the concerns and issues that are<br /><br />
raised.<br /><br />
<br /><br />
To ensure full participation and contribution of community and stakeholders, notification via the local<br /><br />
media, print media, letters, telephone calls and display of banners, indicating time, date and locations of<br /><br />
the public hearing were undertaken prior to the disclosure process.<br /><br />
The following are the summary of the findings, comments and input from key government officials,<br /><br />
participants and affected communities at the three levels where the consultations and disclosure<br /><br />
meetings were held. These inputs have played a definitive role in shaping the focus of the ESIA<br /><br />
document and the development of mitigation/amelioration measures.<br /><br />
<br /><br />
<br /><br />
Community Development undertaken by the company.<br /><br />
<br /><br />
<br /><br />
<br /><br />
Environmental impacts of the project on communities (e.g.: Noise, Pollution of air and water<br /><br />
bodies)<br /><br />
<br /><br />
<br /><br />
<br /><br />
Socio-Economic benefits to the community (e.g. Youth employment, skills training etc.)<br /><br />
<br /><br />
<br /><br />
<br /><br />
Environmental degradation of community agricultural land.<br /><br />
<br /><br />
<br /><br />
<br /><br />
Disposal of used / scrap items (e.g. tyres, chemicals etc.).<br /><br />
<br /><br />
<br /><br />
<br /><br />
Tailings outflow and threat to nearby water sources.<br /><br />
<br /><br />
<br /><br />
<br /><br />
Construction / tarring of nearby road networks.<br /><br />
<br /><br />
<br /><br />
<br /><br />
Effective Monitoring and Evaluation of the implementation of the ESIA report and the EMP<br /><br />
especially on the part of the EPA and the Ministry of Mines etc.<br /><br />
<br /><br />
<br /><br />
<br /><br />
Collaboration and Consultation with the local people by the company.<br /><br />
<br /><br />
<br /><br />
<br /><br />
Company to expand on the road rehabilitation work to other surrounding areas (e.g. RutileBumpeh road).<br /><br />
<br /><br />
<br /><br />
<br /><br />
Company to embark on capacitating its workers in relevant areas, providing skills training<br /><br />
opportunities and the promotion of other economic activities for the community.<br /><br />
<br /><br />
<br /><br />
<br /><br />
EPA-SL and the Mines Ministry to liaise up with local councils to enforce the implementation<br /><br />
and Monitoring of all these plans by the company.<br /><br />

Environmental and Social Impact Assessment (ESIA) Final Report: Prepared by TEDAEnvironmental Consultant: December; 2012.<br /><br />
<br /><br />
Page 1<br /><br />
<br /><br />
Section 1.0 - Introduction<br /><br />
1.1 Background<br /><br />
Sierra Minerals Holdings Ltd. is a joint venture known as VIMETCO Group that owns license to mine<br /><br />
bauxite in the south eastern Province of Sierra Leone. The lease area is located in five chiefdoms within<br /><br />
BO, Moyamba and Bonthe Districts. A brief description of the company’s business overview is presented<br /><br />
below:<br /><br />
<br /><br />
<br /><br />
About 1.4 million tonnes of bauxite is produced annually from five main deposits at Gbonge,<br /><br />
Gondama, Jenega, Konta, and Wunde.<br /><br />
<br /><br />
<br /><br />
<br /><br />
<br /><br />
<br /><br />
It has a complex washing facility at Gondama with :<br /><br />
-<br /><br />
<br /><br />
A capacity of 300t/hr.<br /><br />
<br /><br />
-<br /><br />
<br /><br />
A recovery of > 65%<br /><br />
<br /><br />
-<br /><br />
<br /><br />
A total content of Al203>53% and;<br /><br />
<br /><br />
-<br /><br />
<br /><br />
A low content of SiO2 <4%.<br /><br />
<br /><br />
Its harbor facilities located at Nitti (40km. from Gondama) include: a bauxite dryer, push boats,<br /><br />
barges and a loading facility.<br /><br />
<br /><br />
<br /><br />
<br /><br />
The bauxite mined is shipped to Constanta in Romania for ALUM Tulcea the primary alumina<br /><br />
manufacturing company that produces 600,000 tpa.<br /><br />
<br /><br />
For SMHL to continue with its operations in the Concession area, the Environment Protection Agency<br /><br />
(EPA/SL) is requesting for an Environmental Impact Assessment (ESIA) before a License can be renewed.<br /><br />
The ESIA should outline the existing environmental and social conditions within the mine site and the<br /><br />
surrounding communities to identify potential impacts of the project and the proposed mitigation<br /><br />
measures that SMHL should undertake during its project operations.<br /><br />
1.2 Terms of Reference<br /><br />
This Environmental and Social Impact Assessment report has been prepared in accordance with the<br /><br />
stipulations of the EPA/SL and the following Terms of Reference (TOR) received from SMHL.<br /><br />
<br /><br />
1. To describe and assess the prevailing baseline environmental and socio-economic conditions<br /><br />
of the study area and secondary data collection.<br /><br />
<br /><br />
Environmental and Social Impact Assessment (ESIA) Final Report: Prepared by TEDAEnvironmental Consultant: December; 2012.<br /><br />
<br /><br />
Page 2<br /><br />
<br /><br />
2. Produce project overview including: management, Infrastructure and methods of<br /><br />
production.<br /><br />
3. Describe the national and international regulatory framework applicable to bauxite mining<br /><br />
in Sierra Leone.<br /><br />
4.<br /><br />
<br /><br />
Determine project alternative scenarios.<br /><br />
<br /><br />
5. Identify, predict and determine the intensity of the impacts on the receptors using the<br /><br />
information obtained from objective 1.<br /><br />
6. Propose mitigation measures that would be used to minimize these impacts.<br /><br />
7. Produce a summary of an Environmental Management Plan based on the information<br /><br />
obtained and that contained in the EMP.<br /><br />
8. Conduct a Public Consultation and Disclosure meetings with all stakeholders including the<br /><br />
affected communities at chiefdom, district and regional levels. These will be held to solicit<br /><br />
feedback from all stakeholder’s concerns, and if possible further mitigation measures.<br /><br />
<br /><br />
Tropical Environmental Design Associates, Architects, Engineers, Environmental Designers and<br /><br />
Development Consultants (TEDA) were retained by SMHL to prepare this ESIA.<br /><br />
1.3 Structure of the Report<br /><br />
As much as possible, this ESIA followed the Guidelines provided by the EPA/SL.<br /><br />
Section One: This section presents the introduction that explains project background, Terms of<br /><br />
Reference, purpose, scope, objectives, study limitations and methodology.<br /><br />
Section Two presents the project description including management, infrastructure, machinery, ore<br /><br />
reserves, mining and security.<br /><br />
Section Three is a summary of the legal and institutional framework for environmental issues related to<br /><br />
mining in Sierra Leone.<br /><br />
Section Four presents details if the environmental baseline conditions.<br /><br />
Section Five presents the direct impacts associated with the project and mitigation measures needed to<br /><br />
address the impacts.<br /><br />
<br /><br />
Environmental and Social Impact Assessment (ESIA) Final Report: Prepared by TEDAEnvironmental Consultant: December; 2012.<br /><br />
<br /><br />
Page 3<br /><br />
<br /><br />
Section Six presents a summary of the Environmental Management Plan proposing residual impacts with<br /><br />
the pertinent measures to be addressed during project operations.<br /><br />
Section Seven presents conclusions and recommendations for sustained bauxite operations in the lease<br /><br />
area.<br /><br />
1.4 Project Setting<br /><br />
The project setting is divided into two impact areas: a. Areas of direct social and environmental impacts<br /><br />
immediately and directly affected by the mining operation namely, mine panels, plant site, haul roads,<br /><br />
Nitti Port, Matta Camp and the affected communities within the lease area. b. Areas of project influence<br /><br />
where most of the environmental impacts are influenced by project activities either within or outside<br /><br />
the lease area. Examples of these are villages outside the lease area but linked to the haul roads and<br /><br />
portions of those chiefdoms that are out of the lease area.<br /><br />
The lease area is about 163 kilometers south east of Freetown with the plant site and shipping facilities<br /><br />
located at Gondama and Nitti respectively. The bauxite concession covers an area of 321.2 square<br /><br />
kilometers as delineated in Exploration License EXPL 2/2001. Its principal deposits are concentrated in<br /><br />
Gbonge, Gondama, Jenega, Konta and Wunde. The road leading to Mattru from Freetown which<br /><br />
constitutes part of the haul road passes through the lease area for about 59.5 km. There are many<br /><br />
villages that are affected by the mine activities, but those mostly affected currently include: Gbonge,<br /><br />
Gondama, Jenega, Konta and Wunde. SMHL owns a residential camp at Matta (out of the concession<br /><br />
area) where accommodation and recreation facilities are located 20 km from Gondama and Nitti<br /><br />
respectively. The entire camp is fenced and guarded. It consists of 14 duplex houses for senior staff, 1<br /><br />
building for kitchen and a recreational area, 1 gym building; other building/utility/services that house<br /><br />
the generator and, laundry.<br /><br />
The entire project area is traversed by unpaved roads with the major ones leading to the five chiefdom<br /><br />
head quarter towns of Gbonge, Bumpeh, Gbangbatoke Banta Mokele and Mano Dase. A number of<br /><br />
haulage and access roads constructed during the early mining period that were not reclaimed are used<br /><br />
as foot paths to farms and villages<br /><br />
The proven washed bauxite reserves in the SML lease area are 12.5 million metric 4eigh with an overall<br /><br />
grade of 51% Al203, 48% available alumina (av. Al2O3) and 3.0 % Si02 but total reserves should be at least<br /><br />
30 million metric 4eigh. This reserve estimate will support production for approximately 20 years at an<br /><br />
Environmental and Social Impact Assessment (ESIA) Final Report: Prepared by TEDAEnvironmental Consultant: December; 2012.<br /><br />
<br /><br />
Page 4<br /><br />
<br /><br />
annual production rate of 1.7 million metric 5eigh. The bauxite reserves are dispersed 20% around the<br /><br />
washing plant and 80%, 14-18 km haulage distance from the washing plant.<br /><br />
<br /><br />
Map 1. SMHL Lease Area (ML1/05).<br /><br />

1.5 History and ownership of Bauxite Deposits<br /><br />
The exploration of Bauxite in Sierra Leone started in the 1920’s with the recording of low-grade bauxites<br /><br />
in and around the Freetown Peninsular and Kabala in the Northern Province. In 1958, the bauxite<br /><br />
potential in the Kasila Group Geology was initiated with the mapping of the Mokanji and Gbonge Hills.<br /><br />
On the basis of this study, the Sierra Leone Ore and Metal Company Limited (Sieromco) was formed as a<br /><br />
wholly owned subsidiary of a Swiss Company, Alusuisse.<br /><br />
Environmental and Social Impact Assessment (ESIA) Final Report: Prepared by TEDAEnvironmental Consultant: December; 2012.<br /><br />
<br /><br />
Page 5<br /><br />
<br /><br />
In 1961, Sieromco signed an agreement with the GOSL entitling Sieromco to prospect for and mine<br /><br />
bauxite in certain parts of the country. Sieromco commenced mining bauxite in Sierra Leone in<br /><br />
September 1963 in the Banta/Gbangbatoke and Dasse Chiefdoms in the Moyamba District. Sieromco<br /><br />
mined in the Mokanji area continuously for more than 19 years.<br /><br />
In 1982, following the depletion of the Mokanji deposits, Sieromco relocated its operations to Gondama<br /><br />
(approximately 26 km south-east of Mokanji) and resumed mining in the Banta Mokelleh Chiefdom.<br /><br />
Sieromco mined the Gondama deposits continuously until January 18, 1995, when the company<br /><br />
suspended operations and abandoned the mine site as a result of the civil war.<br /><br />
Alusuisse requested a consulting Firm BH&P to prepare a feasibility study in April 1995 for a possible<br /><br />
reopening of the Sieromco mines. The objectives of the evaluation were to:<br /><br />
<br /><br />
<br /><br />
Confirm the practicality of the mining and transportation methods;<br /><br />
<br /><br />
<br /><br />
<br /><br />
Evaluate possible alternatives; and<br /><br />
<br /><br />
<br /><br />
<br /><br />
Confirm and update the cost of projections.<br /><br />
<br /><br />
Other considerations were also given to the possibility of combining the operations with Sierra Rutile<br /><br />
with arrangements made for contract mining.<br /><br />
Observations made took cognizance of many aspects of mining based on a ten year operating<br /><br />
experience and detailed operational data that were proven accurate.<br /><br />
On October 19, 2000, the GOSL reached an agreement with Alusuisse to acquire the assets and minerals<br /><br />
and mining rights of the company under the Sieromco Share Purchase Agreement. In December 2000,<br /><br />
Sieromco finally shutdown all its business dealings in Sierra Leone and handed over its assets to the<br /><br />
GOSL and relinquished Mining Lease 2131.<br /><br />
Sierra Rutile Limited (SRL) purchased these assets from the GOSL under the Sale and Purchase<br /><br />
Agreement of 20th. November 2001; and on December 1st of the same year, SRL was granted exploration<br /><br />
license EXPL 2/2001 for the area formerly covered by the Sieromco Mining Lease 2131. On 7th<br /><br />
December, 2001, SRL, with consent of the GOSL, assigned its rights under the above exploration license,<br /><br />
to Sierra Minerals Holdings Ltd.1. (SMHL) to fulfill all its obligations, title and interest.<br /><br />
SMHL was incorporated in British Virgin Islands and registered on October, 2001. On 3rd. June 2004,<br /><br />
SMHL entered into engineering, Procurement, construction and commissioning (EPPCC) Agreement with<br /><br />
PW Mining.<br /><br />
Environmental and Social Impact Assessment (ESIA) Final Report: Prepared by TEDAEnvironmental Consultant: December; 2012.<br /><br />
<br /><br />
Page 6<br /><br />
<br /><br />
Another feasibility study was prepared and presented to the Government of Sierra Leone (GOSL) in May,<br /><br />
2003. The study which formed the basis of a re-start Plan was based on sound financial and technical<br /><br />
fundamentals considering the infrastructural and administrative synergies between SRL and SMHL1.<br /><br />
An Environmental and Social Impact Assessment was undertaken by Aqua Terra Consulting during the<br /><br />
period February and March 2003 that served as the basis for the environmental impacts of the project<br /><br />
discussed in the Feasibility Study. In January 2005, SMHL was granted Mining Lease ML1/05 covering an<br /><br />
area of 321.7 km.<br /><br />
SMHL operated with two major contractors: PW Mining International, a full service contract mining<br /><br />
company which operates throughout Africa, and, CZAK Group which was involved in the shipping of the<br /><br />
ore product and other port operations. In 2008, SMHL was acquired to Vimetco Group and two years<br /><br />
later, SMHL, being a subsidiary of Vimetco Group continued with its mining operations selling all<br /><br />
products to ALUM Tulcea Refinery in Romania.<br /><br />
<br /><br />
Map 2. SMHL Areas of Influence<br /><br />

including the issues and concerns raised by the affected communities. It:<br /><br />

1.8 Study Constraints and Limitations.<br /><br />
This ESIA was expected to commence in August 2012 with the aim of submitting the report to the client<br /><br />
in October 2012. Thereafter, an onward submission and feedback from EPA would take place before a<br /><br />
public consultation and disclosure process. Although the consultant worked closely with the client, some<br /><br />
delay was caused in finalising the report and to receive the license.<br /><br />
Othe factors that acted as limitations to the study include:<br /><br />
 Absence of government officials, NGOs and and other local leaders in the consultative meetings<br /><br />
during the field work;<br /><br />
 Inadequacy of updated documentation including maps and other unclassified documents;<br /><br />
 Absence of some community members to participate fully in the consultative meetings due to<br /><br />
the limited time available between farm work, meeting gatherings and other domestic<br /><br />
responsibilities;<br /><br />
<br /><br />
Environmental and Social Impact Assessment (ESIA) Final Report: Prepared by TEDAEnvironmental Consultant: December; 2012.<br /><br />
<br /><br />
Page 9<br /><br />
<br /><br />
 Secondary data that were collected on the villages were not reliable as these have not been<br /><br />
frequently updated. This is particularly so in the case of demography and agricultural production<br /><br />
trends.<br /><br />
 It was not possible to undertake a detailed taxonomic assessment of fauna and flora species due<br /><br />
to the limited time available;<br /><br />
Instead, the assessment presented here supplements other studies already undertaken in previous<br /><br />
years.<br /><br />
1.9 Study Approach<br /><br />
The approach to this ESIA consists of the following phases as outlined in the EPA Act of 2008 as<br /><br />
amended in 2010: Screening, Scoping, Impact Assessment and Public Disclosure.<br /><br />
Screening: This is the initial phase which decides if an ESIA is required for the project and to determine<br /><br />
the extent to which the assessment will be. According to the EPA Guidelines and safeguard policies of<br /><br />
the lending institutions, this is a Class A project because it has the potential to have significant<br /><br />
environmental impacts, which in turn may have an effect on the facilities and those communities that<br /><br />
are affected by mining operations. As required by EPA Act, a Screening form was completed and<br /><br />
submitted to EPA detailing the need and level of the assessment, the acceptability of the consultant and<br /><br />
the public participation process.<br /><br />
Scoping: The scoping phase involved identification of key issues in the screening phase that were further<br /><br />
investigated based on desktop study of relevant environmental and social data collected from published<br /><br />
reports and previous studies of the project area. Stakeholder consultations were undertaken to<br /><br />
determine additional data required for the evaluation of the baseline conditions and the potential<br /><br />
impacts of the project. Public meetings were held in which a diverse group of community members<br /><br />
including local authorities, traditional leaders, opinion leaders, local council representatives, youths and<br /><br />
women’s groups were invited to participate in the surveys.<br /><br />
A door to door sample socioeconomic survey (using a semi-structured questionnaire) was conducted in<br /><br />
43 communities spanning the five chiefdoms (Upper Banta, Lower Banta, Kpanda Kemoh, Bumpe Ngao<br /><br />
and Dasse Chiefdoms respectively).<br /><br />
<br /><br />
Environmental and Social Impact Assessment (ESIA) Final Report: Prepared by TEDAEnvironmental Consultant: December; 2012.<br /><br />
<br /><br />
Page 10<br /><br />

scoping consultation include impacts on:<br /><br />
<br /><br />

<br /><br />
Impact Assessment Phase: This involved an assessment of the significance of the potential impacts<br /><br />
including the cumulative impacts and recommended mitigation measures that could minimise impacts.<br /><br />
The methodology applied for this assessment is presented in the methodology section that is outlined<br /><br />
below. An Environmental Management Plan (EMP) has been prepared that clearly defines all the<br /><br />
residual and other specific actions that SMHL undertakes to implement the measures that will minimise<br /><br />
and mitigate any negative impacts on the human, social and environmental aspects within the project<br /><br />
area. A summary of the EMP is produced under a separate chapter of this report. The plan includes an<br /><br />
Environmental Monitoring Plan, Emergency Response and Contingency Plan (ERCP), Tailings<br /><br />
Management Plan (TMP) and Mine Reclamation and Closure Plan (MRCP).<br /><br />

1.10 Methodology<br /><br />
The methodology for collecting data for the preparation of this ESIA was based on desk study,<br /><br />
stakeholder consultation and field survey.<br /><br />
Desk Study: Relevant reports, literature and other documents were collected and a detailed review of<br /><br />
the secondary data was undertaken during the course of the study. The major reports included the<br /><br />
following:<br /><br />
 Feasibility study report of 2003<br /><br />
 SMHL ESIA and Environmental Social Action Plan (ESAP) reports of 2003<br /><br />
 Exploration and survey reports prepared for SMHL and previous owners the bauxite deposits.<br /><br />
 Community Development Action Plan (CDAP) report of 2010.<br /><br />
 Environmental Management Plan report of 2010.<br /><br />
 SMHL 3rd.quarterEnvironmental Monitoring report of 2012.<br /><br />
Environmental and Social Impact Assessment (ESIA) Final Report: Prepared by TEDAEnvironmental Consultant: December; 2012.<br /><br />
<br /><br />
Page 11<br /><br />
<br /><br />
 SMHL Environmental Report of 2012 for License renewal.<br /><br />
 National and International Policy Documents and,<br /><br />
 Public Data that is available from the Internet.<br /><br />
All of these provided more information about the project and contributed to the improvement of the<br /><br />
data collection.<br /><br />
Impact Prediction: An assessment of the significance of the impacts was made using established<br /><br />
standards and professional judgment. The conventions that were adopted include: a. Significant Rating<br /><br />
Scales; b. Timescales; c. Spatial Scale; d. Likelihood and, e. Severity.<br /><br />
The significant rating scale developed by Weaver and Caldwell (1999) was adapted for this study. This is<br /><br />
explained in Table 1. Timescales are related to the estimated duration of the impacts. The timescales<br /><br />
for rating the impacts in this report are short-term (0-5yrs) medium term (6- 20 years) and long-term<br /><br />
(more than 20 years). Short-term impacts are those that occur during construction and initial operation<br /><br />
activities that may or not be mitigated during the construction period or after.<br /><br />
Spatial scales which describe the spatial extent of an impact in terms of the amount of area affected, i.e.<br /><br />
localized or widespread were expressed as follows:<br /><br />
<br /><br />
<br /><br />
Localised i.e. an impact limited to a small part of the project’s operational area e.g. a few<br /><br />
hundred hectares in extent.<br /><br />
<br /><br />
<br /><br />
<br /><br />
Study Area i.e. impact limited to the immediate vicinity of the project area.<br /><br />
<br /><br />
<br /><br />
<br /><br />
Regional i.e. impact limited to the general vicinity of the project area.<br /><br />
<br /><br />
<br /><br />
<br /><br />
National i.e. impact limited to the country as a whole.<br /><br />
<br /><br />
<br /><br />
<br /><br />
Global i.e. impact limited to the global environment.<br /><br />
<br /><br />
Environmental and Social Impact Assessment (ESIA) Final Report: Prepared by TEDAEnvironmental Consultant: December; 2012.<br /><br />
<br /><br />
Page 12<br /><br />
<br /><br />
Likelihood is the probability that the impact would occur or not. It is explained as: very unlikely, unlikely<br /><br />
and likely. This criterion was applied to all the potential negative impacts in order to determine whether<br /><br />
or not the impacts can be minimised.<br /><br />
The severity of an impact was determined by the magnitude of the significance based on the extent to<br /><br />
which the impact would change under existing conditions or how it would impact upon another<br /><br />
receptor. It is explained as very severe, severe, moderately severe, slightly severe and no effect.<br /><br />
The application of checklists and interaction matrices finally enabled the assessment to arrive at: a. Brief<br /><br />
description of the expected or predicted environmental changes that would result during the operation<br /><br />
of the project. b. Measures that would be implemented to avoid adverse impacts on the environment<br /><br />
and, c. An assessment of the possible alternatives including proposed actions.<br /><br />

High impacts<br /><br />

Of the highest order possible within the bounds of impacts which would occur.<br /><br />
In the case of negative impacts, there would be no mitigation and/or remedial<br /><br />
activity to offset the impact at the spatial or timescale for which it was predicted.<br /><br />
In the case of positive impacts there is no real alternative to achieving the<br /><br />
benefit.<br /><br />
In the case of negative impacts, mitigation and/or remedial activity would be<br /><br />
feasible but difficult, expensive, time-consuming or some combination of these.<br /><br />
In the case of positive impacts, other means of achieving this benefit would be<br /><br />
feasible, but these would be more difficult, expensive, and time-consuming or<br /><br />
some combination of these.<br /><br />
Impact would be real but not substantial within the bounds of those, which could<br /><br />
occur. In the case of negative impacts, mitigation and/or remedial activity would<br /><br />
be both feasible and fairly easily possible. In the case of positive impacts, other<br /><br />
means of achieving this benefit would be about equal in time<br /><br />
Impact would be of a low order and with little real effect. In the case of negative<br /><br />
impacts, mitigation and/or remedial activity would be either easily achieved or<br /><br />
little would be required, or both. In the case of positive impacts, alternative<br /><br />
means for achieving this benefit would likely be easier, cheaper, more effective,<br /><br />
less time-consuming, or a combination of these<br /><br />
Impact would be negligible. In the case of negative impacts, almost no<br /><br />
mitigation and/or remedial activity would be needed, and any minor steps that<br /><br />
might be needed would be easy, cheap and simple. In the case of positive<br /><br />
impacts, alternative means would almost all likely to be better, in one or a<br /><br />
number of ways, than this means of achieving the benefit.<br /><br />
No impacts, not even a very low impact on the system or any of its parts.<br /><br />

Field Survey: Following the stakeholder consultation, a field survey was undertaken with the purpose of<br /><br />
obtaining an overview of the entire operational area that would complement information gathered<br /><br />

sites and dams and the routing of the biophysical and social issues that arise as a result of project<br /><br />

<br /><br />
Socio-economic Survey: A door to door sample socioeconomic survey (using a semi-structured<br /><br />
questionnaire) was conducted in 43 communities spanning the five chiefdoms (Upper Banta, Lower<br /><br />
Banta, Kpanda Kemoh, Bumpe Ngao and Dasse Chiefdoms respectively). The questionnaire were<br /><br />
administered by trained enumerators (9) drawn among resident community members of mostly<br /><br />
teachers. They were closely supervised to ensure effective administration of survey questionnaire and in<br /><br />
turn report to the consultant in charge. The data collected were coded and entered into the SPSS<br /><br />
version 16.0 spread sheet by a trained data entry clerk. Detailed analysis undertaken using frequency<br /><br />
counts, descriptive statistics, cross tabulations, to identify statistically significant differences between<br /><br />
the results of the various segments.<br /><br />
<br /><br />
The household survey was based on a random sample of 180 households and focuses on gathering data<br /><br />
on the socioeconomic baselines of the community influenced by the mining operations. The questions<br /><br />
were designed to seek information on the population and dwelling units, income, livelihood, health,<br /><br />
community participation, resource use and general attitude towards Vimetco SMHL and<br /><br />
concerns/opinions and expectations of the community in the project area. The questionnaire were<br /><br />
administered by trained enumerators (9) drawn among residence community members mostly teachers.<br /><br />
They were closely supervised to ensure effective administration of survey questionnaire and in turn<br /><br />
report to the consultant in charge. The data collected were coded and entered into the SPSS version<br /><br />
16.0 spread sheet by a trained data entry clerk. Detailed analysis undertaken using frequency counts,<br /><br />
descriptive statistics, cross tabulations, to identify statistically significant differences between the results<br /><br />
of the various segments.<br /><br />

Focus Group Discussions: Focus Group Discussions were conducted in which a diverse group of<br /><br />
community members including local authorities, traditional leaders, opinion leaders, local council<br /><br />
representatives, youths and women’s groups were invited to participate. These generally consisted of<br /><br />
focus group discussion/meeting. A total of 5 focus group discussions were held in each of these<br /><br />
chiefdoms as shown below. The issues discussed covered the following areas: a. Community<br /><br />
infrastructure. B. Social and cultural institutions. C. Access to public Services such as education, health<br /><br />
and sanitation. D. Gender issues. E. Social and economic trends and, f. Agricultural production.<br /><br />
All the views expressed by the affected communities helped to identify and evaluate the social and<br /><br />
environmental impacts and the formulation of the mitigation measures. Details of the socio-economic<br /><br />
survey are presented in the Annex of this report.<br /><br />
The household survey was based on a random sample of 180 households and focused on gathering data<br /><br />
on the socioeconomic baselines of the community influenced by the mining operations. The questions<br /><br />
were designed to seek information on the population and dwelling units, income, livelihood, health,<br /><br />
community participation, resource use and general attitude towards Vimetco SMHL and<br /><br />
concerns/opinions and expectations of the community in the project area.<br /><br />
1.11. Project Benefits<br /><br />
Apart from the direct financial benefit to the company, an array of other direct and indirect benefits<br /><br />
that the project could have on the mining community and the country at large are outlined below.<br /><br />
Economic Benefits: The capital expenditure over the life of mine was estimated in 2003 at $28.6 million<br /><br />
in nominal terms excluding capital investment by contractors. This was mainly associated with<br /><br />
refurbishment and purchase of new equipment. In 2010 SMHL Vimetco acquired the bauxite mines from<br /><br />
Titanium Resources Group (TRG) with a market capital of $189 million. The injection of this amount of<br /><br />
money in foreign exchange has boosted the economy of Sierra Leone in terms of foreign exchange<br /><br />
earnings. The project is also generating economic benefits to the GOSL in terms of taxes, duties and<br /><br />
royalties as specified in the mining code.<br /><br />
<br /><br />
Secondly, the project directly and indirectly is injecting money into the local economy through salaries<br /><br />
to workers and supplies outsourced locally which thereby is increasing the purchasing power of the local<br /><br />
population.<br /><br />

for business and income improvement.<br /><br />
<br /><br />
Roads Infrastructure: Most of these roads built before SMHL operations were totally damaged during<br /><br />
the war. The haulage roads to the Gbonge deposits required major road construction work. The existing<br /><br />
road from Gondama to Nitti harbour was also in a serious state of disrepair as a result of lack of<br /><br />
maintenance during inter-war years. Since operations started, SMHL has reconstructed and resurfaced<br /><br />
all roads in its operational area and is investing in maintenance of local roads with other 17eighbouring<br /><br />
companies in the project area. This is facilitating a speedy movement of goods, agricultural produce and<br /><br />
people within and outside the concession area.<br /><br />
Community Development Initiatives: Since November 2012, the company has been financing<br /><br />

2.1 Project Management and Workforce<br /><br />
The SMHL has a highly skilled and experienced workforce with an organizational management capable<br /><br />
of running the project at management and supervisory levels. It comprises the General Manager,<br /><br />
Department Managers, Supervisory positions, Foreman positions and the General Staff in the form of<br /><br />
administration, maintenance, and support services.<br /><br />
SMHL’s General Manager is based at the mine site, and reports to the CEO and manage the SMHL<br /><br />
operations through a structure of Five (5) Departmental Managers and Five (5) Unit Managers. SMHL<br /><br />
Organizational Structure is presented in Figure 9. The General Manager has the overall responsibility for<br /><br />
SMHL operations and collaborates with the other Departmental Managers, including the Production and<br /><br />
Geology Manager, Technical and Repairs Manager, Procurement and Logistics Manager, Finance and IT<br /><br />
Manager, Human Resource and Administration Manager. The responsibilities of these Departmental<br /><br />
Managers will encompass the whole SMHL operations, thus providing valuable human resources for the<br /><br />
SMHL Manager to utilize.<br /><br />
An effective recruitment methodology and an ongoing training and development programs improve the<br /><br />
quality of the personnel to maintain efficiency and sustainability.<br /><br />
A further reduction of the SMHL workforce by contracting operations with clearly defined boundaries<br /><br />
and measurable production units reflect reduction in operating cost models. The operations that are<br /><br />
wholly or partly contracted are as follows:<br /><br />
<br /><br />
<br /><br />
10% Transportation of ore mined to the Washing Plant;<br /><br />
<br /><br />
<br /><br />
<br /><br />
10% Transportation of washed bauxite to Nitti; and<br /><br />
<br /><br />
<br /><br />
<br /><br />
Security Service provision.<br /><br />
<br /><br />
SMHL Department/Unit personnel numbers classified into expatriates, and SL employees and contract<br /><br />
are summarized in the following table. Beginning February 2012, the mine has operated on three (3)<br /><br />
shifts each on eight (8) hours schedule for a twenty-four (24) hours period. Haulage fleets will be run on<br /><br />
two (2) shifts beginning at 700 am to 2300 pm at night. The remaining third (3rd) shift is schedule for ore<br /><br />
processing, repairs and maintenance only at the plant site.<br /><br />
Vimetco, like any other mining projects in a production stage and with a good human personnel<br /><br />
management record world-wide, operates it’s mine with normal hours of work not less than or more<br /><br />
than 44 hours per week.<br /><br />

<br /><br />
2.3 Ore Reserves and Exploration Methods<br /><br />
In 2003 the proven and probable bauxite reserves in the lease area were estimated at 12.5 million<br /><br />
metric tonnes with an overall grade of 53.1 percent Al2O3, 50.1 percent available alumina (av. Al2O3)<br /><br />
and SiO2. This reserve estimate has supported the production for the last ten years at an annual<br /><br />
production of 1.2 million metric tonnes. These reserves were established from the systematic drilling<br /><br />
and sampling carried out by Sieromco.<br /><br />
SMHL accepted the estimated resource figures during the course of their due diligence study in late<br /><br />
2009 undertaking by SC BELEVION IMPEX SRL. Based on historical data and those recently generated by<br /><br />
SMHL-Vimetco, a proven and probable reserve of 31 million tonnes of washed bauxite ore, grading 50.1<br /><br />
to 49.1% Al2O3 and 3.0 – 4.0% SiO2 have been reported. This reserve estimate will support production<br /><br />
for approximately 18 years at an annual production rate of 1.7 million metric tonnes. The current<br /><br />
reserves have been estimated from the Gondama Konta, Wunde and Gbonge groups of deposits.<br /><br />
Exploration work programme is in progress (i.e. mapping, drilling and sampling) to verify the tens of<br /><br />
millions of bauxite within or around the beneficiation plant and the Gbonge deposits in order to<br /><br />
establish newer deposits and to increase the current reserve status of SMHL lease. Development drilling<br /><br />
and sampling are also being carried out to support mine planning and eventual mining.<br /><br />
The exploration program involves line cutting, pitting, drilling, sampling and assaying. The entire lease<br /><br />
area was prospected and classified by Sieromco into measured, indicated or inferred resources. Based<br /><br />
on this available information, SMHL has primarily been engaged in validating these reported resources<br /><br />
(Konta, Wunde, Jenega and Gbonge) in generating its current reserve base and the extension of SMHL<br /><br />
mine life. Exploration pitting and drilling are initially done on a wide grid of 160 X 40 meters and then<br /><br />
closed in to 80x40 meter grid spacing for a resource purpose. Drill/pit grids are further reduced to a<br /><br />
40x40 or 40X20 meter grid for the purpose of estimating reserves.<br /><br />
Drilling is done using EVH Explorer 2012 Reverse circulation rig. The rig generates chips of laterite or<br /><br />
bauxite that are cut by drill bits down hole. The drill chips are sent to a sample collecting bin by an air<br /><br />
pressure. The drill chips are sampled and logged in approximately 1m intervals. Each sample is bagged<br /><br />
and labelled for assaying at Gondama Laboratory.<br /><br />
<br /><br />
Environmental and Social Impact Assessment (ESIA) Final Report: Prepared by TEDAEnvironmental Consultant: November: 2012<br /><br />
<br /><br />
Page 23<br /><br />
<br /><br />
At mine site laboratory samples are weighed to determine the bulk weight of each sample. Particle size<br /><br />
of samples which are +16mm, are reduced to ensure sample is 100% passing 16mm screen. Samples are<br /><br />
mixed to obtain a homogeneously representative sample and then split into two. One half is stored as<br /><br />
reserve sample and the remaining portion is oven dried at 105oC. Dried sample are crushed and<br /><br />
pulverized to 95% passing 500microns for PIMA and XRF analyses. Using the Portable Infra-red Mineral<br /><br />
Analyzer (PIMA), all the samples were analyzed for Total Alumina, Total Silica and Iron Oxides.<br /><br />
Ore Modeling<br /><br />
Preliminary ore modeling has been undertaken on the few of the deposits using Mine Map, a<br /><br />
resource/reserve modeling and mine planning software program which uses both inverse distance<br /><br />
weighting (IDW) and geostatistics to estimate reserves.<br /><br />
The drill hole data on the deposits are processed to meet the database upload requirements of the<br /><br />
software. Initial investigations on grade are made in terms of Available Alumina (AA), which is defined<br /><br />
as “percent alumina minus percent silica”. Definition of the database and upload of data are done,<br /><br />
followed by extraction of drill hole sections.<br /><br />
The drill holes are colored on the basis of grade. Using cut-offs of 48% AA, mineralized zones on each<br /><br />
section were demarcated, which would subsequently serve as constraints for block grade assignment.<br /><br />
Grade interpolation is done in a regularized block model using inverse distance weighting (IDW)<br /><br />
techniques. Block sizes are 10m by 10m in the y and x directions, and 5m in the z direction. Powers of 2<br /><br />
and 3 respectively were used, to enforce preferential weighting along strike over the cross-strike<br /><br />
direction.<br /><br />
<br /><br />
This is done in compliance with the notion that controls on mineralization (primarily<br /><br />
<br /><br />
lithological) have a greater influence along the strike of the ore body than across.<br /><br />
2.4 Mining and Ore Beneficiation<br /><br />
SMHL bauxite project at Gondama, southern Sierra Leone is based on auto-generated ‘self-mining’;<br /><br />
however, the project still requires significant level of capital expenditure inflows to procure the required<br /><br />
mining fleet and necessary support equipment. Prior to this time, the project was on contract mining<br /><br />
which became more costly on a unit cost basis and in November 2010, SMHL-Vimetco took over the<br /><br />
mining operation.<br /><br />
The production planning periods include the short-term plan (One Year) and a long-term plan (or more<br /><br />
years). The short-term plan is target oriented and driven more by Alum-Tulcea demand and<br /><br />
Environmental and Social Impact Assessment (ESIA) Final Report: Prepared by TEDAEnvironmental Consultant: November: 2012<br /><br />
<br /><br />
Page 24<br /><br />
<br /><br />
specifications of the washed bauxite product while the long range plan are covered by mining options,<br /><br />
mining sequence, mine development, operating cost estimates and strategic issues related to the overall<br /><br />
improvement of the mining operations.<br /><br />
Fig 2. Wide-Angle View of Administrative Building; Processing Plant; and Environs<br /><br />
<br /><br />
The SMHL bauxite mining is an open cast mining which requires no blasting of the ore prior to its<br /><br />
excavation. The overburden is removed by dozers and the ore is freely dug or ripped with dozers (D8,<br /><br />
D9). Prior to mining a deposit, the area is demarcated of its ore limit (Staking-out done by mine<br /><br />
surveyors); the demarcated area is then striped/cleared of vegetation and Topsoil, Overburden<br /><br />
removed, using a D8/D9 CAT bulldozer. The vegetation and Topsoil are stored and the overburden<br /><br />
stockpiled. The rotted vegetation and or Topsoil are stored for subsequent use in land re-forestation and<br /><br />
the overburden for reclamation purposes after depletion.<br /><br />
The exposed ore is then mined and excavated, loaded into 20 -25 tonne class off-highway rear dump<br /><br />
trucks, and transported to the washing plant. Multiple mining faces are developed and mined<br /><br />
simultaneously to achieve required blending. The material processing consist of feeding, crushing,<br /><br />
washing, vibratory-screening, classifying and storing of the bauxite ore to produce an exportable<br /><br />
Environmental and Social Impact Assessment (ESIA) Final Report: Prepared by TEDAEnvironmental Consultant: November: 2012<br /><br />
<br /><br />
Page 25<br /><br />
<br /><br />
material with a grade and reactive silica content that will satisfy costumer/market demands. Process<br /><br />
water for the plant is pumped from the Taia River over a distance of about 1.8 km to an existing water<br /><br />
reservoir of about 5,000 m3 from where the water gravitates to the washing plant.<br /><br />
The washing operation involves the removal of clays, silicate and ferruginous minerals/materials which<br /><br />
enters into the slimes as tails and the hand picking of gangue materials from the conveyor belts. The<br /><br />
aim is to upgrade the alumina content and reduce the silica content from the run of mine feed to a<br /><br />
marketable product.<br /><br />
To yield the 1.7 million tonnes of washed bauxite per year the plant operates at a minimum of 18.3<br /><br />
hours per day and 310 days per year. The maximum production rate depends upon the efficiency of the<br /><br />
mining methods and grade controls, since grades and ore recovery are the principal issues in achieving a<br /><br />
marketable product.<br /><br />
A supplementary new water pump has been installed in the plant to recycle used water in order to<br /><br />
reduce/decrease the quantity of water extracted from the Taia River. In an effort to reduce the amount<br /><br />
of slimes discharged into the tailings pond, the granulometric cut of the finest recovered bauxite in the<br /><br />
plant have been reduced from 2mm to 1mm to increase plant recovery/production and hence reduce<br /><br />
the volume of tails disposed into the ponds and hence the streams/rivers down valley. Figure3<br /><br />
represents a simplified processes flow diagram involved/employed in the recovery of washed bauxite<br /><br />
ore through the beneficiation plant.<br /><br />
<br /><br />
Environmental and Social Impact Assessment (ESIA) Final Report: Prepared by TEDAEnvironmental Consultant: November: 2012<br /><br />
<br /><br />
Page 26<br /><br />
<br /><br />
Fig: 3 SMHL Washing Plant Flow Diagram<br /><br />
<br /><br />
Environmental and Social Impact Assessment (ESIA) Final Report: Prepared by TEDAEnvironmental Consultant: November: 2012<br /><br />
<br /><br />
Page 27<br /><br />
<br /><br />
2.5 Bauxite Product Shipping<br /><br />
Final bauxite product is transported by road to the marine port at Nitti in haulage units of 30 tonne<br /><br />
capacity. The distance between the Gondama Washing Plant and Nitti is 38 kilometers. At Nitti, the<br /><br />
bauxite is stockpiled in preparation for shipment.<br /><br />
The existing dryer located at Nitti harbour is reported to have a rated drying capacity of 110 mtph. The<br /><br />
moisture content of washing plant bauxite product is expected to be 14-15% without the use of any<br /><br />
dewatering agents or dewatering screens. However, high moisture content bauxite is difficult to handle<br /><br />
due to its ‘stickiness’ and tendency to freeze in extremely cold weather conditions in parts of Europe.<br /><br />
Moisture reduction from 14-15% down to around 10%, will therefore be necessary to improve its<br /><br />
handling characteristics.<br /><br />
During shipment, the bauxite is loaded from the stockpile by front end loaders onto the conveyor and<br /><br />
into the barges of 2,000 tonne capacity. The loaded barges are moved by pushboat down the Sherbro<br /><br />
River to the Atlantic to meet the ore carriers that are specially adapted with mobile lifting cranes fitted<br /><br />
with grabs to load the bauxite into the ship. These ore carriers are generally compartmentalized into<br /><br />
hatches so that they can load more than one type of product in one shipment. The capacities of the ore<br /><br />
carriers that have been loaded from this port vary from 25,000 to 40,000 metric tons.<br /><br />
<br /><br />
2.6 Power Generation<br /><br />
SMHL runs three Power Generation each located at Nitti, Gondama and Mata camp where<br /><br />
accommodation and recreation facilities are located 20 km from Gondama and Nitti respectively.<br /><br />
Back-up generators will be stationed at the village and construction camp in case of a power outage in<br /><br />
the distribution grid that will normally supply these sites.<br /><br />
<br /><br />
2.7 Water Management<br /><br />
The main sources of water in the mines are from the Taia River and the dams that have been<br /><br />
constructed not far from the mining locations. Process water for the plant is pumped from the Taia River<br /><br />
over a distance of about 1.8 km to an existing water reservoir of about 5,000 m3 from where the water<br /><br />
gravitates to the washing plant naturally. The table below (Table 5) is a survey carried out to measure<br /><br />

2.8 MINES SECURITY<br /><br />
During the period from January 2003 to December 31, 2007, the mine site was under the protection of<br /><br />
SRL security force on a fulltime basis. In 2008, the G4S security service providers took over that<br /><br />
responsibility on January 1, 2008. All SRL personnel with good records were absorbed into the new G4S<br /><br />
Environmental and Social Impact Assessment (ESIA) Final Report: Prepared by TEDAEnvironmental Consultant: November: 2012<br /><br />
<br /><br />
Page 29<br /><br />
<br /><br />
security force. Suitable applicants were recruited and trained to fill the remaining vacant positions and<br /><br />
provide protection for all SMHL mine facilities.<br /><br />
The current G4S security force consists of 168 guards comprising 2 expatriate managers, 3 senior<br /><br />
supervisors, 15 junior supervisors, 12 master guards and 136 general security guards. The G4S and the<br /><br />
Sierra Leone Police Force (SLPF) establish a dual-purpose force comprising a Security Protection Force<br /><br />
(i.e. SLPF) who are armed to provide defensive capability in and around the mine facilities and a Security<br /><br />
Guard Force (G4S) to protect company assets against theft.<br /><br />
Over the past years, the SMHL mining areas have been incident free and the main focus of the security<br /><br />
force has been loss prevention. The SMHL Security Force has an excellent relationship with local<br /><br />
community leaders and local government authorities.<br /><br />

<br /><br />
Section Three - Policy, Legal and Instituitional Framework<br /><br />
3.1 National Legislations<br /><br />
Constitution of Sierra Leone, 1991<br /><br />
<br /><br />
For the purpose of mining activities, the Constitution makes provision for the protection of the rights of<br /><br />
individuals to private property, and also sets principles under which citizens may be deprived of their<br /><br />
property in the public interest as described in Section 21. It also makes provision for the prompt<br /><br />
payment of adequate compensation and access to the court or other impartial and independent<br /><br />
authority for the determination of the land owner’s interest or right, and the amount of any<br /><br />
compensation to which he/she is entitled and for the purpose of obtaining prompt payment of that<br /><br />
compensation.<br /><br />
Relevance to the Project: SMHL project is a central development activity that makes use of the human<br /><br />
and biophysical environment. As such, an EMP and crop compensation procedures are implemented to<br /><br />
address these issues.<br /><br />
<br /><br />
The National Environmental Policy: 1990<br /><br />
<br /><br />
The National Environmental Policy (NEP) was approved by cabinet since 1990 and was subsequently<br /><br />
revised in 1994 (GOSL, 1994). The policy objectives are to:<br /><br />
<br /><br />
<br /><br />
Secure for all Sierra Leoneans a quality of environment adequate for their health and well-being;<br /><br />
<br /><br />
<br /><br />
<br /><br />
Conserve and use the environment and natural resources for the benefit of present and future<br /><br />
generation; restore, maintain and enhance the ecosystems and ecological processes essential<br /><br />
for the functioning of the biosphere; to preserve biological diversity, and uphold the principle of<br /><br />
optimum sustainable yield in the use of living natural resources and ecosystems;<br /><br />
<br /><br />
<br /><br />
<br /><br />
Raise public awareness and promote understanding of the essential linkages between the<br /><br />
environment and development and to encourage individual participation in environmental<br /><br />
improvement efforts.<br /><br />
<br /><br />
Relevance to the Project: This ESIA was carried out to fulfil the obligatory requirements of the NEP since<br /><br />
mining is likely to have significant impacts on the environment.<br /><br />
<br /><br />
Environmental and Social Impact Assessment (ESIA) Final Report: Prepared by TEDAEnvironmental Consultant: November: 2012<br /><br />
<br /><br />
Page 31<br /><br />
<br /><br />
The Mines and Minerals Act, 2009<br /><br />
<br /><br />
This has been established to<br /><br />
<br /><br />
<br /><br />
consolidate and amend the law on mines and minerals;<br /><br />
<br /><br />
<br /><br />
<br /><br />
to promote local and foreign investment in the mining sector by introducing new and improved<br /><br />
provisions for exploration, mine development and marketing of minerals and mineral secondary<br /><br />
processing for the benefit of the people of Sierra Leone;<br /><br />
<br /><br />
<br /><br />
<br /><br />
to ensure that management of the minerals sector is transparent and accountable in<br /><br />
accordance with international best practice;<br /><br />
<br /><br />
<br /><br />
<br /><br />
to promote improved employment practices in the mining sector;<br /><br />
<br /><br />
<br /><br />
<br /><br />
to improve the welfare of communities adversely affected by mining;<br /><br />
<br /><br />
<br /><br />
<br /><br />
To address mining leases and license’s requirements;<br /><br />
<br /><br />
<br /><br />
<br /><br />
to introduce measures to reduce the harmful effects of mining activities on the environment<br /><br />
and;<br /><br />
<br /><br />
<br /><br />
<br /><br />
to provide for other related matters.<br /><br />
<br /><br />
The Wildlife Conservation Act, 1972<br /><br />
<br /><br />
The Wildlife Conservation Act of 1972 was enacted to help regulate the utilization and protection of<br /><br />
wildlife resources. However, the bill is characterized by inadequate capacity for implementation and<br /><br />
enforcement and insufficient and unsustainable sources of funding. The bill is also outdated and merits<br /><br />
review and update to reflect current trends in wildlife protection.<br /><br />
<br /><br />
Factories Act 1974<br /><br />
<br /><br />
The Factories Act of 1974 addresses workers health and safety issues associated with factories. Portions<br /><br />
of the construction activities fall within the definition of a factory based on the following conditions:<br /><br />
<br /><br />
<br /><br />
Part II section 3(v)-“any premises in which mechanical power is used in connection with the<br /><br />
making or repair of articles of metal or wood incidental to any business carried on by way of<br /><br />
trade or for purposes of gain”.<br /><br />
<br /><br />
The Factories Act also includes machines safety, safe working conditions, sanitary amenities, periodic<br /><br />
inspections, factory registration, and guidelines for reporting injuries, accidents and industrial diseases.<br /><br />
<br /><br />
Environmental and Social Impact Assessment (ESIA) Final Report: Prepared by TEDAEnvironmental Consultant: November: 2012<br /><br />
<br /><br />
Page 32<br /><br />
<br /><br />
Relevance to the Project: SMHL Environmental Health and Safety Plans have been designed to conform<br /><br />
to the provisions of this Act. All persons employed by SMHL are required to undergo medical<br /><br />
examinations to ascertain their fitness and also to maintain their health and safety at the workplace.<br /><br />
<br /><br />
Forestry Act: 1988<br /><br />
<br /><br />
The mining of bauxite involves the clearing of the natural vegetation or forest within its operational<br /><br />
area.<br /><br />
Section 18 of this Act stipulates that: The Chiefdom Council of any chiefdom may conclude an<br /><br />
agreement with the Chief Conservator of forests providing for the constitution as a community forest of<br /><br />
any land within the chiefdom, subject to the approval of the District Officer for the District in which the<br /><br />
land is situated.<br /><br />
Every Agreement under this section shall:<br /><br />
<br /><br />
<br /><br />
Describe the area included in the forest by reference to geographical features, markers, coordinates and measurements and indicate the same on a map of suitable scale, which shall be<br /><br />
annexed to the agreement.<br /><br />
<br /><br />
<br /><br />
<br /><br />
Describe the forest resources and potential of the area.<br /><br />
<br /><br />
<br /><br />
<br /><br />
Indicate the purpose of the forest, such as supply of fuel, building poles, production of<br /><br />
commercial timber, protection of soil and water supplies.<br /><br />
<br /><br />
<br /><br />
<br /><br />
Contain a detailed inventory of any rights that will be suppressed upon the constitution of the<br /><br />
forest and provide for adequate compensation for such rights; either in money or through the<br /><br />
allocation or equivalent rights in other land within the chiefdom.<br /><br />
<br /><br />
<br /><br />
<br /><br />
Contain a list of existing rights that will be confirmed by the agreement.<br /><br />
<br /><br />
<br /><br />
<br /><br />
Be valid for such period not exceeding 99 years as is reasonable in view of the purpose for which<br /><br />
the forest is to be constituted.<br /><br />
<br /><br />
Environmental and Social Impact Assessment (ESIA) Final Report: Prepared by TEDAEnvironmental Consultant: November: 2012<br /><br />
