<br /><br />
The National Minerals Agency Act 2012<br /><br />
<br /><br />
The National Minerals Agency Act is established to:<br /><br />
<br /><br />
<br /><br />
Promote the development of the minerals sector by effectively and efficiently managing the<br /><br />

<br /><br />

<br /><br />
<br /><br />
<br /><br />

<br /><br />
Environmental Protection Agency Act, 2008 and Environmental Protection Agency<br /><br />
<br /><br />
(Amendment)<br /><br />
<br /><br />
Act, 2010<br /><br />
<br /><br />
This Act established the Sierra Leone Environmental Protection Agency (SLEPA), to provide for the<br /><br />
effective protection of the environment and for other related matters. It mandates the EPA among<br /><br />
others to:<br /><br />
<br /><br />
<br /><br />
Advise the minister on the formulation of policies on all aspects of the environment.<br /><br />
<br /><br />
<br /><br />
<br /><br />
Issue environmental permits and pollution abatement notices for controlling the volume, types,<br /><br />
constituents and effects of waste discharges, emissions, deposits or other sources of pollutants<br /><br />
of substances which are hazardous and dangerous to the quality of the environment.<br /><br />
<br /><br />
<br /><br />
<br /><br />
Prescribe standards and guidelines relating to ambient air, water and soil quality, air pollution,<br /><br />
water, land and other forms of environmental pollution including the discharge of waste and<br /><br />
the control of toxic substances.<br /><br />
<br /><br />
<br /><br />
<br /><br />
Ensure compliance with any environmental impact assessment procedures laid down in the<br /><br />
planning and execution of development projects.<br /><br />
<br /><br />
<br /><br />
<br /><br />
Impose and collect environmental protection levies in accordance with this Act or regulations<br /><br />
made under this Act.<br /><br />
<br /><br />

Section 24 of the Act lists project activities requiring an Environmental Impact Assessment license which<br /><br />

<br /><br />
Sections 25 and 26 describe factors for determining whether a project requires an environmental<br /><br />

The Act describes the procedures to be followed to obtain permits for both existing and proposed<br /><br />

<br /><br />
Relevance to the Project: Land, water, forest and air resources are major components of the natural<br /><br />
environment. Bauxite mining operations at all stages are impacting on these resources.Extractive uses of<br /><br />
these resources are guided by the various programmes, plans, regulations under the EPA/SL. Adherence<br /><br />
to the relevant legal provision is a requirement in the entire course of SMHL operations.<br /><br />
<br /><br />
Local Government Act, 2004<br /><br />
<br /><br />
The Act establishes the Local Council (LC) as the highest political authority in the locality and confers<br /><br />
legislative and executive powers to be exercised in accordance with this Act. This Act in its First Schedule<br /><br />
under Section 2 establishes the localities, namely: districts, towns and cities. Part II of this schedule also<br /><br />
establishes the number of Paramount Chiefs in each LC. The Third Schedule establishes the functions<br /><br />
devolved to the LCs. The Fourth and Fifth Schedules establish departments under each LC, and a<br /><br />
Valuation List and Rate Books respectively.<br /><br />
<br /><br />
Relevance to the Project: Public consultation and community involvement constitute a core element of<br /><br />
SMHL operations. The implementation of the Community Development Action Plan is done in<br /><br />
collaboration with the local council.<br /><br />
National Lands Policy, 2005<br /><br />
<br /><br />
As provided in the Constitution, the 2005 National Land Policy makes provision for the compulsory<br /><br />
acquisition of land in the public interest. The principles of the land policy include among others:<br /><br />
<br /><br />
<br /><br />
The principle of land as a common national or communal property resource held in trust for the<br /><br />
people and which must be used in the long term interest of the people of Sierra Leone.<br /><br />
<br /><br />
Environmental and Social Impact Assessment (ESIA) Final Report: Prepared by TEDAEnvironmental Consultant: November: 2012<br /><br />
<br /><br />
Page 35<br /><br />
<br /><br />
<br /><br />
<br /><br />
Compensation to be paid for lands acquired through compulsory government acquisition will be<br /><br />
fair and adequate and will be determined, among other things, through negotiations that take<br /><br />
into consideration government investment in the area.<br /><br />
<br /><br />
<br /><br />
<br /><br />
Local Authorities (City and District Councils) may negotiate for land for project development<br /><br />
purposes, but all such grants should be properly documented and processed.<br /><br />
<br /><br />
<br /><br />
<br /><br />
No interest in or right over any land belonging to an individual or family can be disposed of<br /><br />
without consultation with the owner or occupier of the land.<br /><br />
<br /><br />
No interest in or right over any land belonging to an individual or family can be compulsorily acquired<br /><br />
without payment, in reasonable time, of fair and adequate compensation.<br /><br />
<br /><br />
Relevance to the project: During mining, large land areas are acquired for exploration, excavation and<br /><br />
removal of bauxite deposits. SMHL management will therefore be required to ensure proper<br /><br />
reclamation work.<br /><br />
3.2 International Regulatory Framework<br /><br />
Sierra Leone is signatory to many regulatory framework relevant to mining. Below is a summary of the<br /><br />
key conventions, treaties and protocols.<br /><br />
United Nations Convention on Biodiversity<br /><br />
<br /><br />
Sierra Leone became signatory to this Convention in 1994. This is one of the tools that translate the<br /><br />
Principles of Agenda 21 also known as the Rio Summit. Parties to this convention affirm their right over<br /><br />
their biological resources making sure that these resources are used in a sustainable manner.<br /><br />
The relevance of the CBD stems from the fact that mining activities result to:<br /><br />
<br /><br />
<br /><br />
Loss of habitat and fragmentation and the resultant development of waste rock dumps;<br /><br />
<br /><br />
<br /><br />
<br /><br />
Pollution of habitats and water supplies and the creation solid waste such as tailings;<br /><br />
<br /><br />
<br /><br />
<br /><br />
Green House gas emission resulting from the use of heavy duty machinery.<br /><br />
<br /><br />
If adequate mitigation measure measures are not undertaken biodiversity in the project area could be<br /><br />
threatened or lost.<br /><br />
<br /><br />
Relevance to the Project: As much as possible SMHL operations will involve implementation of<br /><br />
mitigation measures for biodiversity conservation. Sierra Minerals Holdings Ltd. will develop a strategic<br /><br />
response to biodiversity conservation and management to enable it to meet the wide range of<br /><br />
expectations of all stakeholders with interests in the company and its activities.<br /><br />
Environmental and Social Impact Assessment (ESIA) Final Report: Prepared by TEDAEnvironmental Consultant: November: 2012<br /><br />
<br /><br />
Page 36<br /><br />
<br /><br />
In the area of biodiversity conservation, SMHL will develop partnership with leading international<br /><br />
organizations and institutions through local institutions and NGOs who will provide a conservation<br /><br />
perspective on the challenges and opportunities raised by mining operations. An outline of strategic<br /><br />
biodiversity issues will be carried out with a concept note setting out a case for biodiversity conservation<br /><br />
and management. Key elements of the strategic thrust will be developed to help SMHL improve on its<br /><br />
biodiversity performance through:<br /><br />
 Identification of major threats, challenges and opportunities to the conservation and<br /><br />
management of biodiversity in the mining area;<br /><br />
 Identification and development of partnership for biodiversity conservation;<br /><br />
 Identification of cross cutting issues addressed by other community organizations; and<br /><br />
 Development of small projects on biodiversity conservation.<br /><br />
<br /><br />
United Nations Framework Convention on Climate Change (UNFCC)<br /><br />
<br /><br />
As a signatory to this convention in 1995, Sierra Leone gathers and share information on greenhouse gas<br /><br />
emissions. The mining of bauxite requires enormous use of fossil fuel in the heavy equipment with<br /><br />
negative implications for the production of harmful emissions. Further, Aluminum is produced by the<br /><br />
electrolysis of alumina. Carbon dioxide is produced by the chemical reaction of oxygen in the carbon<br /><br />
lined steel pots in which alumina is produced. In this process, the perflouronated carbon compound<br /><br />
(PFC), tetraflouramethane (CF4) and hexaflouroethane C2F6) are the powerful greenhouse gases<br /><br />
generated.<br /><br />
<br /><br />
Relevance to the Project: An Environmental Monitoring Plan for air pollution has been prepared to<br /><br />
address air emission in the mining operations. In the area of climate change, SMHL will work with the<br /><br />
(IMMC) and other companies to increase energy efficiency across its production processes in the<br /><br />
following ways:<br /><br />
 Identification of climate related risks and opportunities and development of responses that will<br /><br />
be implemented and monitored using the existing Environmental Management Systems;<br /><br />
 Long-term modeling of likely changes in temperature and rainfall that could pose a hazard to<br /><br />
SMHL’s operations;<br /><br />
 Assessment of climate change impacts for both the operations and affected communities;<br /><br />
Environmental and Social Impact Assessment (ESIA) Final Report: Prepared by TEDAEnvironmental Consultant: November: 2012<br /><br />
<br /><br />
Page 37<br /><br />
<br /><br />
 Ensuring that SMHL’s operations will withstand increased frequency and magnitude of extreme<br /><br />
weather events;<br /><br />
 Joint venturing with nearby SRL to invest in biodiesel production and other agricultural<br /><br />
production as alternative energy resources; and<br /><br />
 Assess other risks that do not have an immediate risk but will harm mining operations in other<br /><br />
ways.<br /><br />
Convention on Wetlands of International Importance Especially as Waterfowl Habitat:<br /><br />
<br /><br />
(Ramsar<br /><br />
<br /><br />
Convention).<br /><br />
<br /><br />
The objectives of the Ramsar Convention are to ensure the wise use of wetlands through stemming the<br /><br />
progressive encroachment on and loss of wetlands now and in the future, while recognizing their<br /><br />
fundamental ecological functions, and economic, cultural, scientific, and recreational value.<br /><br />
The Ramsar Convention on Wetlands (Ramsar) signed by Sierra Leone on 13 December 1999, became<br /><br />
effective on 13 April 2000. Signatory countries to the Ramsar Convention agree to:<br /><br />
<br /><br />
<br /><br />
Include conservation of wetlands in land use planning throughout the country, including<br /><br />
the promotion of ‘wise use’ of wetlands;<br /><br />
<br /><br />
<br /><br />
<br /><br />
Establish nature reserves within wetland areas;<br /><br />
<br /><br />
<br /><br />
<br /><br />
Promote training in the fields of research, management; and<br /><br />
<br /><br />
<br /><br />
<br /><br />
Consult with other signatory countries about implementation of the convention<br /><br />
especially in areas of shared wetlands, shared water systems, and shared species.<br /><br />
<br /><br />
As a party of the Ramsar Convention, Sierra Leone has designated two coastal wetlands as Ramsar Sites,<br /><br />
which are managed under Ramsar regulations.<br /><br />
Based on current information, no Ramsar-specific issues are associated with SMHL mining activities.<br /><br />
<br /><br />
Convention Concerning the Protection of Workers against Occupational Hazards in the<br /><br />
Working Environment due to Air Pollution, Noise, and Vibration (ILO No. 148).<br /><br />
<br /><br />
Sierra Leone became signatory to this convention in 1961. Participating parties may accept the<br /><br />
obligations of this convention separately in respect of air pollution, noise, and vibration. Measures<br /><br />
instituted to occupational hazards associated with these impacts, shall be prescribed by national laws<br /><br />
and regulations as outlined in the Factories Act of 1974.<br /><br />
Environmental and Social Impact Assessment (ESIA) Final Report: Prepared by TEDAEnvironmental Consultant: November: 2012<br /><br />
<br /><br />
Page 38<br /><br />
<br /><br />
Relevance to the Project<br /><br />
The Management of SMHL recognises that the safety and security of its employees and the communities<br /><br />
in which it operates are an integral part of its operations. It sets up an environment in which people<br /><br />
believe it is possible to work injury free, regardless of what role they perform. This is based on the<br /><br />
following guiding principles and policy:<br /><br />
 It makes sure that all injuries are preventable. As such, any work that cannot be done safely<br /><br />
should not be done;<br /><br />
 It ensures that training is provided for all employees so that people will work in a safe and<br /><br />
responsible manner;<br /><br />
 It ensures that its health and safety performances comply with relevant legislation and exceed<br /><br />
community expectations; and<br /><br />
 Avoid the unnecessary use of any hazardous chemicals. Promote awareness with regard to the<br /><br />
storage of all hazardous chemicals and substances throughout the business. Ensure adequate<br /><br />
steps to protect both human health and the environment where the use of such chemicals or<br /><br />
substances is unavoidable.<br /><br />
As part of its Environmental Management Plan, an Occupational Health & Safety Plan is prepared with<br /><br />
the following objectives:<br /><br />
 Define and communicate the principles, practices and expectations of Health & Safety<br /><br />
objectives, SMHL project;<br /><br />
 Set out how the project is designed to manage and reduce risks associated with both routine<br /><br />
activities and unplanned events;<br /><br />
 Define the criteria against which Health & Safety performance will be judged;<br /><br />
<br /><br />
<br /><br />
Describe areas of Health & Safety responsibilities for the SMHL Project team, including<br /><br />
Contractors;<br /><br />
<br /><br />
 Describe the Occupational Health & Safety Standard Operating Procedures (SOPs) that will<br /><br />
ensure that Health & Safety activities are organised, managed and reported in a systematic and<br /><br />
consistent manner;<br /><br />
<br /><br />
<br /><br />
Describe the development of the safety cases and safety plans;<br /><br />
<br /><br />
<br /><br />
<br /><br />
Ensure that the Project provides inherently safe and secure facilities;<br /><br />
<br /><br />
 Address the process of ongoing assessment and continuous improvement of health and safety<br /><br />
performance;<br /><br />

<br /><br />
Convention on International Trade of Endangered Species (CITES)<br /><br />
<br /><br />
The Convention on the International Trade of Endangered Species (CITES) requirements went into effect<br /><br />
in Sierra Leone on 16 January 1995. CITES is a convention that provides for the elimination and/or<br /><br />
reduction in the trade of certain species, including endangered species. CITES provides a list of species<br /><br />
that are required for protection against trade. Species listed in CITES are also those for which trade is<br /><br />
strictly prohibited except in exceptional circumstances. The majority of the species listed in CITES are<br /><br />
also listed as endangered or threatened by the International Union for Conservation of Nature and<br /><br />
Natural Resources (IUCN). CITES also includes species that are not necessarily threatened but for which<br /><br />
trade must be controlled to avoid the species becoming threatened or endangered. While a number of<br /><br />
species exist in the project area, there are no specific issues related to them.<br /><br />
<br /><br />
African Convention on the Conservation of Nature and Natural Resources<br /><br />
<br /><br />
This convention was signed by Sierra Leone in 1968.The African Union (AU) took into force the<br /><br />
protection of the natural environment. The main goals of the convention were to draw up a document<br /><br />
that enhanced environmental protection fostered the conservation and sustainable use of natural<br /><br />
resources, and brought together and coordinated policies in these fields. The states are to recognize the<br /><br />
importance of the natural environment, and create laws and regulations that protect it.<br /><br />
<br /><br />
World Health Organization (WHO 1999)<br /><br />
<br /><br />
World Health Organization (WHO 1999) has established Noise Level Guidelines for various Land Uses.<br /><br />
For residential dwellings or areas used for human habitation, WHO recommends that sound levels be no<br /><br />
higher than 50-55 dB for 16-hours of exposure during daytime hours and 45 dB for 8-hours of exposure<br /><br />
at night. WHO also recommends that persons should never be exposed to a maximum sound level<br /><br />
Environmental and Social Impact Assessment (ESIA) Final Report: Prepared by TEDAEnvironmental Consultant: November: 2012<br /><br />
<br /><br />
Page 40<br /><br />
<br /><br />
(Lmax) greater than 110 dB or a peak sound pressure level (instantaneous, not fast Lmax) greater than<br /><br />
140 dB for adults and 120 dB for children.<br /><br />
<br /><br />
Relevance to the Project<br /><br />
<br /><br />
An Environmental Monitoring Plan has been prepared specifically designed and implemented<br /><br />
for collecting data on ambient air, water quality, noise level including release of pollutants of<br /><br />
major concern and weather.<br /><br />
The Equator Principles (2003)<br /><br />
<br /><br />
This is a set of guidelines signed by ten Banks from seven countries for assessing and managing<br /><br />
environmental and social risk in project financing. The EPs, is based on the International Finance<br /><br />
Corporation (IFC) Performance Standards on social and environmental sustainability and the World Bank<br /><br />
Group Environmental, Health, and Safety Guidelines (EHS Guidelines). They are intended to serve as a<br /><br />
baseline and framework for implementation by each adopting institution on its own social and<br /><br />
environmental policies, principles and practices to its project financing activities.<br /><br />
The banks are pledging to apply this framework to all projects with a capital cost above US$ 50 million,<br /><br />
in all industries globally, and commit ‘not to provide loans directly to projects where the borrower will<br /><br />
not or is unable to comply with [the banks’] environmental and social policies and processes’.<br /><br />
<br /><br />
In a first step, banks will categorize a project in one of three groups, A, B, or C, (group A comprises the<br /><br />
more risky projects) following the environmental and social screening criteria of the International<br /><br />
Finance Corporation (IFC), the private sector arm of the World Bank Group.<br /><br />
For all group A and B projects, sponsors are requested to carry out an Environmental Assessment (EA) in<br /><br />
line with the IFC Safeguard Policies, and World Bank and IFC Pollution Prevention and Abatement<br /><br />
Guidelines. For all group A projects and certain group B projects the sponsor will also prepare and<br /><br />
implement an Environmental Management Plan (EMP), consult with various stakeholders, and report on<br /><br />
the implementation of the EMP.<br /><br />
A number of quality controls are envisaged: compulsory independent expert reviews of EAs and EMPs<br /><br />
for group A projects; and independent expert review of compliance when judged necessary.<br /><br />
<br /><br />
Environmental and Social Impact Assessment (ESIA) Final Report: Prepared by TEDAEnvironmental Consultant: November: 2012<br /><br />
<br /><br />

Policy Issue 4<br /><br />
Land acquisitions for mining are also likely to cause social disruption and economic loss for communities.<br /><br />
As such, it is important that disturbance and loss to the affected communities due to the project are<br /><br />
minimized through proper planning from a very early stage of the project.<br /><br />
Response<br /><br />
A base line socio economic survey is part of the scoping study to identify the degree of impact of the<br /><br />
project on the affected communities and explore the possibility of minimizing these adverse impacts. No<br /><br />
resettlement is envisaged in the existing project. Should there be any possible resettlement, a<br /><br />
Resettlement Plan for the affected people will be prepared and implemented. In addition, extensive<br /><br />
measures are currently taken to avoid displacement of people or other resettlement impacts.<br /><br />
The Equator Principles are aligned with the requirements of the International Finance Corporation (IFC),<br /><br />
which has established a set of Performance Standards that govern environmental considerations of<br /><br />
project financing. The IFC‟s Performance Standards have therefore been used as the yardstick against<br /><br />
which to measure compliance with the Equator Principles. A summary of the IFC Safeguard Policies is<br /><br />
presented below.<br /><br />
<br /><br />
WORLD BANK/INTERNATIONAL FINANCE CORPORATION: SAFEGUARD POLICIES and GUIDELINES.<br /><br />
<br /><br />
The following IFC/World Bank Operational Policies (OP) and guidelines are relevant to the SMHL Project.<br /><br />
<br /><br />
<br /><br />
The World Bank, International Finance Corporation (IFC), and World Health Organization (WHO)<br /><br />
Standards World Bank projects must address effects of the project on biodiversity. Biodiversity<br /><br />
is defined at three levels (ecosystem, species, and genetic) and is often interpreted as a measure<br /><br />
of biological complexity and variation within Project areas. Although there is little specific<br /><br />
regulatory guidance on methods of assessing and monitoring biodiversity, the concept is widely<br /><br />
interpreted, especially internationally, as a measure of ecosystem integrity and stability.<br /><br />
<br /><br />
Key principles defined within both set of guidelines are as follows:<br /><br />
Environmental and Social Impact Assessment (ESIA) Final Report: Prepared by TEDAEnvironmental Consultant: November: 2012<br /><br />
<br /><br />
Page 43<br /><br />
<br /><br />
<br /><br />
<br /><br />
Physical and economic dislocation should be avoided or minimized as much as possible.<br /><br />
<br /><br />
<br /><br />
<br /><br />
Unavoidable displacement should involve the preparation and implementation of a<br /><br />
Resettlement Action Plan, which demonstrates how international best practices have been/will<br /><br />
be met and defines a detailed approach and work plan to guide implementation, handover, and<br /><br />
monitoring and evaluation.<br /><br />
<br /><br />
<br /><br />
<br /><br />
Resettlement should be viewed as a sustainable development program designed to improve the<br /><br />
quality of life of those affected.<br /><br />
<br /><br />
<br /><br />
<br /><br />
Legal standing is not synonymous with eligibility: even those people with no recognizable legal<br /><br />
right or claim to the land they are occupying should be considered eligible for resettlement<br /><br />
assistance.<br /><br />
<br /><br />
<br /><br />
<br /><br />
Resettlement should be a consultative and participatory process: affected people should be<br /><br />
consulted thoroughly, participate in the definition of resettlement policies and procedures, and<br /><br />
in the end choose for themselves from among acceptable and clearly defined alternatives.<br /><br />
<br /><br />
<br /><br />
<br /><br />
OP 4.30 – Involuntary Resettlement: International best practice for private sector-related<br /><br />
resettlement is commonly defined by the June 1990 World Bank Operational Directive 4.30. This<br /><br />
Operational Directive is applied around the world by private investors to govern involuntary<br /><br />
resettlement associated with natural resource and infrastructure development. The Directive<br /><br />
sets the benchmark against which such projects are gauged by international financial<br /><br />
institutions, both private and multi-lateral. Operational Directive 4.30 is internationally<br /><br />
acknowledged as one of the most comprehensive and efficient set of international standards<br /><br />
with respect to the protection of the rights of affected people.<br /><br />
<br /><br />
The key principles of this policy are:<br /><br />
<br /><br />
<br /><br />
Physical and economic dislocation should be avoided or minimized where feasible;<br /><br />
<br /><br />
<br /><br />
<br /><br />
Unavoidable displacement should involve the preparation and implementation of a<br /><br />
resettlement plan. All involuntary resettlement should be conceived and executed as<br /><br />
development programs, with resettlers provided with sufficient investment resources and<br /><br />
opportunities to share in project benefits;<br /><br />
<br /><br />
<br /><br />
<br /><br />
Displaced persons should be (i) compensated for their losses at full replacement cost prior to<br /><br />
the actual move; (ii) assisted with the move and supported during the transition period in the<br /><br />
resettlement site; and (iii) assisted in their efforts to improve their former living standards,<br /><br />
<br /><br />
Environmental and Social Impact Assessment (ESIA) Final Report: Prepared by TEDAEnvironmental Consultant: November: 2012<br /><br />
<br /><br />
Page 44<br /><br />
<br /><br />
income earning capacity, and production levels, or at least to restore them. Particular attention<br /><br />
should be paid to the needs of the poorest groups to be resettled;<br /><br />
<br /><br />
<br /><br />
Community participation in planning and implementing resettlement should be encouraged.<br /><br />
Appropriate patterns of social organization should be established, and existing social and<br /><br />
cultural institutions of resettlers and their hosts should be supported and used to the greatest<br /><br />
extent possible;<br /><br />
<br /><br />
<br /><br />
<br /><br />

impacts on host communities are minimized; and Introduction 1 - 28<br /><br />

affected population. The absence of legal title to land should not be a bar to compensation.<br /><br />

<br /><br />
<br /><br />
OP 4.01 – Environmental Assessment: All projects proposed for IFC financing require an<br /><br />
Environmental Assessment (EA) to ensure that they are environmentally and socially<br /><br />
sustainable. Instruments used to perform the EA may include an environmental impact<br /><br />
assessment, an environmental audit, a hazard or risk assessment, and an environmental action<br /><br />
plan. All IFC projects must be categorized. This policy also sets forth the minimum requirements<br /><br />
for public consultation and public disclosure for projects.<br /><br />
<br /><br />
<br /><br />
<br /><br />
OP 4.04 – Natural Habitats: This policy affirms IFC’s commitment to promote and support<br /><br />
natural habitat conservation and improved land use, and the protection, maintenance, and<br /><br />
rehabilitation of natural habitats and their functions in its project financing. IFC does not<br /><br />
support projects that involve significant conversion or degradation of critical natural habitats.<br /><br />
<br /><br />
<br /><br />
<br /><br />
OP 4.09 – Pest Management: This policy outlines IFC’s support of the use of biological or<br /><br />
environmental control methods instead of pesticides where there is a need for pest<br /><br />
management. Criteria for the use of pesticides, when required, are described.<br /><br />
<br /><br />
<br /><br />
<br /><br />
OP 4.11 - Safeguarding Cultural Property: International best practices with respect to<br /><br />
safeguarding cultural property in IFC-financed projects includes sites having archaeological<br /><br />
(prehistoric), paleontological, historical, religious, and unique natural values. Operational Policy<br /><br />
(OP) 4.11 specifies that the general policy of the World Bank is to assist in preserving cultural<br /><br />
properties and to seek to avoid their elimination.<br /><br />
<br /><br />
<br /><br />
<br /><br />
OP 4.37 – Safety of Dams (September 1999 draft): IFC requires that dams be designed and<br /><br />
constructed by experienced and competent professionals. For dams that are over 15 meters<br /><br />
high; and dams between 10 and 15 meters that present special design complexities, IFC requires<br /><br />

Guidelines include requirements for air emissions, liquid effluents, hazardous chemicals and wastes,<br /><br />
solid wastes, and ambient noise.<br /><br />
<br /><br />

child labor. Forced labor, as defined in the policy statement, consists of all work or service, not<br /><br />
voluntarily performed, that is exacted from an individual under threat of force or penalty.<br /><br />
Harmful child labor consists of employment of children that is economically exploitive, or is<br /><br />

3.3 Institutional Framework<br /><br />
Ministry of Mines and Mineral Resources: The management of all mineral resources in Sierra Leone<br /><br />
is within the purview of the Ministry of Mines and Mineral Resources (MMR) headed by a Minister. The<br /><br />
Ministry has two divisions, Mines and Geological Surveys, both headed by Directors. The administrative<br /><br />
wing of the ministry is headed by a Permanent Secretary. The main responsibility of this Ministry is to<br /><br />
develop policies and programmes for the systematic and economic exploitation of the nation’s mineral<br /><br />
resources and to formulate regulations for the mining industry and related activities to ensure that the<br /><br />
nation derives maximum benefit from the mineral resources. The specific responsibilities include:<br /><br />
<br /><br />
<br /><br />
Formulation of policies for mines and minerals;<br /><br />
<br /><br />
<br /><br />
<br /><br />
Mining negotiations;<br /><br />
<br /><br />
<br /><br />
<br /><br />
Granting of concessions<br /><br />
<br /><br />
<br /><br />
<br /><br />
Mining and minerals exploration<br /><br />
<br /><br />
<br /><br />
<br /><br />
Geological surveys;<br /><br />
<br /><br />
<br /><br />
<br /><br />
Control of explosives for mining;<br /><br />
<br /><br />
<br /><br />
<br /><br />
Inspection of mining machinery and mine sites;<br /><br />
<br /><br />
<br /><br />
<br /><br />
Establishing relations with international mining and geological organizations and<br /><br />
agencies; and<br /><br />
<br /><br />

The National Minerals Agency: This is a new semi-autonomous government agency that is<br /><br />
established by an Act of Parliament (National Minerals Agency Act 2012). It is responsible for the<br /><br />
administration and regulation of the minerals sector. While the MMMR takes full responsibility of policy<br /><br />
implementation, the NMA handles the responsibility of policy implementation. It works closely with<br /><br />
other government agencies with the responsibilities in the mineral sector to support them in<br /><br />
implementing those policy areas from which they are responsible and impact upon the mineral sector. It<br /><br />
is also empowered to hire professionals and reward them according to their performance. The agency is<br /><br />
overseen by a Board of Directors, which helps to remove it from day-day politics.<br /><br />
Environment Protection Agency: This is the agency responsible for the monitoring and enforcing all<br /><br />
environmental regulations in Sierra Leone. Details of the roles and responsibilities are outlined in the<br /><br />
EPA Act presented above.<br /><br />
Other Institutions and line Ministries responsible for environmental management issues in Sierra Leone<br /><br />

Ministry of Agriculture, Forestry and Food Security: To increase agricultural productivity, output,<br /><br />
rural income and employment while ensuring adequate protection of the environment.<br /><br />
Ministry of Lands, Housing, Country Planning and the Environment: Conserves and manages the<br /><br />
natural environment of the country; b. to address the issues of land acquisition, ownership and use.<br /><br />
Ministry of Transport and Communications: Addresses the issues related to transport and<br /><br />
communications. This ministry is involved in land degradation and the introduction of pollutants in the<br /><br />
natural environment, and as such mitigation measures are set for the ministry to implement in the<br /><br />
amelioration of pollution.<br /><br />
Ministry of Local Government and Rural Development: Provides support to the MMMR to manage<br /><br />
certain development funds, monitor the reclamation of mined out areas and coordinate the Diamond<br /><br />
Area Community Development Fund (DACDF).<br /><br />
Ministry of Tourism and Culture: To protect the country’s heritage including monuments, cultural and<br /><br />
historical sites. The development of tourism often leads to infrastructural activities that deter sound<br /><br />

Section 4 – Analyses of Alternatives<br /><br />
Analyses of alternatives constitute part of this ESIA. The purpose is to improve on project design,<br /><br />
construction and operation decision making based on feasible project alternatives (IFC, 2006). It will<br /><br />
provide the basis for identifying optimal project alternatives that are consistent with the goals of the<br /><br />
mining company, funding organization, the government, the mining community and the policy and<br /><br />
regulatory framework established for the mining industry.<br /><br />
Project alternatives that were considered for SMHL project include:<br /><br />
<br /><br />
<br /><br />
No Action alternative;<br /><br />
<br /><br />
<br /><br />
<br /><br />
Alternative for location of staff accommodation;<br /><br />
<br /><br />
<br /><br />
<br /><br />
Bauxite processing location;<br /><br />
<br /><br />
<br /><br />
<br /><br />
Mine closure.<br /><br />
<br /><br />
No Action Alternative<br /><br />
The adoption of the no-action alternative would mean that the project would remain in its current<br /><br />
condition with no mine-related development activity by SMHL. This scenario would mean that the<br /><br />
Environmental Management Plan and the community Development Action Plan that are prepared would<br /><br />
not be implemented. As such, the anticipated project benefits such as employment, foreign exchange<br /><br />
earnings, infrastructure development and community development initiatives would not improve in the<br /><br />
concession area and the country as a whole. This will result in the continuation of in migration into the<br /><br />
area and the resultant poor state of local economy, infrastructure and other public services.<br /><br />
The negative effect would also be seen in the physical environment where in the Taia River and its<br /><br />
tributaries will be continuously impacted by discharge of effluents thereby reducing the surface water<br /><br />
quality of the basin.<br /><br />
Location of staff Accommodation<br /><br />
The first plant site for bauxite mining including all mining activities was first around the Mokanji area.<br /><br />
Following the depletion of the Mokanji deposits in 1982, Sieromco relocated its operations to Gondama<br /><br />
(approximately 26 km south-east of Mokanji) and resumed mining in the Banta Mokelleh Chiefdom.<br /><br />
Sieromco mined the Gondama deposits and has since continued operations there.<br /><br />
Environmental and Social Impact Assessment (ESIA) Final Report: Prepared by TEDAEnvironmental Consultant: November: 2012<br /><br />
<br /><br />
Page 50<br /><br />
<br /><br />
The two styles of accommodation provided for SMHL staff are located 20 – 40 miles away from the plant<br /><br />
site where most of the mining activities take place. The mining activities at the site do not require total<br /><br />
off-site accommodation especially for supervision work during the second and third shifts. The option<br /><br />
therefore is the relocation or expansion of both junior and senior staff accommodation closer to the<br /><br />
mine site. Proximity to the mine site would lead to the following benefits:<br /><br />
<br /><br />
<br /><br />
Reduction in travel time to work;<br /><br />
<br /><br />
<br /><br />
<br /><br />
Increased work supervision at all times;<br /><br />
<br /><br />
<br /><br />
<br /><br />
Proximity to the only Medical Hospital in the mining area;<br /><br />
<br /><br />
<br /><br />
<br /><br />
Decrease in the running cost of vehicles;<br /><br />
<br /><br />
<br /><br />
<br /><br />
Extension of overtime work for certain staff categories;<br /><br />
<br /><br />
<br /><br />
<br /><br />
Increase in the opportunity for the expansion of the Gbonge deposits and future expansion of<br /><br />
shipping facility closer to the four bauxite deposits.<br /><br />
<br /><br />
Ore Processing Plant<br /><br />
The original ore processing plant for the bauxite mine was located at Mokanji. The plant was abandoned<br /><br />
when mining relocated to the Gondama area in 1982. The existing processing plant is in a closer<br /><br />
proximity to the five major deposits and is located within a valley thereby reducing noise level that<br /><br />
impact on the surrounding village communities. For operational considerations, the tailings storage<br /><br />
facility was located as close as possible to the plant site to reduce cost of transportation of the slurry.<br /><br />
The potential for the existing facility to near its storage capacity is high considering mine life of SMHL<br /><br />
operations around the five deposits. Consideration should be given for an alternative storage facility in<br /><br />
the near future.<br /><br />
<br /><br />
Two alternative methods for the disposal of the tailings generated at the site are proposed: a. Disposal<br /><br />
and storage of existing slurries in a min pit and reuse of the existing infrastructure; b. Construction of a<br /><br />
new facility within the same area close to the washing plant.<br /><br />
<br /><br />
Environmental and Social Impact Assessment (ESIA) Final Report: Prepared by TEDAEnvironmental Consultant: November: 2012<br /><br />
<br /><br />
Page 51<br /><br />
<br /><br />
Tailings Disposal and Storage<br /><br />
This option will involve a construction of a mine pit into a tailings storage contained by perimeter<br /><br />
embankments. The slurries would be deposited in the pit as mining continues. Filling would continue for<br /><br />
less than a year and after a decanting period, revegetation would start followed by a full reclamation<br /><br />
work based on the mine reclamation plan.<br /><br />
With perimeter fencing, the area would be secured with no additional impacts.<br /><br />
The major disadvantages of this alternative would be related to short term mine plan as this operation<br /><br />
will concurrently go on with other mining operations.<br /><br />
<br /><br />
Construction of a new storage facility<br /><br />
This option would require concurrent construction work and mining operations with little obstruction.<br /><br />
Construction work would however be more expensive as materials such as sand and gravel would have<br /><br />
to be transported from a distance outside the mining area. Also, the extent of land disturbance would<br /><br />
expect to be greater.<br /><br />
<br /><br />
Preferred alternative for the two options would be controlled by key factors including costs, time of<br /><br />
construction, and availability of equipments, weather conditions and the expected life of the mine<br /><br />
operations.<br /><br />
<br /><br />
Mine Reclamation and closure alternatives<br /><br />
Mine reclamation and closure activities at SMHL operations are implemented to establish a beneficial<br /><br />
post-mining land use, in the case of this project it will consist primarily of agricultural lands. The key<br /><br />
activities will include re-grading disturbed areas to provide topographic relief that blends with the<br /><br />
surrounding areas. Some of the structural measures that will be put in place will include diversions,<br /><br />
backfilling and the construction of grass waterways. On the Gbonge Hills for example, cut and fills are<br /><br />
part of the extraction and reclamation activities.<br /><br />
<br /><br />
This option is requires millions of dollars in earth moving activities, and it cannot be considered as the<br /><br />
best option for the open pits. Backfilling of these pits with tailings slurries would be another feasible<br /><br />
option considering the life of the existing tailings facilities at the plant site.<br /><br />

<br /><br />
Section Five – Environmental Baseline Conditions<br /><br />
This section presents the existing baseline conditions within the vicinity of the SMHL mine footprint<br /><br />
against which the predicted impacts of the projects are analysed. It is divided into two main sections:<br /><br />
Biophysical environment and socio-economic environment.<br /><br />
5.1 Biophysical Environment<br /><br />
5.1.1 Topography and Landforms<br /><br />
The topography of the project area is in the interior plains of the African continental mass approximately<br /><br />
80 to 100 km. wide. The elevation rises from the coast to the interior ranges between 50 and 300m<br /><br />
above sea level. Conspicuous topographic features include: a. residual hills, b. High and low river<br /><br />
Terraces, c .Lateritic Uplands, d. Undulating hills, e. Bottomlands and f. Inland Valley Swamps.<br /><br />
Residual Hills: The two residuals hills which are formed in a ridge like manner are the Mokanji and<br /><br />
Gbonge Hills. The Mokanji Hills which trends in a south-west direction has nearly flat summits with<br /><br />
elevations ranging from 45 to 100 meters. The colluvial foot-slopes occurring on the right hand of the<br /><br />
ridge were excessively mined out by former Sieromco. The Gbonge hills trends in a north-northwest<br /><br />
direction with outcrops on top of the ridge. The hills rise The hills rise above 152 meters above sea level<br /><br />
running from Senjehun on the Sewa river through Gbonge to Kangahun covering a distance of 64 km.<br /><br />
Terraces: These cover a significant portion of the mined out area especially between Mosenesie and<br /><br />
Gondama. They are generally related to the Taia River and its tributaries. They are relatively younger<br /><br />
than the uplands and become more pronounced as the river flows south.<br /><br />
Laterites: Laterites occur in various forms and are mostly hard, brown, porous with hydrated ferric<br /><br />
oxides and alumina and little silica. At higher elevations, they occur as crust of weathered rock masses<br /><br />
and at lower elevations they occur as duricrust.<br /><br />
Undulating Hills: Theses are toposequences that are nearly level with concave slopes near the<br /><br />
bottomlands. The bottomlands have shallow residual soils that develop from the weathering of bedrock<br /><br />
of the colluvial foot slopes.<br /><br />
Inland Valley Swamps: Inland valley swamps are found in the bottomlands and are characterized by<br /><br />
alluvial soils that are silty and poorly drained. They often tend to be narrow, long and flanked by<br /><br />
Environmental and Social Impact Assessment (ESIA) Final Report: Prepared by TEDAEnvironmental Consultant: November: 2012<br /><br />
<br /><br />
Page 53<br /><br />
<br /><br />
concave slopes on each side. Very often, there are small streams that are not deeply incised and dry up<br /><br />
in the dry season. The width of these swamps varies between 20 and 80 metres and the length of the<br /><br />
individual segments are usually between 3 and 5 km. Because of their low gradient and dense<br /><br />
vegetation, the swamp has a very low rate of discharge.<br /><br />
5.1.2 Climate and Weather<br /><br />
The climatic conditions like many areas in Sierra Leone are warm and hot, with marked rainy season and<br /><br />
dry season. The weather cycle is as follows: Harmattan, Dry but humid, Disturbance line squalls, Steady<br /><br />
rains, Disturbance line squalls, Dry but humid and Harmattan.<br /><br />
The harmattan period occurs from late December to late January. Usually after the middle of January,<br /><br />
the rainless and dry harmattan is replaced by a dry but humid period, which extends to the end of<br /><br />
March. This is caused by a retreat of the harmattan air masses to the north and penetration of humid<br /><br />
air from the Atlantic Ocean. The winds come mainly from the west. Often, heavy dew and fog occurs<br /><br />
during the night and early mornings.<br /><br />
From March to the end of June, showers become more frequent, usually accompanied by<br /><br />
thunderstorms. The storms travel east to west against the general wind direction. These disturbance<br /><br />
line storms are usually preceded by a squall of easterly wind; they increase in intensity and frequency to<br /><br />
the end of June. During this period, the weather is very changeable with high humidity. July, August,<br /><br />
and into September are periods of heavy and usually persistent rains. A pause of dry weather conditions<br /><br />
occurs in the rainy season during August with clear and humid weather.<br /><br />
Ninety percent of this rain falls during the rainy season from May to November, leaving the other five<br /><br />
months with no more than 250mm of rainfall. Rainfall data obtained from a nearby station<br /><br />
representative of the project area is presented in Table 8. The relative humidity ranges between 95 to<br /><br />
100 and the mean monthly temperature is almost constant varying from 25oC in August to 28oC in<br /><br />
March. The mean daily maximum temperature is at its lowest in August and highest in March. The<br /><br />
mean daily minimum is at its lowest in January and at its highest in May. A summary of the weather<br /><br />
conditions that are experienced in the project area is presented in Table 3.<br /><br />
<br /><br />

51.3 Drainage and Hydrology<br /><br />
The Jong/Taia River constitutes the major drainage system in the project area. The Jong River drains<br /><br />
from the Sula Mountains and Kangari Hills in the north central of Sierra Leone. The main tributaries<br /><br />
include: Tikote, Kapui, Kwiva, Kati, Kpeyei, and Kwasi. They all flow in a southerly direction in a series of<br /><br />
zig-zag and meanders that reflect the lines of structural weaknesses. In nearly all cases, the drainage<br /><br />
Environmental and Social Impact Assessment (ESIA) Final Report: Prepared by TEDAEnvironmental Consultant: November: 2012<br /><br />
<br /><br />
Page 55<br /><br />
<br /><br />
basins do not follow the Crestline of the two ridges but are imprinted on lower ground on the inland<br /><br />
side of the ridges. Some of these basins have developed into inland valley swamps in their head and<br /><br />
middle waters.<br /><br />

From Mano Dasse to Mattru, the river flows southerly in a series of meanders crossing the MokanjiGbonge ridge in a steep sided valley. At Wubange, it meanders south and it is joined by the Tikote river<br /><br />
draining east from Gbangbama Hills; then by the Kwiva, which drains southwest from a flatland area<br /><br />
around Serabu and crosses the Gbonge ridge in a deep valley at Badui.<br /><br />
The Kapui rises in a dissected plain northeast of the Mokanji Hills. It crosses the hills in a deep straight<br /><br />
valley from Taninahun to Monghere, to receive the Kati, Kpeye and Kwesi tributaries in a wide flatland<br /><br />
Environmental and Social Impact Assessment (ESIA) Final Report: Prepared by TEDAEnvironmental Consultant: November: 2012<br /><br />
<br /><br />
Page 56<br /><br />
<br /><br />
and finally crosses at Yebeima in a series of incised meanders which continue westward to the Gbonge<br /><br />
confluence. The Yebei and Gbendeye rise from a flat ground close to the head of the Kwesi from where<br /><br />
they flow southwesterly across the strike ridge line at Gbangbatoke and Gbanbama respectively.<br /><br />
Some of these drainage patterns have developed into numerous inland valley swamps in their head and<br /><br />
middle waters. The swamps tend to be long and narrow and are generally flanked by convex slopes.<br /><br />
Actual widths of these swamps vary considerably, but the average is about 60m wide while the lengths<br /><br />
of individual segments are usually between 3 and 5km. Many of the swamps dry up entirely in the dry<br /><br />
season and those with low gradients become flooded in the wet season with a low rate of discharge.<br /><br />
The Taia river discharge has a mean flow rate of 668 m3sec in the rainy season with maximum rates<br /><br />
between 1000-1500 m3sec in August and September. During the dry season, the mean discharge falls to<br /><br />
89 m3sec but because of occasional storms there is generally an appreciable amount of water flow.<br /><br />
Runoff is moderate with a mean of 89mm and 15mm in the wet and dry season respectively (Jayakaran,<br /><br />
1987). Like many other areas in the country, there is an annual excess of precipitation over<br /><br />
Evapotranspiration demand. This lends itself to an analysis of a simple water budget accounting that will<br /><br />
provide an opportunity to monitor the water status in the project area throughout the year. Further, it<br /><br />
will provide numerical estimates of the amount of water surplus or water deficit and the periods of their<br /><br />
duration that will be used to monitor changes in the groundwater status area.<br /><br />
The hydrology follows the seasonal rainfall pattern as the flow of the rivers is influenced by the inflow<br /><br />
from their catchments. Annual peak flow, which is about 700m3/sec usually, occurs in August and<br /><br />
September. The flow starts to decrease in October when the rainfall is less and decreases to as low as<br /><br />
about 5m3/sec in April (ESCG, 1988).<br /><br />
During the rainy season there is an excess of precipitation over the reference Evapotranspiration that<br /><br />
cannot be stored as soil water and therefore roughly represents that amount of rainfall input that<br /><br />
becomes available for groundwater storage and runoff. The difference between annual precipitation<br /><br />
and water lost in deep percolation and or surface runoff (EP) is also of interest, since it represents the<br /><br />
actual use of water under local conditions imposed by the environment.<br /><br />
5.1.4 Geology<br /><br />
The geological formations (Precambrian age) found in the SMHL lease have a general NNW –SSE strike<br /><br />
and dipping in a West-South-West direction. Bauxite mineralization in the SMHL exploration license area<br /><br />
Environmental and Social Impact Assessment (ESIA) Final Report: Prepared by TEDAEnvironmental Consultant: November: 2012<br /><br />
<br /><br />
Page 57<br /><br />
<br /><br />
is a result of tropical weathering of alumina-rich facies rocks of the Kasila belt, which formed the<br /><br />
Mokanji, Gondama and Gbonge deposits. The bauxite in the project area is derived mainly from<br /><br />
anorthosites (Mokanji type with 53-56% Al203), leuco-gabbro (Gondama standard type with 52-54%<br /><br />
Al203), norite (Gondama 47-49% Al203) and hornblende/hypersthene gneisses (Mokanji West type with<br /><br />
45-48% Al203). These deposits do not form a continous ore body but rather, a chain of deposits with<br /><br />
topographically controlled breaks in echelon to one another (Mackenzie, 1961).<br /><br />
The petrology of the hypersthenes gneisses indicates a typical texture of the fresh rock and bauxite<br /><br />
laterites. Pale hard gibbsite material represents the plagioclase matrix, while red brown spongy spots<br /><br />
rich in limonite represents the hypersthenes (ibid). The bauxite is fine-grained, generally friable and<br /><br />
shows very little resistance to shock. The Gbonge anorthosite splits into two bands, probably becoming<br /><br />
discontinuous, irregular with highly deformed lenses. The anorthosite comprises alternances of layers<br /><br />
with almost pure plagioclase and layers rich in hypersthene. Quartzite with or without garnet and/or<br /><br />
magnetite occurrences are visible on both sides of the anorthosite belts.<br /><br />
Gabbros and leuco-gabbro occur mainly in the Gondama and Gbonge areas as massive and largely<br /><br />
undeveloped bodies with several elongated bands. The bands are highly deformed and have foliations.<br /><br />
Norites are more abundant than originally reported and bauxite derived from this type of rock is<br /><br />
massive, with low alumina and low silica. A long band of gabbros, leuco-gabbro and norite is also visible<br /><br />
in the western part of the project area, (Wunde IVA, IVB).<br /><br />
Texturally, the Mokanji depositse is fine-grained, generally friable and shows very little resistance to<br /><br />
shock. The Gondama deposits made up of anorthosite splits into two bands, probably discontinuous,<br /><br />
forming irregular and highly deformed lenses. The anorthosite comprises alternating layers of pure<br /><br />
plagioclase and hypersthene. Quartzite with or without garnet and/or magnetite occurrences are visible<br /><br />
on both sides of the anorthosite belts.<br /><br />
Gabbros and leuco-gabbro occur mainly in the Gondama and Gbonge areas as massive and largely<br /><br />
undeveloped bodies with several elongated bands. The bands are highly deformed and have foliations.<br /><br />
Norites are more abundant than originally reported and bauxite derived from this type of rock is<br /><br />
massive, with low alumina and low silica.<br /><br />
A long band of gabbros, leuco-gabbro and norite is visible in the Western part of the project area<br /><br />
(Wunde IVA, IVB).<br /><br />
Environmental and Social Impact Assessment (ESIA) Final Report: Prepared by TEDAEnvironmental Consultant: November: 2012<br /><br />
<br /><br />
Page 58<br /><br />
<br /><br />
Gneiss bodies with hypersthene and hornblende generally surround the gabbros and anorthosite.<br /><br />
Structurally, the project area has a relatively simple tectonics generally comprising two fault systems<br /><br />
that are visible in southern portion around Konta and Gbonge. The first fault system has a NNW – SSE<br /><br />
orientation, delineating in the South the leuco-gabbro and norite formations (rich in bauxite) from<br /><br />
Gbonge Hills. The second fault system has WNW – ESE, developed between the Gbonge bauxite deposits<br /><br />
and Konta bauxite deposits. Generally, the bauxite deposits tend to have an iron-rich cap and pass down<br /><br />
to silicious bauxite.<br /><br />
5.1.5 Surface and Groundwater<br /><br />
Detailed surface and groundwater studies previously prepared and those in past ESHIA reports provide<br /><br />
the baseline information that facilitated the preparation of this impact assessment report. Tables 10 and<br /><br />
Table 11 present a summary of the analytical results obtained for the two periods.<br /><br />
Results for the 2003 study indicate that pH for the selected sampling sites ranged from 4.6 to 6.4. These<br /><br />
values are well below the WHO permissible limits. Electrical Conductivity results are within the threshold<br /><br />
limit recommended by WHO for potable water. Faecal Coliform bacteria were found in 60percent of the<br /><br />
samples while non-faecal coliforms were present in all the water samples. As such, drinking water from<br /><br />
the contaminated sample points was a health risk. Nine out of the 15 samples had dissolved iron far in<br /><br />
excess of WHO limits while manganese ions were below the 0.1mg/1 concentration. Concentrations of<br /><br />
nitrate/nitrogen in all samples were also below the threshold limit and all other chemical ions were<br /><br />
either zero or very minimal and within the permissible limits recommended by WHO.<br /><br />
The 2012 study indicate that Temperature of all the water samples measured was found to range from<br /><br />

practice of waste re-use through the use of the dams should be encouraged.<br /><br />
5.1.6 Soils and Land Use Capability<br /><br />
The soils that occur in the project area are mainly derived from the metamorphic rocks of the Kasila<br /><br />
Group and on a minor scale from the igneous rock of the interior plains. The controlling soil forming<br /><br />
processes include:<br /><br />
 Weathering of primary minerals to form toxic horizons.<br /><br />
 Leaching of soluble substances and the subsequent formation of secondary minerals.<br /><br />
 Plinthization and segregation of iron and subsequent formation of hardened plinthite, duricrust<br /><br />
and laterite.<br /><br />
 Accumulation of surface soils to form epipedons (Odell et al, 1974).<br /><br />
As a result of the high rainfall and temperature, the silicate structures are broken with the resultant<br /><br />
release of silica, aluminum, iron and bases. Hardened laterites, plinthites and duricrust form mostly on<br /><br />
the uplands, though plinthite is often present in the poorly drained hydromorphic soils of the<br /><br />
bottomlands.<br /><br />
Environmental and Social Impact Assessment (ESIA) Final Report: Prepared by TEDAEnvironmental Consultant: November: 2012<br /><br />
<br /><br />
Page 60<br /><br />
<br /><br />
On the colluvial foot-slopes are soils that are generally shallow to moderately deep, very gravelly loams<br /><br />
to clays. On the hill-crests are residual soils that are well drained. Further down slope, the soils become<br /><br />
moderately shallow to moderately deep, gravelly and well drained.<br /><br />
Land capability evaluation was undertaken for this assessment to enhance the matching of crop<br /><br />
requirements in the reclamation processes using both the physical characteristics of the land and<br /><br />
climatic factors. Representative of typical soils of the land facets were evaluated for their agricultural<br /><br />
capability to produce various crops, using the following land attributes or qualities: Rooting space(r),<br /><br />
Soil fertility (f), Availability of moisture (n), Availability of oxygen (o), and resistance to erosion (e). The<br /><br />
FAO survey and land evaluation used in this report define 4 major landforms or facets within the lease<br /><br />
area which were grouped under land system 21 here in known as the Makondu land system<br /><br />
(UNDP/FAO, 1979).<br /><br />
Land quality was interpreted from the measurement of land factors taken in the field as follows:<br /><br />
 Measurement of effective soil depth (d) as a land factor defined rooting space (r) as a land<br /><br />
quality.<br /><br />
 Measurement of nutrient status (n) , salinity ( c), and soil reaction ( a) defined Soil Fertility (f), as<br /><br />
a land quality.<br /><br />
 Measurement of available water capacity ( x) and Climate type (z) both defined Availability of<br /><br />
moisture as a land quality as a land quality<br /><br />
 Measurement of drainage (w), flooding (fl) and permeability ( p) as land factors defined<br /><br />
availability of oxygen( o) as a land quality<br /><br />
 Measurement of slope steepness (s) and surface stones and rocks (y) both defined resistance to<br /><br />
erosion (e) as a land quality.<br /><br />
Eight crops were selected for the pre-mining evaluation scenario based on the potential for Agricultural<br /><br />
Land use. Table 6 shows the number of crops for which land suitability was systematically evaluated for<br /><br />
all the 4 land facets in the study area:<br /><br />
The suitability classes defined within the FAO land system are as follows:<br /><br />
S1: High to Moderate: land with no or moderate to slight limitations for the sustained cultivation of a<br /><br />
given crop. Relatively high yield and a good return on investment can be expected.<br /><br />
<br /><br />
Environmental and Social Impact Assessment (ESIA) Final Report: Prepared by TEDAEnvironmental Consultant: November: 2012<br /><br />
<br /><br />
Page 61<br /><br />
<br /><br />
S2:<br /><br />
<br /><br />
low: Land with moderately to severe limitations for the sustained cultivation of a given crop.<br /><br />
<br /><br />
Adequate yields and a moderate return on investment can be expected.<br /><br />
S3: Very low: land with very severe limitations for the sustained cultivation of a given crop. Very low<br /><br />
yields and only a marginal return on investment can be expected.<br /><br />
N: Nil, i.e. land having limitations which appears as severe as to preclude any possibility of successful<br /><br />
sustained use of a given crop.<br /><br />
(Subscripts such as e, r, f, and m indicate the soil limitations that should be addressed by proper<br /><br />
management for the sustained economic production of a crop at suitability classes of S2 and S3).<br /><br />
<br /><br />
Location of Sampling Points and Rationale for Selection.<br /><br />
In order to evaluate land capability in the mining lease area, 3 auger borings were made in swamps, and<br /><br />
2 pits were dug at locations indicated on map 3. The rationale for choosing this sampling point was to<br /><br />
assess the effectiveness of the reclamation process on land quality.<br /><br />
Site 2: The top –soil or overburden pile at the pile site of the on-going excavation at Gbonge hills. The<br /><br />
rationale for choosing this sampling point was to evaluate the process of top soil management and its<br /><br />
effects on soil fertility while piled up and waiting re-use for reclamation.<br /><br />
Site 3: An undisturbed coffee plantation with steep slope (24% slopes) in Jojohun. The rationale for<br /><br />
choosing this sampling point was to provide a reference soil for the steep upland soils of facet 3 by<br /><br />
which reclaimed land that were once on similar slopes before mining could be compared in terms of<br /><br />
their agricultural capability.<br /><br />
Site 4: Swamps in close proximity to the mine sites at Gbonge. The rationale for choosing this<br /><br />
sampling point was to assess whether the process of sedimentation ( predicted in the 2003 ESHIA<br /><br />
report for the resumption of mining by Vimetco by the Aqua Terra Consulting Firm) by runoff water<br /><br />
flowing downhill from ore excavation sites is active ( during the rainy season) and affecting soil fertility<br /><br />
and crop production in any way.<br /><br />

 Erosion hazard: presence and size of gullies, exposed crop/plant roots, slope percent,<br /><br />
and stoniness and Percent land cover.<br /><br />
 Thickness of the true soil: (A, E and B horizons) in auger borings, gully or soil.<br /><br />

Analysis of the results: Results for the 5 sampling points indicated the following:<br /><br />
Land unaffected by mining activities in land facets 3 and 4 (see soil profile descriptions below), continue<br /><br />
to exhibit similar land capability rating for agricultural use as determined prior to mining. This was<br /><br />
evident in the morphology of the soils on the upland and low lands: The soils unaffected by mining were<br /><br />
dark in colour (10YR 2/1, black, moist), thick in their A or surface horizons and had high density of roots<br /><br />
from both crops and forest species. There was also 100% vegetative cover and absence of gullies on the<br /><br />
uplands and sedimentation in lowland at the foot of its slope. Crop performance (e.g. Coffee on the<br /><br />
upland and paddy rice in the swamps) was also very high. The current land uses on these soils appear to<br /><br />
support productive, sustainable and environmentally sound.<br /><br />

1. The top–soil overburden was lighter in colour (see soil colour below: value and chroma of 4/5) ,<br /><br />
with little to zero land cover and absence of structural aggregates compared to the reference<br /><br />
soils in 1 above. The process of overburden management as reported by the mining and<br /><br />
environmental staff of Vimetco seemed to promote soil nutrient loss through volatilization,<br /><br />
leaching , sheet or rill erosion, little or no addition of organic matter through composting ,<br /><br />
mulching or the planting and incorporation of leguminous cover crops. Since the reclamation<br /><br />
process is delayed until after ore excavation has been completed for a mining site, the<br /><br />
overburden is exposed to destructive climatic agents for as long as the excavation period lasted<br /><br />
which, in some cases went on for over 3-4 years. The team of consultants in Aqua Terra<br /><br />
consulting (2003), indicated in their ESIA report that there was need to “stabilize the soil<br /><br />
material” in order to prevent “severe erosion and landslide”. Evidence of low quality top soil<br /><br />
may be seen in the low tree crop cover and deep gullies that characterize almost all reclaimed<br /><br />
sites. Soil fertility (f) as a land capability quality for tree crops and annual crops is further<br /><br />
degraded by these processes reducing the agricultural capability of the reclaimed lands to as low<br /><br />
as S3 or N.<br /><br />
<br /><br />
Environmental and Social Impact Assessment (ESIA) Final Report: Prepared by TEDAEnvironmental Consultant: November: 2012<br /><br />
<br /><br />
Page 66<br /><br />
<br /><br />
Non -use of cleared vegetation in composting- tree trunks,<br /><br />
twigs, branches and leaves are buried or abandoned<br /><br />
<br /><br />
A sharp deterioration in top soil colour of the original soil from a<br /><br />
dark organic Value and chroma of 2/1 to a light value and<br /><br />
chroma of 4/5 of exposed heaps of overburden on the Gbonge<br /><br />
hills piled some 40 meters from the ore excavation zone.<br /><br />
<br /><br />
2. Soils in swamps at the foot slopes (Land facet 4) of active mining hills are at a high risk of being<br /><br />
buried by sediments from runoff during the rainy season. In their ESIA report on the impact of<br /><br />
mining operations on Topography and Soils in the Vimetco concession area, the team of<br /><br />
consultants in Aqua Terra consulting (2003) explained how “erosion and sedimentation “can<br /><br />
release “large quantities of sediments” with “surface sludge moving down slope”. Nine years<br /><br />
following this prediction, heavy sedimentation was evident on swamp soils proximal to the<br /><br />
Gbonge mining site. This was regarded an environmental flashpoint that should not be allowed<br /><br />
to expand into community swamps as it has the potential to reduce the agricultural capability of<br /><br />
the soils form S1 for paddy rice to as low as S3 or even N due to low fertility (f). Fertility decline<br /><br />
can be caused by the burial or mixing of the organically rich and dark A or surface horizons with<br /><br />
low nutrient inert materials from exposed soil parent materials or “not-soil” from the mining<br /><br />
hills.<br /><br />
The following summarizes the evaluation of soils on the land facets assessed within the mining area:<br /><br />
 Land that was unaffected by the mining operations of Vimetco maintained their agricultural<br /><br />
capability class in which they were originally classified.<br /><br />
 Poor topsoil management appears to have reduced the agricultural capability of the reclaimed<br /><br />
lands to as low as S3 or N, making tree crop establishment largely unsuccessful.<br /><br />
 Sedimentation in some parts of the lowland appears to reduce the agricultural capability of the<br /><br />
soils form S1 for paddy rice to as low as S3 or even N. This negative impact should be addressed<br /><br />
through appropriated cost effective mitigates.<br /><br />
Based on pre mining crop suitability, the following conclusions are made:<br /><br />
Environmental and Social Impact Assessment (ESIA) Final Report: Prepared by TEDAEnvironmental Consultant: November: 2012<br /><br />
<br /><br />

<br /><br />
 That the colluvial soils of the plains were rated as having the highest crop suitability of S1 for<br /><br />
the cashew cash crop.<br /><br />
<br /><br />
<br /><br />
That the soils of the Foot slope and Swamps were rated as having the highest crop suitability of<br /><br />
S1 for paddy rice.<br /><br />
<br /><br />
 The Cashew crop was the most suitable /adaptable for growing on all upland facets compared to<br /><br />
other tree crops evaluated.<br /><br />
 Soils at the top of isolated hills were rated as unsuitable (N) for all the arable crops evaluated<br /><br />
and rated as very low (S3) for growing tree crops. Erosion was cited as the most limiting factor<br /><br />
to producing crops on the soils of this land facet.<br /><br />
 Soils on the steep side slopes show suitability ranging from S1 to S3 for all tree and arable crops,<br /><br />
except rice.<br /><br />
 Four soil/land limitations to agricultural productivity, e, r, f, and m denoting, erosional hazard,<br /><br />
low soil volume for adequate root space, soil fertility and moisture availability, were identified<br /><br />
for soils in the lease area. These are the soil characteristics that need to be maintained or<br /><br />
improved for the land capability rating of the soils to increase. They can therefore serve as<br /><br />
indicators for monitoring mining impact on soil/land agricultural productivity.<br /><br />
 The limitation of erosion attributed to the upland poses a potential risk to sedimentation in the<br /><br />
low lands.<br /><br />
The above baselines on land capability of soils within the Vimetco concession area may now be used<br /><br />
to compare evaluate and predict mining impacts on soil productivity.<br /><br />
