5.1.7 Terrestrial Biological Environment<br /><br />
Flora<br /><br />
Four main terrestrial biological units are found in the concession area: High moist Fragmented Forest,<br /><br />
Forest Re-growth, Upland Grasslands and swamps. The high moist fragmented forest is further divided<br /><br />
in to three units: the Moist Evergreen forest, Moist Semi- deciduous forests and Secondary or Forest<br /><br />
Regrowth.<br /><br />
Moist High Fragmented Forest: The moist evergreen is fragmented mostly found in clusters in the<br /><br />
lowlands, along streams and river courses. Around settlements are also found moist evergreen (rain)<br /><br />
forest interspersed with tree crops. But in the last few years, these have been drastically reduced as a<br /><br />
result of timber exploitation for war rehabilitation works. The original dominant tree species mainly<br /><br />
used as timber include: the mahogany Khaya anthotheca*,Entandrophragma utile*, E. angolensi*, E.<br /><br />
cylindricum’ Tetracarpidium conophorum, African cedar – Afzelia africana, African oak - Oldfieldia<br /><br />
africana*, Terminalia ivorensis, T. superba, Piptadeniastrum africanum, Daniellia thurifera,<br /><br />
Klainedoxa gabonensis, Brachystegia leonensis, Albizia ferruginea, Parkia biolor, Xylia evansii,<br /><br />
Ricinodendron heudelotii, Hannoa klaineana, Erythrophleum ivorense, Irvingia gabonensis*,<br /><br />
Mimusops heckelii, Cathormion altissimum, Amphimas pterocarpoides, Cynometra leonensis,<br /><br />
Tieghemella heckelii*, Heritiera utilis, Lophira procera, Sacoglottis gabonensis*, Triplochiton<br /><br />
Environmental and Social Impact Assessment (ESIA) Final Report: Prepared by TEDAEnvironmental Consultant: November: 2012<br /><br />
<br /><br />
Page 69<br /><br />
<br /><br />
scleroxylon* and Parinari excelsa. Non-timber tree species include: Musanga cecrotodes, carapa<br /><br />
procera and myranthus arboresus.<br /><br />
<br /><br />
Moist Semi-deciduous Forest: The moist semi-deciduous forest has similar characteristics to the<br /><br />
moist evergreen (rain) forest, but with a higher proportion of deciduous forest trees existing. They<br /><br />
are concentrated in the Gbonge Hills area south west of the concession area and relatively closer to<br /><br />
the coast. Before mining activities started, these forests found on hill tops were used mainly for<br /><br />
logging and farming activities. The original dominant tree species that existed here include:<br /><br />
Terminalia ivorensis, T. superb, Parkia bicolour, chlorophora regioa, Parinari excels,<br /><br />
Erythrophleum ivorense and many other evergreen species listed above.<br /><br />
Secondary forests are the dense forests found in clusters representing the last stages of succession into<br /><br />
mature forest after a long period of fallow. Their existence is due to their far away occurrences from<br /><br />
settlements and former sites of villages that are now of extinction. Common tree species found here<br /><br />
include: Musanga cecrotodes, carapa procera and myranthus arboresus. Typical timber species of<br /><br />
younger ages could be found including the Terminalia ivorensis, T. superb and Erythrophleum ivorense.<br /><br />
<br /><br />
Forest Regrowth (Farm Bush): Forest Re-growth is commonly known as farm bush that is derived from<br /><br />
either of the above vegetation as a result of bush fallow cultivation. These farm bushes are found<br /><br />
scattered throughout the area and are of varying ages ranging between five to twelve years. One to two<br /><br />
years of fallow represents the early stages of regrowth consisting of very low shrubs, grasses and<br /><br />
remnants of the previous root crops. Typical species include: Lantana Camara, Cissus afzelli,<br /><br />
Manniophytum fulvum, Abrus precatorius, Smilax krausina and bovini. As the fallows tend to develop<br /><br />
further, pioneer forest species succeed the farm bushes and thickets if that area is no longer used for<br /><br />
farming for a period of time. Typical species include: Dicrostachys glomerata, Harungana<br /><br />
madagascariesis, Nauclea latifola, Alchorinear cordifolia and Trema guieenses and Elaesis guineensis.<br /><br />
Older fallows often have common tree species such as Anisophyllea laurina and Alchornaa cordofolia.<br /><br />
<br /><br />
Environmental and Social Impact Assessment (ESIA) Final Report: Prepared by TEDAEnvironmental Consultant: November: 2012<br /><br />
<br /><br />
Page 70<br /><br />
<br /><br />
Upland Grasslands: Upland grasslands are found mainly in mined out areas that have not been<br /><br />
revegetated and farm bushes that have been excessively been farmed and colonized by grassy<br /><br />
vegetation due to poor and shallow soils and frequent burning in the dry season. Common grass<br /><br />
species include: Andropogon gabonensis, Chasmodium Caudatum and Hyparrhenia rufa.<br /><br />
Chasmodium caudatum is a valuable grazing grass that does not widely occur but gradually been<br /><br />
replaced by Imperata Cylindrica and Pennisetum species.<br /><br />
In the mined out areas are exotic and indigenous flora in the process of regeneration. Common among<br /><br />
these are the fast growing Acacia leptocarpa and Paspalum plicatulum that were once used for<br /><br />
rehabilitation by SIEROMCO Mines. The advantage of using these exotic trees is their ability to fix<br /><br />
nitrogen in their nodules and their fire tolerant properties.<br /><br />
Inland Valley Swamps and Other Aquatic Vegetation: These are aquatic types of vegetation that<br /><br />
occur in seasonally or perennially flooded valley bottoms. They are drained by sluggish fresh water<br /><br />
streams that drain into the main rivers. Typical aquatic species include the raphia palm characterized by<br /><br />
stilt roots and buttresses, mitragyna stipulosa and aquatic grasses and other water tolerant species<br /><br />
which creep as undergrowth.<br /><br />
<br /><br />
In addition to inland valley swamps are other wetlands encountered in the mining area. These are either<br /><br />
artificially constructed or partially destroyed as a result of mining activities. They are either ponds that<br /><br />
are in use or abandoned containing biodiversity with minor threats from damming and contamination.<br /><br />
Gallery forest: Gallery forest is found along the margins of the Taia River and other major streams,<br /><br />
consisting of a dense canopy of trees.<br /><br />
Gallery forest tends to be scanty along the main streams and minor rivers partly due to removal of<br /><br />
vegetation along the river margins to make way for farming activities.<br /><br />
5.1.8 Threats to Vegetation<br /><br />
Because of the current threat of deforestation on Sierra Leone’s biological resources, studies have been<br /><br />
carried out on over a thousand under-exploited economic plants (Cole & Macfoy, 1992; Turay; 1997;).<br /><br />
Five main categories of uses have been mentioned with over 40% of these as medicinal, 21% as food<br /><br />
and cash crops, 20% as timber trees and cottage industries and 15% as ornamentals used in horticulture.<br /><br />
This list has been compiled and reproduced in Annex Table A-2.<br /><br />
Environmental and Social Impact Assessment (ESIA) Final Report: Prepared by TEDAEnvironmental Consultant: November: 2012<br /><br />
<br /><br />
Page 71<br /><br />
<br /><br />
Fauna<br /><br />
The mine site is located in an area that has been previously surveyed and reported in other studies<br /><br />
(I.M.C. Mackay & Schnellman 1995; Knight Piesold Consulting 2003; Aqua Terra Consulting 2003). Many<br /><br />
of the large birds and mammals are not present in large numbers due to human induced impacts. No<br /><br />
detailed field studies were undertaken but reference was made to these previous studies with list of<br /><br />
fauna species used during field survey for confirmation by local residents on the past and current<br /><br />
presence of all species. This list was further used to determine the conservation status in terms of the<br /><br />
following IUCN status:<br /><br />
EX (Extinct): No reasonable doubt that the last individual has died.<br /><br />
EW (Extinct in the wild): Known only to survive in captivity or as naturalized populations well outside its<br /><br />
previous range.<br /><br />
CR (Critically Endangered): The species is eminent risk of extinction in the wild.<br /><br />
EN (Endangered): The species is facing an extremely high risk of extinction in the wild.<br /><br />
VU (Vulnerable): The species is facing an extremely high risk of extinction in the wild.<br /><br />
NT (Near Threatened): The species does not meet any of the criteria that would categorize it as risking<br /><br />
extinction but it is likely to do so in the future.<br /><br />
LC (Least Concern): There are no current identifiable risks to the species.<br /><br />
DD (Data Deficient): There is inadequate information to make assessment of the risks to this species.<br /><br />

the following instead of Near Threatened and Least Concern categories:<br /><br />

The taxonomy and naming of the individual species is based on those used in existing Wikipedia<br /><br />

<br /><br />
<br /><br />
<br /><br />
The Convention of International Trade in endangered Species of Wild Fauna and Flora (CITES).<br /><br />
<br /><br />
The following is a summary of the description of the fauna that exist in the concession area as<br /><br />
categorized above.<br /><br />
<br /><br />
Primates: A total of 9 primates are said to have existed and recorded in and around the mining area.<br /><br />
Four of these are listed as Endangered and Lower risk concern. They include:, Olive and Green monkey,<br /><br />
the western Baboon, Olive Baboon, the Gorrila, Chimpazee, Colobus monkey, Black and white monkey<br /><br />
and the Patas. The widespread of vegetation removal due to mining and farming makes it difficult for<br /><br />
most of these species to be visible. The widespread trade in bush meat and the hunting of animals to be<br /><br />
used as pets are also responsible for the gradual dwindling of these primates.<br /><br />
<br /><br />
Rodents: Rodents make up by far the largest order of mammals. Depending on the type of habitats, they<br /><br />
can live in large numbers, and are smaller in sizes though often weighing up to 25kg. The most popular<br /><br />
of these include: the ground squirrel, the flying squirrel, cane rats and mouse of different order and<br /><br />
genus. They are more widely distributed in young farm bushes and grassy vegetation. The scaly tailed<br /><br />
squirrel is found around old and abandoned settlements and very thick and moist shrubs.<br /><br />
<br /><br />
Environmental and Social Impact Assessment (ESIA) Final Report: Prepared by TEDAEnvironmental Consultant: November: 2012<br /><br />
<br /><br />
Page 73<br /><br />
<br /><br />
Ungulates: Ungulates are even-toed animals such as antelopes whose weight is borne about equally by<br /><br />
the third and fourth toes. They are of great economic value to humans all over the world. They are<br /><br />
preferred game species with very high prices in the mining towns and villages. Eight of these are<br /><br />
commonly found existing in the area. These include duikers of the common duiker, maxwell’s duiker,<br /><br />
yellow-backed duiker, bongo, royal duiker, black duiker and the bushbuck and Jetinks duiker. Their<br /><br />
status has been affected by the extensive alteration of the natural habitat combined with hunting and<br /><br />
trapping. Most of the mining area has been severely overhunted and the antelope population is critically<br /><br />
low.<br /><br />
Lagamorphs (Hares & Rabbits): The two families that exist include: hare/rabbits and Pikas that<br /><br />
resemble rodents but classed as super-families and thus classed under separate order. They have four in<br /><br />
scissors and an upper jaw instead of two. Common are the Lepus microtis and hares.<br /><br />
Hedge Hogs/Shrews and Moles: These are recognized by their spines though they look like rats. The<br /><br />
most common is the four-toed hedgehog. The African Pygmy hedge hog for example, is a nocturnal<br /><br />
animal that begins foraging from dusk to dawn. It has a lifespan of about two to three years in the wild.<br /><br />
Shrews are insectivorous mammals that seldom resemble mice and moles and are stout bodied<br /><br />
burrowers. Common among these are the tiny musk shrew and the mouse-tailed shrew.<br /><br />
<br /><br />
Amphibians/Bats/Reptiles: No threatened amphibians are recorded in the area. They are mostly found<br /><br />
to exist in the mine area in the narrow corridors of the Taia river and other main streams, open<br /><br />
woodlands and swamps. Common among these include: the Buffalo toad, banana frog, lime red frog,<br /><br />
mottled squeaker, long-tailed running frog and the river frog.<br /><br />
Bats are animals whose forelimbs are developed as wings making them the only mammals capable of<br /><br />
flying. Common among them are fruit bats, Light-winged Lesser House Bat, Gland-tailed Free-tailed Bat,<br /><br />
Little Free-tailed Bat, Sierra Leone Free-tailed Bat, and the Dwarf Free-tailed Bat. Some of these bats are<br /><br />
identified as a source of protein for the local residents.<br /><br />
Throughout the area are numerous reptiles ranging from lizards to snakes. The common agama (Gecko)<br /><br />
that is red headed is the most common lizard. It can be found climbing rocks and walls preying on<br /><br />
insects. The dwarf African crocodile is said to be common in the Taia river banks. It is very slow,<br /><br />
nocturnal and digs out burrows to hide and rest in the day. Very numerous snakes are found to exist in<br /><br />
the area. They range in size from small snakes like the red spitting cobra to the King cobra. Other<br /><br />
Environmental and Social Impact Assessment (ESIA) Final Report: Prepared by TEDAEnvironmental Consultant: November: 2012<br /><br />
<br /><br />
Page 74<br /><br />
<br /><br />
common snakes include the green and yellow mamba, the adder, viper, yellow bellied house snake and<br /><br />
the python. A list of these venomous snakes is produced in the Annex Table A.<br /><br />
Birds: Of the 626 birds recorded in Sierra Leone, 23 are said to be of global concern, 12 are globally<br /><br />
threatened. Moreover, 437 of this total are recorded to be residents in Sierra Leone and 137 are regular<br /><br />
visitors. Ten sites are established as important Bird Area (IBA) of which 3 are unprotected. There is a<br /><br />
high degree of endemism, but relatively few are threatened. Subsistence hunting of large birds and<br /><br />
game birds has led to a general decline in bird populations. The mining area does not fall within any of<br /><br />
the above categories. However there exist many bird species that are characteristic of the coastal and<br /><br />
interior plain area of south west of the country.<br /><br />
<br /><br />
5.2 Socio-economic Environment<br /><br />
5.2.1 Demography<br /><br />
The population of Moyamba District using the last 2004 census figure is 260,910. The total density is<br /><br />
estimated to be 60 persons per square km. This is almost the average figure for the mining population<br /><br />
that concentrates along the main roads in towns like Mokanji, Moriba Town, Mogwemo and<br /><br />
Gbangbatoke. The population is unevenly dispersed among the chiefdoms within the mining<br /><br />
community. As expected, there is a greater concentration of population in villages around mining sites<br /><br />
and chiefdom headquarters. The civil conflict ten years ago and its adverse impacts on all types of<br /><br />
economic activities have certainly caused the population to migrate from some chiefdom.<br /><br />
Another common feature of the population is its youthfulness with over 40% aged less than 15 years.<br /><br />
There is a relative shift in the age pattern of fertility with an earlier onset of reproduction, and an earlier<br /><br />
peak of fertility, and a high ratio of performance in the younger ages for the entire project area. The<br /><br />
reasons that seem to account for these include:<br /><br />
<br /><br />
<br /><br />
the relatively low status of women in the communities;<br /><br />
<br /><br />
<br /><br />
<br /><br />
the relatively younger population;<br /><br />
<br /><br />
<br /><br />
<br /><br />
earlier onset of child bearing, especially through teenage pregnancies;<br /><br />
<br /><br />
<br /><br />
<br /><br />
high dropout rates from schools which give younger women little alternatives than to start their<br /><br />
families; and<br /><br />
<br /><br />
<br /><br />
<br /><br />
relative absence of family planning methods.<br /><br />
<br /><br />
Environmental and Social Impact Assessment (ESIA) Final Report: Prepared by TEDAEnvironmental Consultant: November: 2012<br /><br />

Public Health is within the purview of the Peripheral Health Unit (PHU) of the Ministry of Health and<br /><br />
Sanitation. It is the delivery point for primary health care with three main categories. The Community<br /><br />
Health Centre (CHC) found in almost all chiefdom headquarters carries out health prevention measures,<br /><br />
cures, promotes health activities. It is also in charge of overseeing the other PHUs in the area. All of the<br /><br />
five chiefdoms have at least one community Health Centre. Community Health Posts perform similar<br /><br />
functions to CHCs but have fewer facilities and often refer patients to health centres. Maternal and Child<br /><br />
Health Posts (MCHP) are the first level of contact on the ground and are found in most villages in all<br /><br />
chiefdoms. All these health units refer difficult cases and emergencies to Serabu Hospital, which is the<br /><br />
general hospital with medical, surgical, laboratory, radiological and other diagnostic and treatment<br /><br />
services.<br /><br />
<br /><br />
Maternal health has remained poor. Although accurate statistics for the chiefdoms as a whole are not<br /><br />
available, it is estimated that the maternal mortality ratio high. Contributory factors are cultural<br /><br />
practices such as gender power imbalances, the low level of education among girls, and poor access to<br /><br />
skilled birth attendance.<br /><br />
<br /><br />
One recent innovation in the public health sector is the introduction of “Free Health Care Medical<br /><br />
Insurance” that was launched in 2010. It is a system of free health care for pregnant and breast feeding<br /><br />
<br /><br />
Environmental and Social Impact Assessment (ESIA) Final Report: Prepared by TEDAEnvironmental Consultant: November: 2012<br /><br />
<br /><br />
Page 76<br /><br />
<br /><br />
women and children under five funded by the United Nations Fund for Population Activities (UNFPA),<br /><br />
The World Bank and the Department for International Development (DFID).<br /><br />
<br /><br />
Malaria is the main reason why outpatients consult in local health centers. It is responsible for about 30<br /><br />
percent of all hospital attendances and is listed first in the top ten causes of mortality in all age groups<br /><br />
under the age of 16 years. People are however willing to use bed nets which can be treated with<br /><br />
permethrin to control mosquitoes.<br /><br />
<br /><br />
Children under five are predisposed to outbreaks of immunizable diseases such as measles, tetanus and<br /><br />
whooping cough since there is both low immunization coverage and poor mother-child health<br /><br />
monitoring in the area. The overall immunization coverage has however increased over the years. Poor<br /><br />
nutrition and inadequate growth monitoring may lead to malnutrition and delayed mental and physical<br /><br />
development among children under five.<br /><br />
<br /><br />
The reproductive health of adolescents and youth also continues to be a major health challenge.<br /><br />
Contributory factors include forced marriages, and poor access to information and youth-friendly<br /><br />
reproductive health services. High levels of sexual debut, teenage pregnancy, school drop-outs and<br /><br />
resultant low level of education are manifestations of this health challenge for the girl child. The<br /><br />
communities also have their own stock of indigenous knowledge about the treatment of a variety of<br /><br />
illnesses such as malaria, fever, general pains or sicknesses, post-natal sickness, poisoning, etc.<br /><br />
5.2.3 Water Supply<br /><br />
The main sources of drinking and domestic water are from shallow wells, streams, swamps and the<br /><br />
ponds that are constructed by the mining company. Even though some of these wells were constructed<br /><br />
by NGOs over the years, they are not regularly treated or monitored to ascertain the quality of water.<br /><br />
Without the provision of various water sources by SMHL, access to water for many in the community is<br /><br />
lacking. There are more much evidence of water pollution as a result of the bauxite mining and<br /><br />
processing operations. Sanitation facilities are limited especially in the smaller settlements where often<br /><br />
no pit latrines exist.<br /><br />
<br /><br />
Water sources in the mining community are from rivers, streams and wells with some fitted with hand<br /><br />
pumps. The Taia River and its tributaries serve as a permanent and valuable source of water which is<br /><br />
Environmental and Social Impact Assessment (ESIA) Final Report: Prepared by TEDAEnvironmental Consultant: November: 2012<br /><br />
<br /><br />
Page 77<br /><br />
<br /><br />
used mainly for mining, fishing, and for human consumption. Apart from those noted above, other<br /><br />
perennial surface-water sources available in the area are the reservoirs and ponds that have been<br /><br />
constructed for mining which were abandoned a long time ago by the then SIEROMCO Minerals. The<br /><br />
main water body that constitutes ground water occurrences is the Kasila series, the Taia river terraces<br /><br />
and the Inland Valley swamps. The Kasila is usually shallow in the wet season rising to about 0.5m below<br /><br />
the ground surface in some areas. Generally, ground water level decreases with seasonal fluctuation in<br /><br />
precipitation. In the dry season, ground water drops linearly with no recharge from rainfall.<br /><br />
<br /><br />
Ground water analyses have been previously undertaken in villages around project sites from existing<br /><br />
hand dug wells. The results indicate that ground water quality characteristics are controlled by<br /><br />
fluctuations in water levels in the systems (ESCG), 1990; IMC Mackay and Schnellman, 1997; Aqua Terra<br /><br />
Consulting, 2004). In the wet season, water from most of the wells is often clean with pleasant taste. In<br /><br />
the dry season on the other hand, some of the water in the wells become dry or coloured with<br /><br />
unpleasant taste. The laterites become highly soluble in the wet season making the water acidic. The<br /><br />
study by Aqua Terra Consulting indicates that pH for the selected water points ranged from 4.6 – 6.4<br /><br />
with a mean value of 5.6. These values are outside permissible limits of 6.5 – 8.5 as recommended by<br /><br />
World Health Organization (WHO). All the points had turbidity value of 5NTU, which is the maximum<br /><br />
threshold for clear water as recommended by WHO. Faecal coli form bacteria were discovered in 60 of<br /><br />
the samples. As such, drinking water from these points is a health risk.<br /><br />
<br /><br />
5.2.4 Education<br /><br />
The level of education in the mining community is relatively low. There are less than 20 Secondary<br /><br />
schools in the entire concession area, even though few primary schools exist. Of these, Lower Banta has<br /><br />
the largest number of all the schools, followed by Bumpe and Dasse respectively. Overall, the number of<br /><br />
students enrolled at both primary and secondary schools has drastically increased in the last three to<br /><br />
four years. This has led to the increase in the density of classes that is far beyond average. The increase<br /><br />
is affecting the efficiency and capacity of the educational system especially at the primary stage.<br /><br />
<br /><br />
The GOSL and a number of NGOs are providing educational services in terms of construction of<br /><br />
buildings, provision of teaching and learning materials. However, certain factors are standing the way of<br /><br />
improving the educational processes within the chiefdoms including: overcrowding classrooms; poor<br /><br />
Environmental and Social Impact Assessment (ESIA) Final Report: Prepared by TEDAEnvironmental Consultant: November: 2012<br /><br />
<br /><br />
Page 78<br /><br />
<br /><br />
quality of school buildings; poor spatial distribution of schools; inadequacy of school buildings; low<br /><br />
wages for teachers; high failure rates of school children in public exams; and a large number of overaged children.<br /><br />
5.2.5. Land and Water Resources<br /><br />
The right to land in the area is vested in the community. Ownership is inherited for specific lineages or<br /><br />
household with the eldest male administering the land. At community level, authority over land is<br /><br />
vested in the chiefs and the overall power over land in the entire chiefdom is vested in the paramount<br /><br />
Chief. Land acquisition for mining has a direct adverse impact on the livelihood especially when<br /><br />
agriculture is the main livelihood source for the community. Though land shortage is not a major<br /><br />
concern raised by the community, the issue of proper and adequate rehabilitation of mined out areas is<br /><br />
raised as a matter of concern. The communities argued that there would be adequate land for farming if<br /><br />
the mined out areas could be adequately rehabilitated with fertile top soil.<br /><br />
<br /><br />
Other constraints to land resource utilization in the farming communities include:<br /><br />
<br /><br />
<br /><br />
Low capital base and high costs of inputs;<br /><br />
<br /><br />
<br /><br />
<br /><br />
Lack of mechanization as a result of terrain configuration;<br /><br />
<br /><br />
<br /><br />
<br /><br />
Low level of management skills;<br /><br />
<br /><br />
<br /><br />
<br /><br />
Low level of incentives for farmers such as lack of cooperatives that can assist farmers in bulk<br /><br />
purchasing and access to attractive markets.<br /><br />
<br /><br />
.<br /><br />
5.2.6. Transport and Communications<br /><br />
Apart from the principal Mokanji – Gbangbatoke – Nitti Port road, which is regularly maintained by<br /><br />
SMHL, the road network other than the feeder haulage roads in the mining area is poorly maintained.<br /><br />
Feeder roads to most of the villages are hardly ever graded, although SMHL has also done considerable<br /><br />
work in maintaining the road to Gbonge from Gondama. Modes of transport are limited especially<br /><br />
during the wet season.<br /><br />
<br /><br />
5.2.7. Social and Political Organizations<br /><br />
There is a strong social and political influence within the communities ranging from strong community<br /><br />
spirit, special bonds, civic bonds, networks, extended friendships and trust to enable communities to<br /><br />
Environmental and Social Impact Assessment (ESIA) Final Report: Prepared by TEDAEnvironmental Consultant: November: 2012<br /><br />
<br /><br />
Page 79<br /><br />
<br /><br />
act together effectively to pursue common objectives. There is a low level of social ties that influences<br /><br />
community development especially in areas of decision making. The ability to mobilize resources is<br /><br />
generally low in all four chiefdoms, so also is their capacity for financial management and revenue<br /><br />
generation. Based on membership, the predominant social institutions are highly skewed towards men.<br /><br />
The members of a group interviewed were usually heads of households and therefore almost always<br /><br />
males; the number of women members remains negligible. The extent to which women and, to a lesser<br /><br />
degree, youth, can draw on this form of social capital is, therefore, limited. The wide variety of<br /><br />
community or village level institutions and organizations that may have impact on community<br /><br />
development programmes in the area can be divided into: Governmental, Indigenous and NonGovernmental Organizations.<br /><br />
<br /><br />
Government Institutions include: Associations established to promote specific development activities<br /><br />
under the supervision of extension officers, e.g. Project Management Committees (PMC); Village<br /><br />
Development Committees; Ward Development Committees (WDC); Local level Party Organizations set<br /><br />
up to organize elections or act as channels of government based on political patronage.<br /><br />
<br /><br />
Indigenous Organizations include: Traditional Authorities- chiefs and elders; Groups set up to organize<br /><br />
mutual support activities; Producers’ Unions; Women’s Associations; Work Groups; Social Clubs of<br /><br />
young men and women set up to achieve social activities; Church/Mosque Organizations with strong<br /><br />
political and social structures.<br /><br />
<br /><br />
Non-Governmental Organizations are the key actors in the community development process. They<br /><br />
operate in all the communities by providing them with schools, wells and pumps, clinics, micro-credits,<br /><br />
etc.<br /><br />
<br /><br />
Local Council is a recent innovation in public administration after its demise in the early 1970’s. As an<br /><br />
entity, they are bedeviled by the lack of adequate logistical support and capacity to effect the desired<br /><br />
change in the communities. Community’s awareness for development in the area id therefore limited.<br /><br />
5.2.8. Land Tenure<br /><br />
Land acquisition for mining has a direct adverse impact on the livelihood especially when agriculture is<br /><br />
the main livelihood source for the community. Though land shortage is not a major concern raised by<br /><br />
Environmental and Social Impact Assessment (ESIA) Final Report: Prepared by TEDAEnvironmental Consultant: November: 2012<br /><br />
<br /><br />
Page 80<br /><br />
<br /><br />
the community, the issue of proper and adequate rehabilitation of mined out areas is raised as a matter<br /><br />
of concern. The communities often argue that there would be adequate land for farming if the already<br /><br />
mined out areas could be adequately rehabilitated with fertile top soil. Community engagement with all<br /><br />
stakeholders will therefore constitute the cornerstone to establishing and maintaining long term<br /><br />
support for the implementation of the MRCP.<br /><br />
<br /><br />
5.2.9. Economic Activities<br /><br />
Cash cropping and subsistence farming are the two major systems of agriculture. The main cash crops<br /><br />
grown today are oil palm, coffee and whilst there has recently been extensive planting of cassava for<br /><br />
garri production. The main subsistence food crops grown are rice, cassava, sweet potatoes, maize,<br /><br />
beans, groundnuts, cocoyam, millet, sorghum, peas, sesame, and yams. A range of vegetables are grown<br /><br />
throughout the year including tomatoes, spring onions, pepper, eggplants and cucumber.<br /><br />
<br /><br />
Trees planted on reclaimed mined out lands are used for a wide range of reasons including: a source of<br /><br />
fuel and building materials and, to improve soil moisture and fertility. The main fruit trees are jackfruit,<br /><br />
avocado, mango, oranges and pawpaw.<br /><br />
<br /><br />
While socio-economic and cultural changes are impacting upon the community development with<br /><br />
evidence of a growing appreciation of gender concerns, the differences in gender roles and rights<br /><br />
remain intact and distinct in all the chiefdoms. The farming production economy, which is prevalent in<br /><br />
most parts of the chiefdoms, typifies this - a family enterprise with clearly defined gender roles with<br /><br />
men as the key decision-makers, except for women-headed households who often get engaged in petty<br /><br />
trading. Men control about 70% of the family expenditure with women controlling about a 25% and<br /><br />
other members of the family, mainly sons, control the rest. Women still control only a limited range of<br /><br />
activities and have restricted access to decision-making in the community. They lack decision-making<br /><br />
power on land tenure issues and rarely own productive resources such as land or tree crops. They have<br /><br />
limited sources of income compounded by the inability to access markets for their products. Other<br /><br />
challenges faced by women include constrained access to information and a paucity of resources to<br /><br />
address issues such as sanitation in the homestead.<br /><br />
<br /><br />
Environmental and Social Impact Assessment (ESIA) Final Report: Prepared by TEDAEnvironmental Consultant: November: 2012<br /><br />
<br /><br />
Page 81<br /><br />
<br /><br />
Besides people working in the mines, about 90% of the households in the community are farmers, while<br /><br />
many other people undertake other occupations such as petty trading, fishing and bike taxi driving.<br /><br />
Based on the 2010 survey, the average sustainable land holding in the chiefdoms is 0.90 ha per<br /><br />
household with an annual agricultural income of about $350. Those engaged in fishing have a higher<br /><br />
income of about $1250 per year. The important categories of expenditure are education,<br /><br />
food/household essentials, health care, farming, taxes, transport, credit and home building. The costs<br /><br />
that are considered to impose hardships on a family are,- education, acquiring a piece of land for<br /><br />
housing, and health services. People are able to save during the productive seasons of November to<br /><br />
February. However, savings are inadequate to address needs during the wet season months of June, to<br /><br />
September when incomes are low and expenditures high.<br /><br />

There are some variations in the ways the population derives its income. The larger settlements of<br /><br />
Gbangbatoke, Mokanji, Gbonge and Mosenesie and Walihun, carry higher populations derive their<br /><br />
incomes from either government or NGOs due to the level of education attained by these people.<br /><br />
Employment with the two mining companies operating in the area offers another source of income for<br /><br />
some of the local community although this is limited by the low education and skills level available.<br /><br />
Company employment policy has a preference for locals in the recruitment of unskilled jobs.<br /><br />

The community is further constrained in exploiting more fully its financial capital as it lacks access to<br /><br />
credit facilities. Financial services, such as banking and savings services, are under-developed or<br /><br />
unavailable. For instance, there are no micro-credit lending institutions to facilitate the expansion of<br /><br />
micro-enterprise development.<br /><br />
5.2.10. SURFACE LAND OWNERSHIP<br /><br />
Surface land ownership in the mining lease area and other parts of the provinces is vested in indigenous<br /><br />
families. Landowners generally use surface lands for permanent occupation in the form of physical<br /><br />
structures, tree plantations and temporary occupation in the form of shifting cultivation and cattle<br /><br />
grazing.<br /><br />
The agreement with the GOSL through the Mining Agreement, establishes SMHL’s right to occupy and<br /><br />
utilize permanently and temporarily within its mining lease such parts of the surface land, whether<br /><br />
government owned or otherwise, as may be reasonably required for accessory works and installations<br /><br />
that are necessary or useful for its operations, and such part of the surface as may be required for its<br /><br />
prospecting and mining operations.<br /><br />
SMHL pays annually, in advance, a surface rent under the mining lease for all land occupied or used.<br /><br />
This amount will be distributed among the Paramount Chiefs, Landowners and Chiefdom<br /><br />
Administration.<br /><br />
Through the Ministry of Agriculture, with its listing of fees for economic and subsistence crops, SMHL<br /><br />
makes satisfactory arrangements for payment of a fair and reasonable compensation for damage to any<br /><br />
crops, trees, farms, Permanent and temporary structures or works that occur as a result of prospecting<br /><br />
and or mining operations.<br /><br />
<br /><br />
Environmental and Social Impact Assessment (ESIA) Final Report: Prepared by TEDAEnvironmental Consultant: November: 2012<br /><br />
<br /><br />
Page 83<br /><br />
<br /><br />
Section SIX – Potential Impacts and Mitigation Measures<br /><br />
This section discusses the existing and potential environmental and social impacts that are associated<br /><br />
with SMHL operations and the mitigation measures required to minimize these impacts to acceptable<br /><br />
levels. The section only deals with the existing project operations and not past activities and impacts<br /><br />
that were conducted by previous studies or future mining operations other than the current deposits<br /><br />
that are being mined.<br /><br />
6.1. Biophysical Environment<br /><br />
6.1.1 Topography<br /><br />
Mining activities at SMHL operational areas involve the removal of vegetation and the opening up of<br /><br />
mine panels. Vegetation is also removed in areas that are used for the stockpiling of overburden and<br /><br />
ore. Topsoil removal and subsequent stripping of the overburden is done with the use of bulldozers and<br /><br />
excavators with the resultant alteration of the topography and landforms. Mining progresses further<br /><br />
with backfilling of mine panels that are levelled to match the pre-existing landscape as much as possible.<br /><br />
Other concurrent activities involve haulage and other road rehabilitation works undertaken especially<br /><br />
during the wet season.<br /><br />
<br /><br />
A total of about 1300 acres of land area has been disturbed as a result of this activity and the impact is<br /><br />
expected to last forever though with less severity. Under the current mining methods, the significant<br /><br />
rating scale for this activity is moderate. The use of heavy equipment such as bulldozers, excavators and<br /><br />
trucks utilized during the mine’s operation is also resulting in soil compaction in the wet season often<br /><br />
beyond permeability which can lead to the creation of pools after periods of heavy rainfall.<br /><br />
<br /><br />
The major mitigation measure for the above impacts is the reclamation of mined out areas to a near<br /><br />
original habitat. The Mine Reclamation and Closure Plan (MRCP) that has been prepared for SMHL that<br /><br />
details the major activities involved in grading and levelling of the local topography that is currently<br /><br />
been disturbed. A summary of this plan is presented under a separate chapter of this report.<br /><br />
<br /><br />
The overburden materials and the ore stockpiles are often susceptible to erosion and can lead to<br /><br />
sedimentation of waterways. To mitigate this impact, detention storages should be constructed to<br /><br />
collect open pit water and runoff drainage to ensure that the receiving environment water quality is<br /><br />
achieved prior to discharge.<br /><br />
Environmental and Social Impact Assessment (ESIA) Final Report: Prepared by TEDAEnvironmental Consultant: November: 2012<br /><br />
<br /><br />
Page 84<br /><br />
<br /><br />
The use of heavy equipment such as bulldozers, excavators and trucks utilized during the mine’s<br /><br />
operation is also resulting in soil compaction in the wet season often beyond permeability which can<br /><br />
lead to the creation of pools after periods of heavy rainfall.<br /><br />
<br /><br />
To mitigate the impact of compaction in the wet season, designated routes should be utilized for the<br /><br />
movement of heavy vehicles and machinery and the transportation of ore and other materials from the<br /><br />
deposit sites. The implementation of these measures will eventually be not much of a concern for the<br /><br />
project.<br /><br />
6.1.2 Air Quality<br /><br />
The common operations that impact on air quality include land clearing, excavation, earth moving, and<br /><br />
storage of materials, handling and transportation. The mechanization includes the use of ripper<br /><br />
bulldozers, excavators, tippers/dumpers and graders. Other operations include the use of stockpile<br /><br />
materials, drying and loading of the bauxite at Nitti Port, operations at the mechanical workshop and<br /><br />
the burning of farm bushes during the dry season by local residents.<br /><br />
<br /><br />
Air emissions that result from these activities include carbon monoxide (CO), oxides of nitrogen (NOx),<br /><br />
sulfur SOx), Total Organic Compounds (TOC), and Carbon dioxide (CO2). The major source of air<br /><br />
emissions is the combustion of diesel fuel used by the mine equipment fleet, mobile mining equipment,<br /><br />
other transportation, process plant operations, and the production of electricity. The receptors include<br /><br />
machine operators and nearby personnel within the plant site. The impact of such emission on air<br /><br />
quality is however minimal and the impacts are insignificant in the long term.<br /><br />
<br /><br />
No assessment has been undertaken on the estimated rate of emission but it is expected that the<br /><br />
predicted concentration of these Green House Gases would be well below the criterion for all the<br /><br />
receptors and dust generation would only be severe in the dry season. Air quality assessment thus<br /><br />
constitutes a key environmental monitoring plan for SMHL operations. SMHL should ensure that a<br /><br />
periodic checking on regular emissions based on monitoring instrumentation is made and subsequent<br /><br />
reporting that describes conditions and corrective measures to avoid future impact on air quality.<br /><br />
<br /><br />
Airborne particulates become very high during the dry season but very low in the wet season when<br /><br />
emissions are only limited to vehicular operations from mining and ore beneficiation. The receptors<br /><br />
Environmental and Social Impact Assessment (ESIA) Final Report: Prepared by TEDAEnvironmental Consultant: November: 2012<br /><br />
<br /><br />
Page 85<br /><br />
<br /><br />
include project site boundary, nearby villages especially those settlements along major haul roads. The<br /><br />
impact is only high in the dry season and insignificant in the wet season.<br /><br />
<br /><br />
There is an increase in dust with increasing traffic especially on the haul roads between Gbonge,<br /><br />
Gondama and Nitti. This affects the local communities within the access road and this is expected to<br /><br />
continue throughout the life of the mining operations. The scale of the impact is however local and may<br /><br />
increase only in the dry season.<br /><br />
<br /><br />
Mitigation measures need to be implemented to reduce the level of dust. This will include: reduction in<br /><br />
speed limits. Construction of speed bumps through villages and stabilization of road materials in<br /><br />
sensitive spots along the haul roads. Dust suppression through watering should form a major activity all<br /><br />
throughout the dry season to mitigate the impact of dust on the affected receptors. Vehicular operators<br /><br />
are also advised to adhere to the safety rules especially those introduced during training programmes.<br /><br />
With these mitigation measures applied, there will be no short-term and long-term impacts.<br /><br />
6.1.3 Noise<br /><br />
Most of the noise at the mine site is generated from activities of mining equipment such as excavators,<br /><br />
bulldozers, frontend loaders, mine trucks and stationary equipment including the wash plant, power<br /><br />
plant, dryer building at Nitti Port and the mechanical workshop. Heavy truck movement along the haul<br /><br />
road is another source of noise.<br /><br />
<br /><br />
The plant site is located within a valley about 3 kilometres from the nearest village and separated from it<br /><br />
by farm bush. As such, it is unlikely that noise will impact on local residents. However, noise is having<br /><br />
effects on the workers in and around the plant site where most of the fixed noises are located. Noise is<br /><br />
having effects on wild life in the areas of mining activities; and since mining and ore processing go on for<br /><br />
two to three shifts a day. Highly significant noise levels will occur in the open pits and plant area all year<br /><br />
round.<br /><br />
<br /><br />
It was not possible to calculate daytime sound pressure levels for the various components. But SMHL<br /><br />
environmental report for the third quarter of this year estimates noise levels for the various locations<br /><br />
and the results are reproduced in Table 16.<br /><br />

two ways from mine panels to wash plant and to Nitti Port. This noise traffic will have a relatively minor<br /><br />
effect on the noise receptors along the main haul roads. These short-term noise impacts to residents will<br /><br />
be minimal but can be locally significant to employees in certain work areas at the plant site and mine<br /><br />
panels.<br /><br />
