6.1.4 Geology and Soils<br /><br />
The topsoil in the area is thin and residual on both the foot slopes and hill slopes where most mining<br /><br />
activities take place. The major impact of mining on the local geology is visible in the removal of<br /><br />
overburden that is used in construction works and the excavation of ore deposit from the mine panels.<br /><br />
Soil erosion results due to this activity and the impact is high during heavy rainfall events when most<br /><br />
soils are washed and deposited in streams and valleys. This impact is unavoidable even though the<br /><br />
physical disturbance is low in the short and long term. Whilst these impacts could be significant,<br /><br />
mitigation measures follow the same procedures outlined for the reclamation of the mined out areas.<br /><br />
<br /><br />
The topsoil is however recovered, stockpiled and eventually used in the reclamation works. The<br /><br />
suitability of these soils for crop productivity constitutes part of this ESIA as a mitigation measure. SMHL<br /><br />
will implement the rehabilitation programme for the soils by comparing the quality of the pre-disturbed<br /><br />
soil to that of the exposed soils in the mined out areas. This will set up the criteria for soil enhancement<br /><br />
for different crops.<br /><br />
<br /><br />
Major mitigation measures include:<br /><br />
<br /><br />
<br /><br />
Separation of super soil from sub soil during removal.<br /><br />
<br /><br />
<br /><br />
<br /><br />
Proper grading and reshaping of the disturbed areas.<br /><br />
<br /><br />
<br /><br />
<br /><br />
Adequate crop suitability procedures for reclamation work.<br /><br />
<br /><br />
Environmental and Social Impact Assessment (ESIA) Final Report: Prepared by TEDAEnvironmental Consultant: November: 2012<br /><br />
<br /><br />
Page 88<br /><br />
<br /><br />
<br /><br />
<br /><br />
Prompt timing of re-vegetation on reclaimed lands to avoid top soil removal in the early rain<br /><br />
events.<br /><br />
<br /><br />
<br /><br />
<br /><br />
Establishment of sediment raps, bunds and where necessary construction of diversion ditches.<br /><br />
<br /><br />
<br /><br />
<br /><br />
Regular inspection of reclamation and repair works where needed; and<br /><br />
<br /><br />
<br /><br />
<br /><br />
Use of rock mulch on unprotected surfaces.<br /><br />
<br /><br />
6.1.5 Surface Water<br /><br />
Mining activities at SMHL project sites adversely impact on surface water quality. These processes<br /><br />
include:<br /><br />
 Vegetation removal for mining leaving behind very large tracts of land exposed to direct rainfall<br /><br />
and soil erosion. The eroded materials are transported into waterways thereby increasing<br /><br />
surface water turbidity.<br /><br />
 Runoff from solid wastes in water bodies resulting in contamination and blockage.<br /><br />
 Spillage of fuel or oil which will contaminate nearby drainage ways.<br /><br />
 Runoff from waste disposal sites from the plant site and Camp.<br /><br />
<br /><br />
Impacts to surface water quality from these activities can have a locally high significance if no mitigation<br /><br />
measures are applied as a result of changes in water quality.<br /><br />
<br /><br />
Currently, streams and other water bodies are being monitored at sensitive locations to ensure that<br /><br />
mining activities do not affect the downstream supply systems. The washing Plant does not require any<br /><br />
chemical reagent in ore processing. The decant water is thus expected to meet the IFC effluent<br /><br />
discharge guidelines.<br /><br />
To mitigate the adverse impacts on surface water quality, the following measures are proposed:<br /><br />
 Stockpiles and overburden should not be placed at nearby drainage ways.<br /><br />
 Waste oil, fuel and other lubricants should be stored in strong containments.<br /><br />
 Tailings Management Plan should be properly implemented. As much as possible, leakages into<br /><br />
earth fill dams should be avoided.<br /><br />
Efforts should be made in the wet season to ensure that rain water is diverted from the TSF. This is<br /><br />
achieved by constructing ditches around the perimeter of the TSF and channelled to the nearby drainage<br /><br />
system.<br /><br />
Environmental and Social Impact Assessment (ESIA) Final Report: Prepared by TEDAEnvironmental Consultant: November: 2012<br /><br />
<br /><br />
Page 89<br /><br />
<br /><br />
6.1.6 Visual Resources<br /><br />
The former Sieromco mine pits and the current mined out areas around Gbonge deposits are the main<br /><br />
intermittent views around haul roads characterized by lower shrub vegetation and secondary forests.<br /><br />
Activities that are associated with visual impacts include former mine pits and scars, abandoned power<br /><br />
lines, former Sieromco plant sites around Mokanji, perimeter fences, traffic flow, dusts and<br /><br />
accumulation of these on leaves, speed bumps, burrow pits, tailings storage facilities and mine faces.<br /><br />
The major receptors include villages along main haul and access roads. These impacts are localized and<br /><br />
occur in the short term especially in the dry season. The following measures are proposed to minimize<br /><br />
visual intrusions that are outlined above.<br /><br />
 Where construction of power lines would be required, features should be designed so as to<br /><br />
achieve landscape designs.<br /><br />
 Burrow pits and mine faces should be graded so as to achieve professional landscape designs.<br /><br />
Impoundment dams should be rehabilitated to meet international standards.<br /><br />
6.1.7 Accidental Release of Sludge from Tailings Storage Facility<br /><br />
The facility was constructed with mechanisms that protect direct discharge of sediments into the earth<br /><br />
filled dams. Any failure is likely to have a discharge into these dams and local drainage systems<br /><br />
downstream of the facility. This could increase turbidity, conductivity and inorganic particulates in the<br /><br />
systems. Because of the relative immobility of the mud and the seasonality of weather conditions, the<br /><br />
severity of such an impact would be expected to be local and low with an overall impact rating of<br /><br />
moderate (high severity; low likelihood).<br /><br />
<br /><br />
Recommended mitigation measures to minimize the impact potential include rehabilitation,<br /><br />
development and implementation of the Tailings Management Plan coupled with regular inspection<br /><br />
programme.<br /><br />
6.1.8 Wildlife, Protected Areas and Cultural Properties<br /><br />
There are no significant wildlife habitats reported to exist in the concession area, no wildlife sanctuaries<br /><br />
or protected site habitats have been identified in the study corridor. Therefore, no mitigation measures<br /><br />
are recommended. No known protected cultural or historical sites were identified except those found<br /><br />
surrounding villages including local religious facilities, local monuments, holy trees/springs or old burial<br /><br />
grounds. These sites will not directly be affected by land expropriation or lie in the vicinity of the works.<br /><br />
<br /><br />
Environmental and Social Impact Assessment (ESIA) Final Report: Prepared by TEDAEnvironmental Consultant: November: 2012<br /><br />
<br /><br />
Page 90<br /><br />
<br /><br />
6.1.9 Borrow Pits and Quarry Sites<br /><br />
Borrow pits and quarries are opened within road corridors in order to supply sub-base material and<br /><br />
crushed stone. The principal environmental concerns relate to the visual effects of quarrying and the<br /><br />
effects of access road rehabilitation works. There is less concern regarding impacts on development in<br /><br />
agricultural lands or close to settlements, since there does not seem to be a high probability of such<br /><br />
sites are being developed. Nevertheless, these aspects need to be considered during future quarry site<br /><br />
development.<br /><br />
The principal concerns in relation to borrow pits include:<br /><br />
<br /><br />
<br /><br />
Access roads and borrow pits themselves may be developed in materials which are highly<br /><br />
erodible, and may contribute to enhanced erosion of a sensitive landscape;<br /><br />
<br /><br />
<br /><br />
<br /><br />
Deep pits may be developed which are a safety hazard to people especially if they fill with water<br /><br />
during the wet season and become breeding grounds for insect disease vectors;<br /><br />
<br /><br />
<br /><br />
<br /><br />
Since borrow pits will almost certainly be developed close to the road corridors, even if they are<br /><br />
not located in agricultural lands, access roads will probably have to traverse cultivated land, with<br /><br />
adverse effects on landholders if they are not properly reinstated when pit use ceases.<br /><br />
<br /><br />
<br /><br />
<br /><br />
Even though the landscape has only average visual quality borrow pits will probably be<br /><br />
developed in elevated parts of the landscape, and scarring by working faces can permanently<br /><br />
spoil an otherwise pleasant view.<br /><br />
<br /><br />
6.1.10 Flora and Fauna<br /><br />
The mining activities of SMHL operations include clearing of vegetation for mining and stockpiling and<br /><br />
the deposition of mine spoils in unspecified areas. All these activities are impacting on the flora and<br /><br />
fauna species even though some of the land areas were previously disturbed before SMHL operations.<br /><br />
The impact is low to moderate of ecological value such as the loss of habitat, disturbance of rare species<br /><br />
and wild life. With increase in population in the concession area, the demand for bush meat increases<br /><br />
with the purchasing power of the people working for SMHL. The modification of the habitat is expected<br /><br />
to last long, but with implementation of mitigation measures, short, medium and long term impacts will<br /><br />
be expected to be favourable.<br /><br />
<br /><br />
Environmental and Social Impact Assessment (ESIA) Final Report: Prepared by TEDAEnvironmental Consultant: November: 2012<br /><br />
<br /><br />
Page 91<br /><br />
<br /><br />
There is also an ensuing expansion of farming activities to meet the food demands for the mining<br /><br />
community. This type of farming that is practiced has negative impacts only in the short term and is<br /><br />
localized. The following mitigations are proposed:<br /><br />
 Prompt mine reclamation activities in the mined out areas should be enhanced.<br /><br />
 If vulnerable or endangered species exist within the mine sites, measures should be taken to<br /><br />
create alternative sites.<br /><br />
6.2. Socio-economic Impacts<br /><br />
This section analyses the negative socio-economic impacts of the project activities on the social,<br /><br />
economic and biophysical environment and describes the recommended mitigation measures to<br /><br />
minimize, or mitigate these negative impacts.<br /><br />
6.2.1 Community Health and Safety<br /><br />
The GoSL provides Free Primary Health Care to children under the age of five and their mothers. SMHL<br /><br />
employees and their families have access to the health clinic at the mine site and referrals are often<br /><br />
made to the Serabu Hospital located 11 km. from the Gondama site. The likelihood of accidents and<br /><br />
work-related illnesses on the job as well as accidents occurring between mining related equipment and<br /><br />
local vehicles are inherent at SMHL operations. Though located in a rural setting, the mining area is<br /><br />
about 10 km. away from the closest medical facility and the likelihood for accidents to occur is assumed<br /><br />
to be low.<br /><br />
<br /><br />
Only a few foreign workers are hired by SMHL most of these are highly educated mine engineers.<br /><br />
Therefore, the risk of spreading communicable diseases as a result of the project is considered minor<br /><br />
(medium severity; low likelihood). However, SMHL and its senior staff personnel should initiate<br /><br />
discussions of communicable diseases and sexually transmitted diseases into its health and safety plans.<br /><br />
The mitigation measures recommended to address this impact include the following:<br /><br />
• Provision of additional health facilities to the five affected chiefdoms;<br /><br />
• Provision of health services to all workers and their families, which will avoid new demands on health<br /><br />
services by Project out-of-area workers.<br /><br />
<br /><br />
Environmental and Social Impact Assessment (ESIA) Final Report: Prepared by TEDAEnvironmental Consultant: November: 2012<br /><br />
<br /><br />
Page 92<br /><br />
<br /><br />
• Avoid and minimize environmental effects that have potential to affect health, including dust, noise,<br /><br />
and any effects that may negatively affect livelihood resources, such as contamination of the rivers or<br /><br />
farmlands.<br /><br />
<br /><br />
<br /><br />
Preventions should be taken against fire accidents and security should be imposed at all times.<br /><br />
<br /><br />
6.2.2 Traffic and Road Safety<br /><br />
The expansion of SMHL operations to Gbonge has increased vehicular traffic over the last few years.<br /><br />
Heavy vehicular transportation between Gondama and Nitti Port is estimated at about 6 trips per trucks<br /><br />
per shift and between Gbonge and Gondama 10 trips per shift per truck. This increase is of major<br /><br />
significance especially when the haul roads are concurrently been used by both SMHL and other public<br /><br />
vehicles. In some cases these haul roads are used by commercial bike riders. Similarly, pedestrians along<br /><br />
the road alignment will be exposed to the greatest risk. The greatest at risk are the linear settlements<br /><br />
such as Gbonge, Konta, Gondama, Mosenesie, Foala and Wunde.<br /><br />
Drivers, bike riders and pedestrians are gradually becoming accustomed to the increased traffic and<br /><br />
vehicle speeds, and it is likely that the number of accidents will show any level of concern.<br /><br />
Most of the traffic is currently associated with the Project is between Gondama and Gbonge.This traffic<br /><br />
represents relatively minimal safety risk to other vehicles. But the traffic between Gondama to Nitti Port<br /><br />
poses a greater risk to local residents as it is being used by other public vehicles. As such, the increase in<br /><br />
traffic is rated a moderate impact (moderate severity; moderate likelihood).<br /><br />
<br /><br />
Mitigation measures include:<br /><br />
<br /><br />
<br /><br />
<br /><br />
Provide driver safety training to all workers, including contractors, who are expected to<br /><br />
drive as part of their duties, especially to be alert for pedestrians along the private haul<br /><br />
road;<br /><br />
<br /><br />
<br /><br />
<br /><br />
Impose speed limits;<br /><br />
<br /><br />
<br /><br />
<br /><br />
Schedule truck traffic along the public highways to avoid peak hours; and<br /><br />
<br /><br />
 Introduce safety campaigns amongst the communities.<br /><br />
Implementation of these measures would reduce the impact to minor scale. The unpaved road requires<br /><br />
excessive repairs and maintenance to support traffic flow in the wet season.<br /><br />
Environmental and Social Impact Assessment (ESIA) Final Report: Prepared by TEDAEnvironmental Consultant: November: 2012<br /><br />
<br /><br />
Page 93<br /><br />
<br /><br />
6.2.3 Population Movements<br /><br />
With the existing high level of unemployment in the country, any development of projects such mining<br /><br />
is expected to attract a huge number of people seeking for jobs. Since the restart of bauxite mining, the<br /><br />
project area has attracted a large number of people seeking for job and providing support services such<br /><br />
as petty trading and commercial sex work. The influx in the area is cumulative as there are other mining<br /><br />
operations going on in nearby communities. The increase in population is expected to increase with the<br /><br />
demand for goods and services. The negative impacts that are occurring include; prostitution, spread of<br /><br />
diseases such as HIV and AIDS, STDs, disruption of social and cultural values of host communities, drug<br /><br />
and alcohol abuse, theft, domestic violence and noise. These impacts are expected to last for a long time<br /><br />
on a wider local level, but the significance is moderate.<br /><br />
To minimize such impacts, SMHL is expected to give job preference to the local population and only<br /><br />
when the local population is exhausted that hiring should be made from outside the mining community.<br /><br />
There is also a need for an increase in the provision of goods and services by introducing job<br /><br />
opportunities such as poultry and piggery farming, vegetable gardening, bakery, brick making,<br /><br />
entertainment centres, etc.<br /><br />
6.2.4 Gender Impacts<br /><br />
SMHL mining operations like most industries are having a severe impact on gender. These include: a.<br /><br />
Impacts on land ownership, b. impacts on economic activities, c. teenage pregnancy and early marriage.<br /><br />
Land is mainly owned by the section chiefs who are mainly male. At family level, land is mainly owned by<br /><br />
the eldest male (in the case of inheritance) or by the father who is the head of the household. Surveys<br /><br />
that have been done in the area indicate that men have more access to land for farming activities than<br /><br />
women. This situation may likely have a negative impact on the productive potential of women in many<br /><br />
of the villages in the area. The major economic activities include small scale farming, fishing and petty<br /><br />
trading. Due to the increase in the transportation cost, many women sell their crops at farm gate prices<br /><br />
to traders, thus getting less from the crops they cultivate.<br /><br />
There are variations in the roles and responsibilities in relation to economic activities. Women are<br /><br />
mainly involved in market gardening and men largely involved in working in the mines, swamp and<br /><br />
upland rice cultivation.<br /><br />
<br /><br />
There is a high level of teenage pregnancy in the project area. They survey reported that girls generally<br /><br />
drop out of school at an early stage. Many of the young girls who drop out school as a result teenage<br /><br />
pregnancy eventually end up in the bigger settlements like Moriba Town, Mokanji and Gbangbatoke.<br /><br />
Environmental and Social Impact Assessment (ESIA) Final Report: Prepared by TEDAEnvironmental Consultant: November: 2012<br /><br />
<br /><br />
Page 94<br /><br />
<br /><br />
Social activities such as video shows and dances are responsible for the increasing rate of teenage<br /><br />
pregnancy.<br /><br />
<br /><br />
There are few secondary schools existing in the five chiefdoms. Failure to complete secondary school<br /><br />
education would mean an early marriage as there are no other socio-economic pursuits than trading.<br /><br />
Women in the community generally marry at an average age of 17 years and are liable to bear an<br /><br />
average of 8 children during the child bearing period.<br /><br />
<br /><br />
These impacts outlined above are expected to last a long time occurring at a local scale. The severe<br /><br />
impact would be low to moderate and irreversible in some cases. Mitigation measures to address these<br /><br />
impacts include:<br /><br />
 Through SMHL’s corporate social responsibility program, it can promote other sectors of the<br /><br />
economy through training, establishing micro credit schemes and through serving as a<br /><br />
market for the agricultural produce by women.<br /><br />
<br /><br />
 To prevent idleness among teenagers which lead to pregnancy, they should be provided with<br /><br />
family planning strategies and activities that promote social interaction and positive<br /><br />
community behaviour. Some of these social activities may include sports competitions with<br /><br />
the nearby schools and theatre.<br /><br />
<br /><br />
 Women, whose roles are mainly production and reproduction should participate actively in<br /><br />
decision-making processes concerning community life.<br /><br />
6.2.5 Community Expectations and Apprehensions<br /><br />
Bauxite mining has been going on the area for over fifty years by both SMHL and former SIEROMCO<br /><br />
Company. These have continued to provide employment opportunities and community development<br /><br />
initiatives in all the affected chiefdoms. These communities especially the two chiefdoms of Banta feel<br /><br />
that SMHL should provide more. Their job and other economic expectations continue to be high<br /><br />
including their proximity to the nearby SRL Ltd. Company.<br /><br />
<br /><br />
Interactions between SMHL and the community continue to go on to address concerns and complaints<br /><br />
such as destruction of farmlands with inadequate crop compensations to farmers, dissatisfaction over<br /><br />
Environmental and Social Impact Assessment (ESIA) Final Report: Prepared by TEDAEnvironmental Consultant: November: 2012<br /><br />
<br /><br />
Page 95<br /><br />
<br /><br />
reclamation methods, pollution in streams and rivers, inequitable implementation of development<br /><br />
projects etc.<br /><br />
<br /><br />
Failure on the part of SMHL to address the concerns and needs of these local issues can have a direct<br /><br />
impact on the acceptability of the project within the host communities. The study however reveals that<br /><br />
the mining operations are expected to have a positive rating of project benefits to these communities.<br /><br />
Recommended mitigation measures to address these issues include the following:<br /><br />
 Implement and promote a more systemic grievance resolving mechanism associated with<br /><br />
SMHL project; and<br /><br />
 Implement the Community Development Action Plan (CDAP) and the ESIA (this document) that<br /><br />
outlines the stakeholder consultation process which is the means to optimize local economic<br /><br />
benefits and the promotion of SMHL’s corporate social responsibility. Successful application of<br /><br />
the process has the potential to increase the significant rating of the impact of the operations..<br /><br />
6.3 Cumulative Impacts<br /><br />
However, irrespective of their nature, the impacts of mining are likely to be experienced across the<br /><br />
region where mine concessions have been granted. Although it is not the role of this ESHIA to<br /><br />
recommend management for other mining operations, and the other operations must be subject to<br /><br />
their own EIA processes, the impacts of mining in the region must be seen holistically and appropriate<br /><br />
management measures must be implemented to limit the cumulative negative impacts of this and<br /><br />
adjacent mining concessions. It will therefore be important for the mining operations to communicate<br /><br />
and develop co-operative strategies to mitigate their cumulative impacts.<br /><br />
<br /><br />
Both social and biophysical impacts can be expected to be cumulative in nature. The most important<br /><br />
cumulative impacts are:<br /><br />
<br /><br />
<br /><br />
Positive social impacts will result to improved economic conditions in the region. The creation<br /><br />
of a number of mining operations will result in the creation of a large, albeit dispersed market<br /><br />
for goods and services, which will significantly stimulate the local economy and act as a catalyst<br /><br />
for the government to improve service delivery in the region.<br /><br />
<br /><br />
<br /><br />
<br /><br />
Negative social impacts, for instance:<br /><br />
o a population influx (due to other mining operations)<br /><br />
o Price inflation<br /><br />
<br /><br />
Environmental and Social Impact Assessment (ESIA) Final Report: Prepared by TEDAEnvironmental Consultant: November: 2012<br /><br />
<br /><br />
Page 96<br /><br />
<br /><br />
o Change in livelihood from agriculture to mining<br /><br />
o Increased social delinquency<br /><br />
<br /><br />
<br /><br />
Ecosystem function in terms of terrestrial faunal species is already heavily degraded. Thus the<br /><br />
miming activities will compound the existing faunal impoverishment due to existing land use<br /><br />
and settlement. Most threatened large mammals and birds have been impacted nationally by<br /><br />
unsustainable exploitation and previous land use patterns. Further loss of such species due to<br /><br />
the potential impacts would exacerbate this loss. The significant loss will need to be addressed<br /><br />
as post mining activities such as farming may likely not reverse this situation. These impacts<br /><br />
cannot be mitigated in the near future. It is however proposed that a Strategic Environmental be<br /><br />
made for the entire two mining communities that will address this issue.<br /><br />
<br /><br />
<br /><br />
<br /><br />
Impacts on water quality and quantity in the Taia river system and ultimately the entire region.<br /><br />
<br /><br />
<br /><br />
<br /><br />
Diversification of mining operations in Bonthe and Moyamba Districts: There is an ongoing<br /><br />
exploration programme for dry mining by SRL that likely overlap with current mining activities at<br /><br />
SRL and SMHL. The most important cumulative impacts that is associated withese activities are<br /><br />
those related to the diversification of mining operations in the two communities. These impact<br /><br />
may be difficult to assess as all the activities are different from each other and the ESIA that is<br /><br />
prepared can be considered as site specific assessing set of activities only associated with a<br /><br />
single operation.<br /><br />
<br /><br />
<br /><br />
<br /><br />
The road transport infrastructure connecting the two projects to one another needs to be<br /><br />
recognised in terms of its cumulative effect, rather than simply relative to each of the individual<br /><br />
projects. Even though the infrastructure is already in place, an important public concern is dust<br /><br />
generated by haulage trucks and other publc transport. A mitigation action that will be pursued<br /><br />
by the two mines is to resurface the haul roads with bitumen especially for post mining use.<br /><br />
<br /><br />
Cumulative impacts have been addressed in the mitigation measures identified in this Study; and these<br /><br />
mitigation measures have been incorporated in the ESMP. Some of the mitigation measures will require<br /><br />
the setting up of cooperative structures with other mining concessionaires within the region.<br /><br />
<br /><br />
Environmental and Social Impact Assessment (ESIA) Final Report: Prepared by TEDAEnvironmental Consultant: November: 2012<br /><br />
<br /><br />
Page 97<br /><br />
<br /><br />
Section Seven – Environmental Management Plan<br /><br />
This Environmental Management Plan for SMHL is complimentary to the Environmental and Social<br /><br />
Impact Assessment (ESIA) presented in this report that clearly defines all the residual and other specific<br /><br />
actions that SMHL undertakes to implement the measures that will minimize and mitigate any negative<br /><br />
impacts on the human, social and environmental aspects within and around the operational area.<br /><br />
Four main environmental plans are outlined for implementation including: a. Environmental Monitoring<br /><br />
Plan, b. Emergency Response and Contingency Plan for health and safety workers and properties, c.<br /><br />
Tailings Management Plan and d. Mine Reclamation and Closure Plan. Attention is also paid to specific<br /><br />
environmental elements including air, noise, and waste and water quality. Proper implementation of<br /><br />
the plans will be supported by Standard Operating Procedures (SOP’s), Training, Documentation,<br /><br />
Monitoring, Evaluation and Reporting. Corrective actions will be suggested for review by management.<br /><br />
7.1 Environmental Monitoring Plan<br /><br />
The Environmental Monitoring Plan is specifically designed and implemented for collecting data on<br /><br />
ambient air, water quality, noise level including release of pollutants of major concern and weather.<br /><br />
The objective of air quality management on the mine site is to minimise the generation of suspended<br /><br />
concentrations of deposition of mine derived dust both at the nearest residential villages around the<br /><br />
mine and those linear settlements along the haul roads. To monitor air quality, SMHL will operate and<br /><br />
maintain a network of 4 depositional dust gauges on the mine site to be monitored monthly in<br /><br />
accordance with National and International standards. A book keeping system will be developed for<br /><br />
presenting the raw data. This will be in a form of tables and graphs of the original data. The tables will<br /><br />
further be used to produce statistical summaries which will form the basis of further analysis and<br /><br />
interpretation.<br /><br />
The major sources of noise in mining and related operations in SMHL’s operational area include loading,<br /><br />
excavation, crushing, screening, beneficiation, movement of heavy earth moving vehicles and truck<br /><br />
loading. The locations are the mine sites at Gbonge, Konta, and Wunde, The Washing Plant at<br /><br />
Gondama, Nitti Port and along the main haul roads. Noise levels shall be monitored both during day and<br /><br />
evening on the same day while in operation.<br /><br />
<br /><br />
Environmental and Social Impact Assessment (ESIA) Final Report: Prepared by TEDAEnvironmental Consultant: November: 2012<br /><br />
<br /><br />
Page 98<br /><br />
<br /><br />
The water quality monitoring plan is developed to satisfy the water quality requirements of the EPA/SL.<br /><br />
It is designed to provide information regarding the overall water quality within SMHL project sites,<br /><br />
identify potential water quality problems related to the project operations and how such factors can be<br /><br />
controlled, and to develop measures for the protection and mitigation of water quality. In situ and<br /><br />
analytical water quality monitoring will be performed over a nine month period during the first week of<br /><br />
each month from April to December. The date, time, location, weather conditions and the in situ water<br /><br />
quality data will be recorded on a standard form which will later be transcribed into an electronic format<br /><br />
in a Microsoft Excel Spreadsheet. GPS coordinates and photographs will be taken at each site to<br /><br />
document conditions at the time of sampling.<br /><br />
7.2 Emergency Response and Contingency Plan (ERCP)<br /><br />
This Plan is Sierra Minerals Holdings Ltd. operation’s guide to all procedures and courses of action that<br /><br />
should be followed in the case of any on-site emergency. It identifies those responsible for taking action<br /><br />
immediately after the discovery of and during the response to an emergency, as well as their respective<br /><br />
duties.<br /><br />
Specifically, it will:<br /><br />
<br /><br />
<br /><br />
address any disaster, mishaps or accidents that will disrupt the normal operations of the<br /><br />
industry;<br /><br />
<br /><br />
<br /><br />
<br /><br />
guide the administration to be better prepared to respond to emergencies in its operational<br /><br />
area in an effective manner; and<br /><br />
<br /><br />
<br /><br />
<br /><br />
Ultimately, help to save the lives, reduce property loss or damage as well as the company’s<br /><br />
liability.<br /><br />
<br /><br />
The plans that have been developed for a number of emergencies include:<br /><br />
Fire/Explosion Emergency: In preparing for fire incidences, the Environmental Health and Safety Officer<br /><br />
(EHSO) will be responsible for training a team that will respond to all accidents in how to suppress fire,<br /><br />
rescue personnel and all other drills relating to fire incidences.<br /><br />
The response plan for fire or explosion will require an immediate action and to undertake a recovery<br /><br />
operation to restore essential services to the mine site, cleaning and replacement of materials and<br /><br />
equipments that were damaged.<br /><br />
<br /><br />
Environmental and Social Impact Assessment (ESIA) Final Report: Prepared by TEDAEnvironmental Consultant: November: 2012<br /><br />
<br /><br />
Page 99<br /><br />
<br /><br />
Accidental spills or release of hazardous materials: The purpose of the Spill Prevention and Control<br /><br />
Plan is to establish procedures and guidelines to prevent oil discharges on SMHL Project sites at<br /><br />
Gondama, Nitti Port and Mata Camp. This Plan specifically establishes proper procedures and<br /><br />
equipments needed to address any potential discharges of oils.<br /><br />
SMHL owns six barges that transport bauxite materials from Nitti Port. For spills or release of hazardous<br /><br />
materials in these barges, both preventive and preparatory measures have to be undertaken. A special<br /><br />
emergency response procedure will be developed for both measures.<br /><br />
Road Accidents: The major operational areas of SMHL are interspersed with villages mostly situated<br /><br />
along the main road from Mokanji to Nitti Port, from Mosenesie to Gondama, to Serabu and Mattru.<br /><br />
These roads are used both by public transport, Sierra Rutile Mines and SMHL.<br /><br />
Currently, SMHL outsources contractors that transport mine products to the plant and to Nitti Port. The<br /><br />
contractors will be required to sign agreement with SMHL for compliance with all codes and regulations<br /><br />
that bind transportation of mine products. All vehicles will be equipped with first aid kit and fire<br /><br />
extinguishers.<br /><br />
Floods: The overall objective of this plan is to support the timely and coordinated response to<br /><br />
anticipated floods in the rainy season. This will effectively minimize the impact of such floods on<br /><br />
personnel, infrastructure and the environment. The major receptors will include: mining sites, haulage<br /><br />
roads, main road between Gondama and Mosenesie, main tributaries of the Taia River and the inland<br /><br />
valley swamps within the project area. Other specific sectors to be affected will include: water and<br /><br />
sanitation, bridges and culverts, road sections, communication infrastructure, meteorological and other<br /><br />
monitoring equipments and disruption of electricity.<br /><br />
Crisis Situations/Social Strife: These situations are brought about by unpredictable circumstances that<br /><br />
degenerate into uncontrollable proportions causing chaos. To address these incidences, SMHL will<br /><br />
develop a plan for the dissemination of information to the public. This will include setting up a<br /><br />
mechanism for dealing with grievances in such that issues can be addressed formerly and amicably.<br /><br />
Roles and Responsibilities: A team of qualified personnel from within SMHL’s management will have<br /><br />
direct responsibilities for addressing all emergencies presented above. These include:<br /><br />
<br /><br />
<br /><br />
The General Manager is the overall head who will be responsible for coordinating all activities<br /><br />
and actions at the project site.<br /><br />
<br /><br />
Environmental and Social Impact Assessment (ESIA) Final Report: Prepared by TEDAEnvironmental Consultant: November: 2012<br /><br />
<br /><br />
Page 100<br /><br />
<br /><br />
<br /><br />
<br /><br />
The Environmental Health and Safety Manager who directly reports to the site manager will be<br /><br />
responsible for the overall implementation of the EHS Plan.<br /><br />
<br /><br />
<br /><br />
<br /><br />
The Environmental Officer (EO) will assist the EHSM in responding to all emergencies by<br /><br />
coordinating efforts with other site personnel to mitigate the potential hazards, assist EHSM to<br /><br />
prepare reports, disseminate information on emergencies to staff, work site supervisor during<br /><br />
emergencies, to remediate events and report accordingly.<br /><br />
<br /><br />
<br /><br />
<br /><br />
The Community Affairs Manager (CAM) will be responsible for all matters that have to do with<br /><br />
public relations. In consultation with the General Manager, he will be responsible for<br /><br />

<br /><br />
<br /><br />
<br /><br />
He will lead the site Supervisors in responding to emergencies and work with the HSO to<br /><br />
remediate potential hazards and emergencies.<br /><br />
<br /><br />
<br /><br />
<br /><br />
Site Supervisors who will be responsible for identifying potential events and coordinating any<br /><br />
response to events that occur in their work area.<br /><br />
<br /><br />
<br /><br />
<br /><br />
A response team which will be recruited and trained by the EHSM for all emergencies on site.<br /><br />
<br /><br />
Communication and Monitoring: The methods and procedures that will be applied in the event of<br /><br />
emergencies will include: a. Internal Communication that will be used to alert workers to hazards by<br /><br />
means of alarms, cell phones and radios. b. Training will be required during the workshop for all<br /><br />
employees on this system of communication.<br /><br />
SMHL will establish and maintain a monitoring system of its activities that could have an environmental<br /><br />
health and safety impact. This will include a recording of information on performance, and an evaluation<br /><br />
of compliances with legal and other requirements.<br /><br />
7.3 Tailings Dam Management Plan<br /><br />
Currently, Gondama plant site has two dumps for storing the sludge which is a slurry mixture of mineral<br /><br />
suspended matters and industrial water. The two sludge settling and storage dumps are built to be<br /><br />
operated and to function independently so that the repairs or emergency interventions do not disturb<br /><br />
the main activity. The sludge settling pond no.1 is built East from the washing plant and consists of<br /><br />
three washing sludge retention ponds separated by embankments (separating dams) and provided with<br /><br />
clear and storm water discharge spillways. The sludge settling pond no. 2, built west from the washing<br /><br />
Environmental and Social Impact Assessment (ESIA) Final Report: Prepared by TEDAEnvironmental Consultant: November: 2012<br /><br />
<br /><br />
Page 101<br /><br />
<br /><br />
plant takes into consideration the settling operations and water clearing operation under normal<br /><br />
parameters before the water is discharged in the water streams.<br /><br />
SMHL’s production target is set up at 1.7 million tons of washed bauxite per year, the washing plant<br /><br />
operating with a production capacity of 330 tons/h. The washing operation consists of removing the<br /><br />
tailings with clay, ferruginous mineral silica content, pieces of rocks from the conveyor belts as well as<br /><br />
other materials which are in the ore composition. Therefore, as calculated in the time frame and for the<br /><br />
purpose reaching a good washing efficiency, the washing plant requires 10,000 cubic meters of water/<br /><br />
day.<br /><br />

<br /><br />
To reduce the quantity of water used in the bauxite washing operations, a study was prepared<br /><br />
concerning the installation of a new pumping station in order to recirculate and reuse the clear water so<br /><br />
as to reduce the caught water requirement and water discharged to surface outlets. This would also<br /><br />
lead to a reduction in the costs associated with the electric power currently consumed from using the<br /><br />
open system and which could be applied in the next stage. An additional water pump has been installed<br /><br />
in order to reduce the quantity of water caught from Taia River.<br /><br />
The downstream side of the embankment shall be provided with spillways, grassy berms to prevent the<br /><br />
mentioned incidents (which are likely to cause failures of the hydro-technical constructions, including<br /><br />
dump closing, if the depression curve is above the attention level) from happening.<br /><br />
Monitoring: The monitoring is achieved by the sludge dump operator and the daily and/ or weekly<br /><br />
observations, readings are recorded in the Registry Book for the control and monitoring of the settling<br /><br />
pond behaviour over time, where the day, hour, data taken in the measuring points, comments and<br /><br />
conclusions are recorded.<br /><br />
Environmental and Social Impact Assessment (ESIA) Final Report: Prepared by TEDAEnvironmental Consultant: November: 2012<br /><br />
<br /><br />
Page 102<br /><br />
<br /><br />
The monitoring activity is mainly focused on verifying the following elements whose purpose is to<br /><br />
minimize the ecological, social and economical risk situations.<br /><br />
The monitoring of the sludge deposit area and the embankments freeboard shall include the recording<br /><br />
of the area width, the hydraulic surface area and the excessive accumulation of storm waters in the<br /><br />
pond.<br /><br />
The monitoring also consists of quarterly land surveys which provide updated information regarding the<br /><br />
coordinates and levelling of the area as compared to reference data, so that the horizontal and vertical<br /><br />
movements on the sinking landmarks comply with the limits set up in the regulation and not endanger<br /><br />
the embankments safety.<br /><br />
The sludge dump operation is monitored by the following measuring and control systems:<br /><br />
<br /><br />
<br /><br />
Hydrometric gauges;<br /><br />
<br /><br />
<br /><br />
<br /><br />
Fix and mobile topographic landmarks network;<br /><br />
<br /><br />
<br /><br />
<br /><br />
Control and sinking landmarks, mileposts, placed in the areas sensitive to wetting;<br /><br />
<br /><br />
<br /><br />
<br /><br />
Observation areas for possible ex-filtration’s;<br /><br />
<br /><br />
<br /><br />
<br /><br />
Sampling and monitoring points for the quality of waters discharged into the water streams.<br /><br />
<br /><br />
The current assessment activity concerning the safety of the sludge dump components mainly consists<br /><br />
of the following:<br /><br />
<br /><br />
<br /><br />
Daily checking by the internal personnel of the constructions, operational facilities, slurry<br /><br />
transport ducts slipways operation. Responsible Chief Miners, Topometer SMHL<br /><br />
<br /><br />
<br /><br />
<br /><br />
Daily reading of the piezometric level at the observation drillings. Responsible Environmental<br /><br />
Labs<br /><br />
<br /><br />
<br /><br />
<br /><br />
Verification of potential movements at the landmarks monitoring the hydro-technical<br /><br />
constructions safety and stability.<br /><br />
<br /><br />
<br /><br />
<br /><br />
Verification twice a year of the topographic landmarks height levels and coordinates, of the<br /><br />
horizontal and vertical movement value in the case of control landmarks positioned on the crest<br /><br />
and slopes of the pond.<br /><br />
<br /><br />
Environmental and Social Impact Assessment (ESIA) Final Report: Prepared by TEDAEnvironmental Consultant: November: 2012<br /><br />
<br /><br />
Page 103<br /><br />
<br /><br />
The monitoring of the embankment constructions behaviour in time is a job assignment of the operating<br /><br />
personnel and the results interpretation shall be made by the engineer responsible for the sludge dump<br /><br />
supervision.<br /><br />
Monitoring of the sludge dump also includes physical- chemical analyses performed in the laboratory by<br /><br />
means of fix and mobile instrumentation in order to check the compliance of the emissions and<br /><br />
emissions within the maximum accepted limits stipulated by the regulations in force for the following<br /><br />
environmental factors:<br /><br />
<br /><br />
<br /><br />
Air – analysis of the air emissions shall be performed once a year in the area bordering the<br /><br />
sludge dump and the analyses results shall be interpreted against the suspended dust and<br /><br />
particulate matter maximum accepted limits stipulated by the regulations in force.<br /><br />
<br /><br />
<br /><br />
<br /><br />
Surface water – physical- chemical analyses shall be performed on the water discharged to the<br /><br />
outlet at intervals as specified for each of the characteristic indicators: suspended matters,<br /><br />
heavy metals. The results of these analyses shall also be compared to the maximum approved<br /><br />
limits stipulated by the applicable regulations.<br /><br />
<br /><br />
<br /><br />
<br /><br />
Soil – soil samples shall be taken from the sludge dump vicinity at least once a year, especially<br /><br />
from those areas located in the main direction of the wind and laboratory analyses shall be<br /><br />
performed to determine the heavy metal content at 30cm average depth.<br /><br />
<br /><br />
The alternative to build successive ponds and embankment downstream from the operational ponds of<br /><br />
the eastern dump is under analysis, however, such possibility can only be taken into consideration<br /><br />
during the last years of operation and useful deposit running-off in the mining perimeter, in order not to<br /><br />
endanger the safe operation of the dams and possibly of the embankments raised at maximum height of<br /><br />
the crest as stipulated in the regulation.<br /><br />
<br /><br />
7.4 Waste Management Plan<br /><br />
This Waste Management Plan (WMP) was prepared for bauxite mining activities by Sierra Mineral<br /><br />
Holdings, I. Ltd (SMHL). The plan addresses methods of handling, storing and disposing of wastes that<br /><br />
will generated during SMHL’s operation.<br /><br />
<br /><br />
Specifically, it describes the procedures, methods and<br /><br />
<br /><br />
framework that will be applied to waste generation and disposal.<br /><br />
<br /><br />
Environmental and Social Impact Assessment (ESIA) Final Report: Prepared by TEDAEnvironmental Consultant: November: 2012<br /><br />
<br /><br />
Page 104<br /><br />
<br /><br />
The objectives of this Management Plan are to:<br /><br />
<br /><br />
<br /><br />
Establish Standard Operating Procedures (SOPs) for SMHL construction, operations and closure<br /><br />
activities;<br /><br />
<br /><br />
<br /><br />
<br /><br />
Define the company’s requirements and procedures to guide the Project Management Team<br /><br />
and other Project contractors;<br /><br />
<br /><br />
<br /><br />
<br /><br />
Define roles and responsibilities of the waste management team;<br /><br />
<br /><br />
<br /><br />
<br /><br />
Present remedial measures to minimize waste generation; and<br /><br />
<br /><br />
<br /><br />
<br /><br />
Define monitoring and reporting procedures for waste management.<br /><br />
<br /><br />
The plan addresses the management of both industrial and domestic waste. The plan covers all activities<br /><br />
that could result in the generation of waste and the potential adverse effects of waste and domestic<br /><br />
waste throughout all phases of the Project. The main issues that are addressed include:<br /><br />
<br /><br />
<br /><br />
Types of wastes to be generated:<br /><br />
<br /><br />
<br /><br />
<br /><br />
Methods used to identify those wastes;<br /><br />
<br /><br />
<br /><br />
<br /><br />
Handling procedures and<br /><br />
<br /><br />
<br /><br />
<br /><br />
Methods of disposal<br /><br />
<br /><br />
The types of wastes that are expected to be generated during the construction and operational stages of<br /><br />
the project are identified. Depending on the types to be generated, certain wastes are expended to be<br /><br />
disposed of at site, reused or recycled. The waste disposal facilities and methods of disposal are fully<br /><br />
discussed in this plan.<br /><br />
Waste Management Procedures<br /><br />
SMHL will apply the following management procedures for waste.<br /><br />
Waste Recycling: SMHL consults with the community on the demand for any recyclable products such<br /><br />
<br /><br />
as scrap metal, used tires, cardboard and wood which may have a value or a use locally. The company<br /><br />
also plans to look at commercial options for recycling; if recycling facilities are available and accessible<br /><br />
on a commercially-justifiable basis, waste will be recycled. If not, waste will be disposed of in accordance<br /><br />
with this Management Plan<br /><br />
<br /><br />
Environmental and Social Impact Assessment (ESIA) Final Report: Prepared by TEDAEnvironmental Consultant: November: 2012<br /><br />
<br /><br />
Page 105<br /><br />
<br /><br />
Solid Waste: This includes all office wastes, concrete, steel, rubble, timber, plasterboard and mixed<br /><br />
<br /><br />
construction waste. These are all classified as non-hazardous. These will mainly be generated during the<br /><br />
operational phase with minor amounts generated in the construction and decommissioning phases.<br /><br />
5.4 Liquid Wastes<br /><br />
<br /><br />
The procedure for managing these wastes includes the following:<br /><br />
<br /><br />
 Any spills will be cleaned up using absorbent materials and placed in bins;<br /><br />
<br /><br />
<br /><br />
There will be no onsite disposal of liquid direct to the soil surface or storm water collection<br /><br />
system; and<br /><br />
<br /><br />
<br /><br />
<br /><br />
Non-hazardous waste bins will be finally disposed of to the incinerator or the landfill which is<br /><br />
an engineered cell within the Tailings Storage Facility (TSF).<br /><br />
<br /><br />
Litter: This includes any general litter on-site, and the objective is to minimise the visual impact of litter<br /><br />
<br /><br />
on the site and surrounding area, and minimise the potential to attract vermin. This will be achieved<br /><br />
through the provision of appropriate disposal facilities around the project site.<br /><br />
Mine and Processing Waste: For the mine and processing wastes, it is the responsibility of the<br /><br />
<br /><br />
Operation Manager to undertake waste characterization and manage the monitoring program to ensure<br /><br />
that the proposed waste management practices are appropriate to the wastes being generated. .<br /><br />
Responsibilities, Training and Record Keeping<br /><br />
During SML’s operation, the Environment Health and Safety Manager will be responsible for the<br /><br />
operation and record keeping of the Waste Management Plan.<br /><br />
Monitoring and Control<br /><br />
The following monitoring programs will be undertaken by SML and appropriate records will be kept.<br /><br />
<br /><br />
<br /><br />
<br /><br />
Refuse quantity and material delivered to the domestic waste facility, hazardous waste<br /><br />
facility and composing facility;<br /><br />
<br /><br />
<br /><br />
<br /><br />
Surface and groundwater monitoring;<br /><br />
<br /><br />
Environmental and Social Impact Assessment (ESIA) Final Report: Prepared by TEDAEnvironmental Consultant: November: 2012<br /><br />
<br /><br />
Page 106<br /><br />
<br /><br />
<br /><br />
<br /><br />
Surrounding vegetation and soils around waste facilities; and<br /><br />
<br /><br />
<br /><br />
<br /><br />
Non-environmental features, such as, fences, roads, and signs.<br /><br />
<br /><br />
Notice will be placed around the landfill and hazardous facility to promote worker and resident safety.<br /><br />
The Environmental, Health and Safety Manager will be responsible for distributing and maintaining<br /><br />
these notices and signs. The wastes facilities will be maintained with security in prevent unauthorised<br /><br />
access to the landfill site.<br /><br />
<br /><br />
7.5 Occupational Health and Safety Plan<br /><br />
This Occupational Health and Safety (OH&S) Plan is Sierra Minerals Holdings Ltd. operation’s guide to all<br /><br />
procedures and courses of action that should be followed to ensure the effective management of<br /><br />
worker health and safety by SML Project. The plan contains the methods that shall be used to prevent<br /><br />
adverse effects occurring during construction and operations of the project, monitoring and determining<br /><br />
for effective mitigation measures.<br /><br />
In this regard, all employees and contractors of SMHL will adhere to the OH & S procedures contained<br /><br />
in this report to ensure a timely response to employees’ health and safety. It has also been prepared to<br /><br />
addresses the requirements that are set out in the guidelines of the International Finance Corporation<br /><br />
(IFC), the Environment Protection Act 2009 of Sierra Leone and the National Environmental Policy of<br /><br />
2000. It is also expected that the Plan will be reviewed and updated to meet the requirements of the<br /><br />
international safety standard, OHSAS 14001.<br /><br />
Purpose and Scope<br /><br />
The purpose of this Occupational Health & Safety Plan is to:<br /><br />
<br /><br />
 Define and communicate the principles, practices and expectations of Health & Safety<br /><br />
objectives, SMHL project;<br /><br />
 Set out how the project is designed to manage and reduce risks associated with both routine<br /><br />
activities and unplanned events;<br /><br />
 Define the criteria against which Health & Safety performance will be judged;<br /><br />
Environmental and Social Impact Assessment (ESIA) Final Report: Prepared by TEDAEnvironmental Consultant: November: 2012<br /><br />
<br /><br />
Page 107<br /><br />
<br /><br />
<br /><br />
<br /><br />
Describe areas of Health & Safety responsibilities for the SMHL Project team, including<br /><br />
Contractors;<br /><br />
<br /><br />
 Describe the Occupational Health & Safety Standard Operating Procedures (SOPs) that will<br /><br />
ensure that Health & Safety activities are organised, managed and reported in a systematic and<br /><br />
consistent manner;<br /><br />
<br /><br />
<br /><br />
Describe the development of the safety cases and safety plans;<br /><br />
<br /><br />
<br /><br />
<br /><br />
Ensure that the Project provides inherently safe and secure facilities;<br /><br />
<br /><br />
 Address the process of ongoing assessment and continuous improvement of health and safety<br /><br />
performance;<br /><br />
 To provide education and training for staff at SMHL’s operations in health and safety issues;<br /><br />
<br /><br />
The plan is applicable to all SMHL project related activities including camp and port facilities and<br /><br />
contractors. In all cases, Contractors will be required to submit their safety management plans to SMHL<br /><br />
for review and approval for use if is of a higher standard or exceed those provided by the Contractors.<br /><br />
<br /><br />
Roles and Responsibilities<br /><br />
The General Manager is the overall head who will be responsible for coordinating all activities and actions at the<br /><br />
project site. He will work with other managers who will be responsible for specific activities that will have to do<br /><br />
with the health and safety of workers. Along with the Environmental Health and Safety Manager and the<br /><br />
Community Affairs Manager, he will also be responsible for all other relations outside the project site as and<br /><br />
when needed to make sure that the mining community is adequately informed about events.<br /><br />
<br /><br />
Hazard Identification and Risk Analyses<br /><br />
All hazards identified at SMHL operations are assessed in accordance with the Risk Matrix prepared in<br /><br />
this plan which takes into account the probabilities of occurrence and severity of consequences in<br /><br />
relation to personnel and assets of the project and surrounding environment. In this regard, all<br /><br />
employees and contractors will be involved in the training of hazard identification and risk assessment<br /><br />
process so that day-to-day hazards are identified and control measures are determined and<br /><br />
implemented.<br /><br />
<br /><br />
Environmental and Social Impact Assessment (ESIA) Final Report: Prepared by TEDAEnvironmental Consultant: November: 2012<br /><br />
<br /><br />
Page 108<br /><br />
<br /><br />
The following steps that SMHL plans to identify and manage risks during the life cycle of its operation<br /><br />
will include:<br /><br />
 Identification of hazards that exist in the project area;<br /><br />
 Assessment of risks entailed in these hazards;<br /><br />
 Evaluation of the degree of the risk represented by each hazard;<br /><br />
 Evaluation of the consequences resulting from the hazards by selecting the category which best<br /><br />
describes the effects of the mishaps on personnel, the general public and the environment.<br /><br />
<br /><br />
Identification of Hazards<br /><br />
This will entail gathering of information on biophysical, chemical and technical properties of the project<br /><br />
site and surrounding areas. Inclusive of these are artificial accidents and natural hazards such as fire,<br /><br />
explosion, equipment failures etc.<br /><br />

Risk Analyses<br /><br />
Knowledge of potential risks from these natural hazards, in both the environment, mine facilities, mine<br /><br />
personnel and the public is necessary for establishing the health, safety, and environmental<br /><br />
management objectives and how to manage them with the most appropriate mitigation measures. The<br /><br />
determination of the risk of an incident/accident associated with each hazard at the project’s<br /><br />
operational sites will entail:<br /><br />
 Identification of the potential failures or accidents;<br /><br />
 Quantification of the material that may released when any failure takes place;<br /><br />
 An estimation of the probability of occurrences; and<br /><br />
<br /><br />
<br /><br />
An evaluation of the effects of such occurrences based on certain scenarios such as most<br /><br />
probable and worst case events.<br /><br />

<br /><br />
Purchasing and Control of Materials and Services<br /><br />
To minimize the hazards and risk associated with the supply of materials and services form contractors<br /><br />
and other service providers, SMHL will:<br /><br />
 Incorporate its health and safety requirements in the tender specification all major contracts.<br /><br />
 Reserve the right to review of the tendering of contractors’ health and safety systems and<br /><br />
performance prior to the commence of any business;<br /><br />
 Require the contractor to examine SMHL’s procedures such as hazard identification and risk<br /><br />
assessment, permit to work, safety risk assessment, communications and emergency response;<br /><br />
 Train employees and contractors in the health, safety and environmental implications<br /><br />
associated with purchases and the control measures available to minimise risks associated with<br /><br />
identified hazards;<br /><br />
<br /><br />
<br /><br />
Incorporate health, safety and environmental requirements into purchase specifications and<br /><br />
/or purchase orders;<br /><br />
<br /><br />
 Ensure that employees and contractors are consulted prior to purchase of materials and<br /><br />
equipment with potential health and safety issues;<br /><br />
 Reserve the right to inspect and verify materials against purchase specifications;<br /><br />
<br /><br />
<br /><br />
Ensure that provision be made for a competent person to check the health, safety and<br /><br />
environmental implications of any new chemical and for material safety data sheets (MSDS) to<br /><br />
be made available to employees prior to use of the chemicals concerned; and<br /><br />
<br /><br />
<br /><br />
<br /><br />
Keep MSDS Registers up to date and in a form accessible to employees and sub-contractors in<br /><br />
an emergency situation.<br /><br />
<br /><br />

<br /><br />
First Aid Responses<br /><br />
The key objective of the First Aid response is to demonstrate that there is an effective emergency<br /><br />
response process put at the mine site put in place to reduce the impact of an emergency to levels that<br /><br />
meet the expectations and needs of personnel, and the mining community. All the personnel at the<br /><br />
project site will be first trained, from which a specialist team will be set up and trained in the first aid<br /><br />
response plan that will involve the identification of potential risks and the provision of feedback to<br /><br />
improve on the response plan. First aid boxes will be installed at all strategic locations including vehicles<br /><br />
that are in use during operations. To achieve the above objectives, SMHL shall undertake the following:<br /><br />
 Ensure that qualified first-aid is provided at the project site at all times;<br /><br />
 Equipped first-aid station and eye wash points should be provided close to all work places<br /><br />
where immediate flushing with water is the recommended first-aid response.<br /><br />
 Develop and maintain first-aid procedure;<br /><br />
 Develop an emergency response plan to include the procedure and structure for emergency<br /><br />
response;<br /><br />
 Develop specific potential emergencies as outlined in the risk matrix; and<br /><br />
 Prepare and implement an emergency response manual for the project to include procedures<br /><br />
for integrating SMHL procedures with other contractors’ procedures.<br /><br />
<br /><br />
Incident/Accident Reporting<br /><br />
The objective is to demonstrate that appropriate procedures are put in place for the systematic<br /><br />
reporting of accidents, incidents and near misses; and for an effective follow up and review of safety<br /><br />
performance made to prevent measures of adverse situations. The process will include:<br /><br />
 An identification of the causes of incidents and accidents;<br /><br />
 Ensuring a systematic approach for reporting and investigating incidents and accidents; and<br /><br />
 Preventing future accidents and incidents.<br /><br />
<br /><br />
The Safety Officer will compile and maintain records of each accident/incident occurring, including<br /><br />
details in each case of any corrective action taken. To achieve this, SMHL shall develop, implement and<br /><br />
maintain incident/accident reporting and investigating procedure to include:<br /><br />
 Incident reporting system;<br /><br />
Environmental and Social Impact Assessment (ESIA) Final Report: Prepared by TEDAEnvironmental Consultant: November: 2012<br /><br />
<br /><br />
Page 116<br /><br />
<br /><br />
 Accident reporting system; Corrective action register with records of all occurrences and<br /><br />
progress on the follow up of corrective actions.<br /><br />
<br /><br />
Employee Health and Safety Data System<br /><br />
SMHL will maintain a data system for the collection, storage, analysis, retrieval and dissemination of all<br /><br />
health and safety information on its employees.<br /><br />
<br /><br />
In collaboration with SMHL medical personnel, the EHS Manager and the Human Resources Manager,<br /><br />
the Safety Officer shall develop, implement and maintain a procedure for the collection, maintenance<br /><br />
and confidential retention of employee health and safety information. The procedure will detail<br /><br />
performance indicators that will be compared to similar mining operations. Such data will be reported<br /><br />
on monthly basis.<br /><br />
<br /><br />
To ensure the integrity of SMHL project and safety of its personnel, a health monitoring system will<br /><br />
established to ensure that employees are fit for work and be able to work safely at all times. To achieve<br /><br />
this, SMHL shall develop, implement and maintain a health monitoring procedure which will include:<br /><br />
 A medical report prior to employment;<br /><br />
 Injury management and return to work system; and<br /><br />
 Fitness for work procedures.<br /><br />
<br /><br />
Monitoring and Training<br /><br />
Occupational health and safety monitoring programs will be established to verify the effectiveness of<br /><br />
prevention and control strategies. The occupational health and safety monitoring program should<br /><br />
include: Safety inspection and testing which will include regular inspection and testing of all safety<br /><br />
features focusing on engineering and personal protective features, work procedures, places of work,<br /><br />
installations, equipment, and tools used. All instruments installed or used for monitoring and recording<br /><br />
of working environment parameters should be regularly tested and calibrated, and the respective<br /><br />
records maintained. Monitoring and analyses should be conducted according to internationally<br /><br />
recognized methods and standards.<br /><br />
<br /><br />
Environmental and Social Impact Assessment (ESIA) Final Report: Prepared by TEDAEnvironmental Consultant: November: 2012<br /><br />
<br /><br />

<br /><br />
Training activities for employees and visitors should be adequately monitored and documented.<br /><br />
Emergency exercises, including fire drills, should be documented adequately. Other service providers<br /><br />
and contractors will be required to submit to SMHL adequate training documentation before start of<br /><br />
their assignment.<br /><br />
<br /><br />
7.6 Mine Reclamation and Closure Plan<br /><br />
Under the current EPA/SL requirements, SMHL is required to provide updates to the reclamation Plan as<br /><br />
mining proceeds. These updates will include revision of the reclamation and closure plan that will<br /><br />
address the actual site conditions. It will include new data such as the reclaimed sites to date, proposed<br /><br />
waste dump sites, etc. Periodic reviews of the plan will be undertaken in accordance with EPA/SL<br /><br />
requirements.<br /><br />
The reclamation plan that is developed is in consistent with the following guidelines and regulations:<br /><br />
<br /><br />
<br /><br />
Guidelines addressed by the EPA/SL,<br /><br />
<br /><br />
<br /><br />
<br /><br />
Best standard practices of the International Finance Corporation (IFC),<br /><br />
<br /><br />
<br /><br />
<br /><br />
SMHL guidelines for reclamation of tailings pond and other facilities.<br /><br />
<br /><br />
The reclamation objectives for the plan include:<br /><br />
<br /><br />
<br /><br />
Converting areas disturbed by mining to a condition appropriate for pre-mining uses such as<br /><br />
cropland and small residential development.<br /><br />
<br /><br />
<br /><br />
<br /><br />
Decommissioning and salvaging on mine sites, facilities, buildings, equipments and ancillary<br /><br />
facilities.<br /><br />
<br /><br />
<br /><br />
<br /><br />
Ensuring the mitigation of adverse impacts in the post mining scenario, including residual<br /><br />
problems such as soil erosion, slope failures, waste piling on slopes, and ground water seepage<br /><br />
from mine works.<br /><br />
<br /><br />
<br /><br />
<br /><br />
Establish a long-term biophysical stability in the disturbed areas so as to minimize the<br /><br />
requirements for long-term monitoring.<br /><br />
<br /><br />
Mine Reclamation and Closure Methods: SMHL has been engaged in reclamation activities concurrently<br /><br />
<br /><br />
with mining, thereby reducing reclamation costs at the end of the project as well as developing and<br /><br />
improving reclamation techniques to maximize vegetation success and productivity.<br /><br />
The activities to be undertaken by SMHL will include:<br /><br />
Environmental and Social Impact Assessment (ESIA) Final Report: Prepared by TEDAEnvironmental Consultant: November: 2012<br /><br />
<br /><br />
Page 118<br /><br />
<br /><br />
<br /><br />
<br /><br />
Slope Stability and Surface Water Protection: Some of the structural measures that will be put<br /><br />
in place will include diversions, backfilling and the construction of grass waterways. On the<br /><br />
Gbonge Hills for example, cut and fills will be part of the extraction and reclamation activities.<br /><br />
<br /><br />
<br /><br />
<br /><br />
Soil Salvage and Redistribution: Materials that are used for reclamation will be collected from<br /><br />
previously stockpiled wastes produced prior to excavation. The quantity of soil that will be<br /><br />
placed over the entire site will vary according to the availability or volume of stockpiles, the<br /><br />
plant species that is used, and the proposed land use of the site and the steepness of the slope.<br /><br />
<br /><br />
<br /><br />
<br /><br />
Surface Grading and Seedbed Preparation: Surface grading will be consistent with the<br /><br />
surrounding topography. Sediment and erosion control measures may include installation of silt<br /><br />
fences, diversion channels and sediment traps. The final result will be a scenario of land use that<br /><br />
will be safe for farming by the local community. For the mine panels, reclamation will involve<br /><br />
stabilizing the panel surfaces and establishing vegetation cover. Backfilling will be carried out<br /><br />
until a Salvaging and Demolition of Facilities: Demolition procedures for all facilities will<br /><br />
include removal of: a. hazardous materials from sites; b. equipments and buildings; c. power<br /><br />
lines; d. burial of concrete foundations etc.<br /><br />
<br /><br />
Specific Reclamation Methods: Specific reclamation activities will be undertaken including:<br /><br />
<br /><br />
<br /><br />
Access roads constructed during Sieromco period. The major roads to Gbonge, Walihun, Nitti<br /><br />
and Mokanji are used both by SMHL and the public with repairs done by the former. These may<br /><br />
not be required for closure, but the repair works will be taken over by the Sierra Leone Roads<br /><br />
Authority (SLRA). Only access and haulage roads of no economic significance will be closed by<br /><br />
ripping to remove compaction and regarding to blend with the local terrain.<br /><br />
<br /><br />
<br /><br />
<br /><br />
Waste Facilities: The closure of this facility will include salvaging of the soil to cover the dump<br /><br />
site shaped to enhance good drainage conditions. This cover will be revegetated using the<br /><br />
necessary soil treatment.<br /><br />
<br /><br />
<br /><br />
<br /><br />
Stockpiles: Progressive reclamation of these sites will include regarding, construction of soil<br /><br />
cover, and revegetation. It is expected that all stockpiles will be completely processed prior to<br /><br />
closure. The covered and reclaimed areas will be monitored and where necessary, maintained<br /><br />
for years.<br /><br />
<br /><br />
<br /><br />
<br /><br />
Tailings Pond: Currently, physical upgrading of the tailings pond at Gondama is necessary and<br /><br />
this will be initiated very soon. Plans are also ahead for a new tailings facility to be constructed.<br /><br />
Reclamation and closure of any these facilities will commence at the end of the mining<br /><br />
<br /><br />
Environmental and Social Impact Assessment (ESIA) Final Report: Prepared by TEDAEnvironmental Consultant: November: 2012<br /><br />
<br /><br />
Page 119<br /><br />
<br /><br />
operations. Based on the available information on the physical structure of the pond, the<br /><br />
establishment of vegetative cover is the most suitable strategy. Revegetation trials will be<br /><br />
undertaken to select the most effective method. Because of its physical properties, additional<br /><br />
growth medium would be required to enhance stabilization and plant growth. Once properly<br /><br />
stabilized, revegetation will be done accordingly.<br /><br />
<br /><br />
<br /><br />
gentle rolling topography is established. Methods of revegetation will include seeding, plant<br /><br />
propagation and tree or shrub planting. Alley Farming will be introduced to minimize the<br /><br />
commonly bush fallowing practices. The method will provide green manure from hedgerows for<br /><br />
the tree crops, provide shade during fallow periods, create barriers that will control soil erosion<br /><br />
on sloping lands and provide wood-fuel for domestic purposes.<br /><br />
<br /><br />
Community Involvement: In the implementation of this MRCP, SMHL will implement an effective and<br /><br />
transparent engagement, communication and verifiable reporting arrangements with the company’s<br /><br />
stakeholders making sure that the affected communities are given the opportunity to ask questions<br /><br />
discuss the plan and express any concerns they may have, so that SMHL will be able to respond to<br /><br />
comments received.<br /><br />
Closure Cost Estimates, Implementation schedule and Monitoring: The closure estimate presented<br /><br />
<br /><br />
herein is based on the general assumption that mining will proceed as planned, and the concurrent<br /><br />
reclamation activities would be based on the mine plan of operations as amended. The estimated cost<br /><br />
(Table 13) is $26,675,000.00. This estimate is based on the assumption that SMHL continues to<br /><br />
undertake the rehabilitation and future decommissioning at the end of the project. The highest cost<br /><br />
estimate is that for returning the land to its prior use for agriculture and forestry. It must be however<br /><br />
noted that although SMHL is performing concurrent reclamation, adequate monitoring report is not<br /><br />
available to provide details of the quality of work performed so far. Also, due to the fact that SMHL has<br /><br />
not produced plans indicating final limits of mining and land disturbance, the estimates have been<br /><br />
prepared based on professional judgment. An estimate based on actual plans may result in an estimate<br /><br />
higher than these provided.<br /><br />
Post closure monitoring will be undertaken to ensure that the reclamation and closure objectives are<br /><br />
met. The aspects that will be monitored will include:<br /><br />
<br /><br />
<br /><br />
Vegetation: Site inspection to determine abundance and composition, and the overall success of<br /><br />
revegetation.<br /><br />
<br /><br />
Environmental and Social Impact Assessment (ESIA) Final Report: Prepared by TEDAEnvironmental Consultant: November: 2012<br /><br />
<br /><br />
Page 120<br /><br />
<br /><br />
<br /><br />
<br /><br />
Slope Stability: Site inspection of physical conditions for soil erosion and slope failures at the end<br /><br />
of the wet season.<br /><br />
<br /><br />
<br /><br />
<br /><br />
Water Quality: monitoring of parameters for quality and recovery.<br /><br />
<br /><br />
<br /><br />
<br /><br />
Security Deposit: A security deposit to be provided by SMHL is considered as part of the<br /><br />
<br /><br />
reclamation and closure liability for the mines. The estimate that is provided here assumes that<br /><br />
SMHL is performing progressive reclamation activities. As such, SMHL should be entitled to a<br /><br />
reduction in the security requirements agreed upon between SMHL and the GOSL. At the time<br /><br />
of preparing this report, no security bond has been prepared and this is anticipated as soon as<br /><br />
possiblic.<br /><br />
<br /><br />
7.7 Public Consultation and Disclosure Plan<br /><br />
The Public Consultation and Disclosure meeting was held for SMHL-Vimetco Bauxite Project’s ESIA draft<br /><br />
report on Monday 28th January 2013 at Serabu, Moyamba and Bo City Council Hall (Regional level<br /><br />
meeting). The consultation was meant to provide stakeholders including local residents, nongovernmental organizations (NGOs), civil society groups, government and other interested parties with<br /><br />
project information and to allow them to fully participate once more in the preparation of the ESIA final<br /><br />
report and its implementation process. The meeting was chaired in Krio for common understanding, a<br /><br />
total of about 121 participants were recorded in the attendance list. The meeting started off with both<br /><br />
Christian and Muslim prayers.<br /><br />
Key Objectives of the Public Disclosure and Consultation Process<br /><br />
The key objectives of the public consultation and disclosure are to:<br /><br />
<br /><br />

report,<br /><br />

<br /><br />

and appropriate consideration be given to those issues.<br /><br />
