<br /><br />
Page 126<br /><br />
<br /><br />
Section 8 – Conclusions and Recommendations<br /><br />
8.1 Conclusions<br /><br />
When all the impacts are considered the two most prominent are those that are prevalent on surface<br /><br />
water, including the hydrology and natural drainage systems in the natural environment and the<br /><br />
disconnect existing between the communities expectations and apprehensions and the realistic gains<br /><br />
which may accrue through a more equitable transfer mechanism. Although there will be a number of<br /><br />
social impacts of high significance, the majority of the impacts on social conditions are of moderate<br /><br />
significance.<br /><br />
The positive economic impact of the mines is highly significant, and the revenue from taxation that will<br /><br />
accrue to government provides resources that can be used to significantly improve the condition of<br /><br />
infrastructure in the South east region of the country . The economic benefit to the local economy is also<br /><br />
significant, since the project will not only support several hundred employees directly, but it will also<br /><br />
support significantly more of their dependents. It is estimated that, on average, each employee will<br /><br />
support eight dependents or family members. This should result in a general improvement in standards<br /><br />
of living in the study area and provides an alternative livelihood to the current subsistence farming.<br /><br />
<br /><br />
There is however the need for the Company to institute a more systemic mechanism for the resolution<br /><br />
of social contentions and provide a more effective platform for dialogue with the host communities and<br /><br />
other stakeholders. It would also be imperative that the Community Development Action Plan (CDAP) be<br /><br />
validated and implemented.<br /><br />
8.2 Recommendations<br /><br />
<br /><br />
<br /><br />
Agricultural Development Fund should be made available through the District Council<br /><br />
<br /><br />
<br /><br />
<br /><br />
Ensure prompt payment of surface rent and compensation to the appropriate beneficiary<br /><br />
<br /><br />
<br /><br />
<br /><br />
Re-assessment/re-evaluation of total land area slated as concession zone<br /><br />
<br /><br />
<br /><br />
<br /><br />
Involve the communities in land negotiation and benefit sharing mechanism in order to avoid<br /><br />
future disputes and disruption in the mining operation.<br /><br />
<br /><br />
<br /><br />
<br /><br />
Develop sustainable livelihood framework in the community – skills training, scholarships,<br /><br />
income diversification schemes etc<br /><br />
<br /><br />
Environmental and Social Impact Assessment (ESIA) Final Report: Prepared by TEDAEnvironmental Consultant: November: 2012<br /><br />
<br /><br />
Page 127<br /><br />
<br /><br />
<br /><br />
<br /><br />
Implement the Community Development Action Plan. (CDAP).<br /><br />
<br /><br />
<br /><br />
<br /><br />
Embark on effective land reclamation and rehabilitation scheme.<br /><br />

Topography<br /><br />

Reclaim Mined-out Areas, Reclaim Stockpile Areas, Use designated routes<br /><br />
in the wet season.<br /><br />
<br /><br />
Geology<br /><br />
<br /><br />
Separation of super soil from sub soil during removal.<br /><br />
Proper grading and reshaping of the disturbed<br /><br />
Adequate crop suitability procedures for reclamation work.areas.<br /><br />
Prompt timing of re-vegetation on reclaimed lands to avoid top soil removal<br /><br />
in the early rain events.<br /><br />
Establishment of sediment raps, bunds and where necessary construction<br /><br />
of diversion ditches.<br /><br />
Regular inspection of reclamation and repair works where needed; and<br /><br />
Use of rock mulch on unprotected surfaces.<br /><br />
<br /><br />
Air<br /><br />
<br /><br />
Dust Suppression in the Dry season, Provision of mask to employees in<br /><br />
dusty areas,<br /><br />
use of gaseous control equipment at plant site,<br /><br />
apply traffic safety rules and regulations,<br /><br />
reduce speed limits,<br /><br />
construct speed bumps in villages along haul roads,<br /><br />
<br /><br />
Noise<br /><br />
<br /><br />
Future facilities with high noise impacts should be located well away from<br /><br />
noise sensitive areas.<br /><br />
Mine faces should be used as noise barriers in the immediate vicinity of<br /><br />
villages such as Gbonge, Jenega and Konta.<br /><br />
Protective hearing devices should be used when employees are at work<br /><br />
especially at or near earth-moving equipment, wash plants and power<br /><br />
houses.<br /><br />
<br /><br />
Surface Water<br /><br />
<br /><br />
Stockpiles and overburden should not be placed at nearby drainage ways.<br /><br />
Waste oil, fuel and other lubricants should be stored in strong<br /><br />
containments.<br /><br />
Tailings Management Plan should be properly implemented.<br /><br />
As much as possible, leakages into earth fill dams should be avoided.<br /><br />
Efforts should be made in the wet season to ensure that rain water is<br /><br />
diverted from the TSF<br /><br />
<br /><br />
6<br /><br />
<br /><br />

<br /><br />
Where construction of power lines would be required, features should be<br /><br />
designed so as to achieve landscape designs<br /><br />
Impoundment dams should be rehabilitated to meet international<br /><br />
standards.<br /><br />
Burrow pits and mine faces should be graded<br /><br />

from TSF<br /><br />

<br /><br />
1.2.<br /><br />
Household characteristics<br /><br />
Analysis of socioeconomic data at chiefdom level in the selected communities studied, show that<br /><br />
majority of interviewed respondents were males (71.7%). Although the nature of these are male<br /><br />
dominated as indicated in the number of respondents, the team solicited information from a substantial<br /><br />
number of women (28.3%). This implies that the reported socioeconomic issues in the chiefdoms have<br /><br />
included both men and women feelings and perceptions.<br /><br />
<br /><br />
Environmental and Social Impact Assessment (ESIA)<br /><br />
<br /><br />
Page 143<br /><br />
<br /><br />
As presented in Table A-3 majority of the interviewed respondents in the chiefdoms where data were<br /><br />
collected are married (86.7%). Cases of widowed were very low if not non-existence in some chiefdom.<br /><br />
With an exception of the Upper and Lower Banta Chiefdoms, where the number of widows was<br /><br />
reported (10.3 and 7.7% respectively), the remaining of the chiefdoms had no widows.<br /><br />
An overwhelming majority of respondents were heads of household while 14.8 percent were de jure<br /><br />
household heads that were either never married, were divorced or widowed female headed<br /><br />
households. Among the male respondents, the large majority (85.2%) were de facto heads of<br /><br />
households.<br /><br />
<br /><br />
An analysis of the educational status of respondents, which was defined in terms of different levels of<br /><br />
schooling are presented in Table A 3. There were considerable variations observed among respondents<br /><br />
regarding their educational attainment. In most cases some respondents cited more than one level of<br /><br />
education. In addition to formal schooling some also cited Koranic education. However, 60.1% of<br /><br />
respondents had no formal education (illiterate), 12.3% up to primary school and 10.4% up to secondary<br /><br />
level. Only a small proportion had attained tertiary education. This group comprised teachers and<br /><br />
employed members of staff residing in the chiefdoms. In general, this implies that many people in the<br /><br />
project area had limited opportunities to access income generating activities that demands educational<br /><br />
skills.<br /><br />
On average, a high proportion of respondents had a mean age of 55.6 years and a standard deviation of<br /><br />
15.7 years. Dasse, lower Banta had the highest number of aged respondents, while Kpanda Kemoh had<br /><br />
the lowest number.<br /><br />
Other household characteristics<br /><br />
Table A 4 provides information on other characteristics of household dwellings in the surveyed<br /><br />
communities, such as ownership, roofing, wall and flooring materials. An overwhelming majority of<br /><br />
respondents (94.4%) indicated that they owned house in their respective communities surveyed. The<br /><br />
type of roofing, wall and flooring materials can be viewed as an indicator of the quality of housing, a<br /><br />
dimension of wealth, as well as indicator of health risk.<br /><br />
<br /><br />

1.2.1. Household Assets<br /><br />
In general, household asset ownership is low (Table A 5). Respondents were asked whether they own<br /><br />
physical household assets. The responses are presented at Table xxx. The results show that:<br /><br />
<br /><br />
<br /><br />
70% (n=126) households have a radio with highest proportion in Kpanda Kemoh and Bumpe<br /><br />
chiefdoms. Interestingly, radios are owned only by the male headed households implying a low<br /><br />
asset base among female headed household members.<br /><br />
<br /><br />
<br /><br />
<br /><br />
Slightly above half of the sampled households (53.9%) own a large pestle and mortar with high<br /><br />
proportion in communities in the Upper Banta chiefdoms. These are used for pounding food for<br /><br />
home consumption.<br /><br />
<br /><br />
<br /><br />
<br /><br />
74 households (41.1%) have mobile phones with highest in Kpanda Kemoh Chiefdom.<br /><br />
<br /><br />
<br /><br />
<br /><br />
Nine households, all male headed, have a TV and a generator. Six of these reside in the Lower<br /><br />
Banta Chiefdom. These are not found in Upper Banta and Kpanda Kemoh Chiefdom.<br /><br />
<br /><br />
Environmental and Social Impact Assessment (ESIA)<br /><br />
<br /><br />

<br /><br />
<br /><br />
<br /><br />
Twelve households have a sewing machine, 4 of which are found in Upper Banta and Dasse<br /><br />
chiefdoms<br /><br />
<br /><br />
<br /><br />
<br /><br />
In all communities surveyed only 11 (male headed) households own a bike and five had<br /><br />
motorcycles.<br /><br />

<br /><br />
1.2.2. Infrastructure: Roads<br /><br />
The road network was generally poor in most of the company’s operational areas. This is particularly<br /><br />
evident in many of the communities in Bumpe, Dasse and Upper Banta chiefdoms respectively. The<br /><br />
roads are inaccessible during the raining season and transport is difficult to obtain due to the bad nature<br /><br />
of the roads. Many people in the communities therefore either travel long distances on foot or by<br /><br />
bicycle or motorcycle (‘okada’). The roads in Kpanda Kemoh chiefdoms are preferably accessible all year<br /><br />
round.<br /><br />
<br /><br />
Environmental and Social Impact Assessment (ESIA)<br /><br />
<br /><br />
Page 147<br /><br />
<br /><br />
1.3.<br /><br />
<br /><br />
Household drinking water<br /><br />
<br /><br />
To assess the socioeconomic conditions under which the population lives, respondents were asked to<br /><br />
give specific information about their household environment. The survey collected information about<br /><br />
drinking water, including the source, perceptions about the quality of drinking water, any treatment<br /><br />
prior to drinking and persons who usually collect the water. The source of drinking water is an indicator<br /><br />
of whether or not the water is suitable for drinking. Sources of water believed to be relatively free of<br /><br />
disease are improved sources such as piped water into dwelling units; public taps/standpipes, bore<br /><br />
holes, protected dug wells, protected spring and rainwater. Non improved sources, like unprotected dug<br /><br />
wells, unprotected springs, and surface water are more likely to have disease-causing agents that have a<br /><br />
negative impact on health.<br /><br />
Table xxx presents information on the drinking water of households at the chiefdom level of Vimetco<br /><br />
operational communities. With the exception of households in Kpanda Kemoh chiefdoms that obtain<br /><br />
drinking water from improved sources: 51.2% from tap and 41.5% from protected well/bore hole<br /><br />
respectively, more than sixty percent of the respondents obtains drinking water from non-improved<br /><br />
source: 26.1% from unprotected well/bore hole and 34.4% from river/streams.<br /><br />
<br /><br />
Table A-6<br /><br />
<br /><br />

<br /><br />
Chiefdom<br /><br />

<br /><br />

1.3.1. Who Fetches Water<br /><br />
The traditional image of a woman as a mother and housewife underline a clear-cut division of labor<br /><br />
between men and women. Women perform the bulk of household work. Their domestic responsibilities<br /><br />
include food production, processing, preparation and storage as well as the provision of fuel and water,<br /><br />
sanitation, cleaning the house and laundry. Hence the role of fetching water as it has been revealed by<br /><br />
the respondents (men and women) is clearly the responsibility of women (48.9), men (5%), boys<br /><br />
(37.2%), girls (60.0%), women and girls 1.1%, However, men who do not have wives do the fetching of<br /><br />
water for themselves.<br /><br />
<br /><br />
Environmental and Social Impact Assessment (ESIA)<br /><br />
<br /><br />
Page 149<br /><br />
<br /><br />
1.3.2. Sanitation facilities<br /><br />
Poor sanitation coupled with unsafe water sources increases the risk of water-borne diseases and<br /><br />
illnesses due to poor hygiene in the company’s operational areas. Studies have shown that the absence<br /><br />
of proper toilet facilities and improper disposal of faecal material increases exposure to the risk of<br /><br />
diseases like dysentery, diarrhoea, and typhoid fever. Members of households with improved sanitation<br /><br />
facilities are less likely to contract these communicable diseases.<br /><br />
Figure xxx shows that overall, 54.1% of households had no toilet facility available. This is so particularly<br /><br />
in communities in the Bumpe, Dasse and Kpanda Kemoh Chiefdoms respectively. Others in the Upper<br /><br />
Banta and Lower Banta reported to have access to toilet facility. The most common toilet facility is the<br /><br />
open pit latrines (93.7%), which is more likely to be used by majority of the households.<br /><br />

Economic activity<br /><br />
<br /><br />
Data collected on the main economic activity of household members is presented in table A 9. The Table<br /><br />
shows the limited opportunities available in the chiefdoms. In the communities surveyed, most adults<br /><br />
are engaged in agriculture in their own account as their primary and secondary economic activities. Very<br /><br />
few have business as their main economic activity. In upper Banta there is a greater possibility of<br /><br />
accessing formal employment than the rest of the chiefdoms were Vimetco operations are taking place.<br /><br />
Hunting and collection of forest products are not primary economic activity of any person but is an<br /><br />
important subsidiary in Lower Banta and Upper Banta respectively.<br /><br />
<br /><br />
Data on the use of health facilities are presented in Table xxx below. These show that, overall, 84.9% of<br /><br />
survey households have some form of health care available in the communities. The health care service<br /><br />
provision is provided through the community health centres and clinics with wider provision available to<br /><br />
the population.<br /><br />

When asked whether they have used this health services in the community and for what purpose,<br /><br />
majority of the respondents answered in the affirmative. High levels of these households reported<br /><br />
members of their families had used the vaccination services but there is no information available as to<br /><br />
what vaccinations have been given or whether courses are completed. There have been strong<br /><br />
campaigns to increase vaccination in rural areas and this appears to have been successful.<br /><br />
<br /><br />
A significant proportion of households also indicated using the antenatal check-ups for the number of<br /><br />
births in the survey villages. The high number of reported use of ante-natal check-ups indicates increase<br /><br />
awareness of the risk of home deliveries and high levels of hospital deliveries. Other households<br /><br />
reported that someone was sick.<br /><br />
<br /><br />
Overall, 50.6% of households reported deaths. There is no historical data on sickness or deaths.<br /><br />
Registration of births and deaths is only possible in cities and is not universal even where possible.<br /><br />
Figure A 3 records the family stated cause of death – these reflects the lack of medical diagnosis, the<br /><br />
lack of autopsy facilities and prevalence of belief in witchcraft. There was no sufficient time to<br /><br />
investigate what people really understand by these records and this would make an interesting.<br /><br />

The study further revealed that most of the crops cultivated by farmers are local variety sourced from<br /><br />
market retailers, friends and relatives and proceeds from seed bank. There are instances in which NGOs<br /><br />
working in the communities of the operation area provides seeds to farmers for cultivation.<br /><br />
<br /><br />
Environmental and Social Impact Assessment (ESIA)<br /><br />
<br /><br />
Page 155<br /><br />
<br /><br />
1.5.2. Average Land Size Owned and Used for Crop and ownership<br /><br />
by gender<br /><br />
Land is the basic resource depended upon by a majority of rural communities in Sierra Leone. The<br /><br />
amount of land owned and/or operated varies between communities’ dependence on the availability of<br /><br />
land resources and the nature of social structures governing access to land. In the selected villages of<br /><br />
the five chiefdoms, the average land size owned by individual households ranged from 1.1 to 3.5 ha with<br /><br />
the overall mean land size being 2.4 ha (Table A 21). The main use of the land is crop production. The<br /><br />
main crops that are being grown in these chiefdoms/villages are cassava and rice. In these chiefdoms,<br /><br />
the average land size set for cassava and rice range from 3.0 ha to 3.5 ha with an overall average being<br /><br />
3.25 ha.<br /><br />
In terms of ownership of crops grown by households, both the men and women in the households<br /><br />
participate in cultivation of these crops. The results show greater participation of the gender in crops<br /><br />
such as rice, cassava, groundnuts, vegetables and yam production. Agroforestry crop, such as oil palm,<br /><br />
cocoa and coffee, production activities is a mostly male dominated as reported by 58.1% of the<br /><br />
respondents. Also a significant proportion of the women are engaged in the production of beans and<br /><br />
benni seed.<br /><br />

Across chiefdoms, the overriding purpose of growing crops was for both cash income and household<br /><br />
food supplies. From the table above, overwhelming majority of respondents (92.3%) grow vegetables for<br /><br />
both cash income and household food; 85.3% groundnuts, 83.3% potatoes and 83.5% cassava. For<br /><br />
agroforestry crops, the survey households were divided for both income/cash earner and both.<br /><br />
However, agroforestry crops production were not found in Lower Banta, Bumpe and Dasse Chiefdoms.<br /><br />
<br /><br />
Environmental and Social Impact Assessment (ESIA)<br /><br />
<br /><br />
Page 157<br /><br />
<br /><br />
1.5.3. Loss of land for agriculture and other purposes<br /><br />
Overall, 54.3% of household’s survey revealed that they are likely to affected by the loss of land to the<br /><br />
project, when compared to 45.7% of respondents who said no. The most obvious impacts of this loss of<br /><br />
land to agricultural include:<br /><br />
<br /><br />
<br /><br />
Removal of vegetation cover, will severely alter the availability of food and loss of access to<br /><br />
common resources<br /><br />
<br /><br />
<br /><br />
<br /><br />
Pose environmental and social challenge due to potential disruptions to ecosystems and local<br /><br />
communities<br /><br />
<br /><br />
<br /><br />
<br /><br />
Mud erosion on swamps will lead to loss of swamps<br /><br />
<br /><br />
<br /><br />
<br /><br />
Destroy livelihood activity<br /><br />
<br /><br />
<br /><br />
<br /><br />
Crop yields are affected due to declining soil productivity/soil fertility<br /><br />
<br /><br />
<br /><br />
<br /><br />
Loss of farmland will cause insecurity in farming<br /><br />
<br /><br />
1.5.4. Farming system<br /><br />
Household respondents indicated the major farming systems commonly practiced in the<br /><br />
chiefdoms is mixed cropping (94.9%), mixed farming (80.8%) and sole cropping (57.1%).<br /><br />

Environmental and Social Impact Assessment (ESIA)<br /><br />
<br /><br />
Page 160<br /><br />
<br /><br />
not participate in community meetings revealed that it is as a result of the high level of poverty and<br /><br />
most times meetings are not called. Table A 27 presents the percentage of households having members<br /><br />
attending community work meetings of different types: many households attend more than one type of<br /><br />
meeting. Community gathering/meeting and social/religious work and club are the most commonly<br /><br />
attended. About 43% host political gathering and fewer communities are involved in community<br /><br />
construction works. The high rate declared for women’s attendance at community meeting is interesting<br /><br />
as the team has found that fewer women than men have been observed as attending community<br /><br />
consultation meetings relating the Vimetco Bauxite mining project.<br /><br />

1.6.2. Women’s participation in meetings<br /><br />
Household perceptions of women’s participation (by contributing to decision making) were that an<br /><br />
overwhelming majority of them (96.4%) believed were able to participate in community meetings.<br /><br />
Overall, 63.7% of households believed that it was difficult for women to participate in meetings,<br /><br />
primarily because they had less time available, gender discrimination, farming activities and through<br /><br />
other work to do with childcare and domestic chores that prevented participation of women in<br /><br />
community/public meetings.<br /><br />

1.7.<br /><br />
<br /><br />
General perceptions<br /><br />
<br /><br />
1.7.1. Community needs and concerns<br /><br />
The following common concerns were raised by considerable number of FGDs participants across the<br /><br />
concessionary zone:<br /><br />
<br /><br />
<br /><br />
The benefits received from the mining company is not commensurate to their needs<br /><br />
<br /><br />
Environmental and Social Impact Assessment (ESIA)<br /><br />
<br /><br />
Page 162<br /><br />
<br /><br />
<br /><br />
<br /><br />
Mining in the communities are done in an haphazard manner, especially in the Gbonge (Kpanda<br /><br />
Kemoh Chiefdom) and these was considered unacceptable as it is not economical and<br /><br />
environmentally friendly.<br /><br />
<br /><br />
<br /><br />
<br /><br />
Land owners are not involved in the negotiation process for their land;<br /><br />
<br /><br />
<br /><br />
<br /><br />
Road network in their chiefdoms are deplorable<br /><br />
<br /><br />
<br /><br />
<br /><br />
Lack of knowledge about the acreage assigned as concession zone for the mining operation<br /><br />
<br /><br />
<br /><br />
<br /><br />
Agricultural Development Fund is not beneficial for the farmers; rather it is benefiting only the<br /><br />
<br /><br />
paramount chief and the Ministry of Agriculture, Forestry and Food Security Officials.<br /><br />
<br /><br />
<br /><br />
Frequent delay in the payment of both surface rents and compensation for land and crop loss<br /><br />
<br /><br />
<br /><br />
<br /><br />
No positive impact on the socio-economic status of the affected communities – no alternative<br /><br />
<br /><br />
livelihood opportunities are not provided by the company<br /><br />
<br /><br />
<br /><br />
No proper rehabilitation of mined areas with economic trees<br /><br />
<br /><br />
1.7.2. Perceptions of Environmental Quality<br /><br />
There is no marked difference in the perceptions of the natural environment among the different<br /><br />
communities (figure A 4). Slightly more than sixty percent of the respondents (62.1%) had a negative<br /><br />
opinion about the environmental conditions of the operational communities in the last two years. An<br /><br />
overwhelming majority of respondents (97.2%) in Lower Banta felt that the quality of the environment is<br /><br />
very bad. In all, the communities expressed the deplorable status of health and sanitation hygiene<br /><br />
conditions, deforestation and soil degradation, pollution (air and noise) and health risk resulting from<br /><br />
dust emissions.<br /><br />

1.7.3. Problems with Vimetco operations<br /><br />
When survey respondents were asked whether there were any problems associated with Vimetco<br /><br />
operations in the area, the most commonly reported issue was pollution (mentioned by 87.8% of<br /><br />
respondents), dust (83.3% respondents) and noise (75.0% respondents).<br /><br />

<br /><br />
1.7.4. Advantages of Vimetco operations<br /><br />
When asked whether there are any advantages of Vimetco presence in the area, the most commonly<br /><br />
named benefits were jobs, assistance to the community and benefits to the national economy.<br /><br />
However, small proportion of respondents felt that the company operations will not help provide an<br /><br />
improved infrastructure and facilities such as the road, utilities and recreational facilities; income<br /><br />
diversification opportunities and brighten and modernize the area (Figure A 6). The general perception<br /><br />
of the bauxite mining activities in the chiefdoms was viewed as a curse than a blessing. They said that<br /><br />
the mining activity has not contributed meaningfully to community development initiatives rather than<br /><br />
the destruction of the natural environment. To reverse this trend the community recommends a<br /><br />
thorough review of concession agreements reached to reflect mutual benefits for all relevant<br /><br />
stakeholders in the mining process.<br /><br />

Page 165<br /><br />
<br /><br />
ANNEX – C Summary of issues discussed in FGDs across the surveyed communities<br /><br />
<br /><br />
1. Loss of land used for agricultural and other purposes to bauxite mining by SMHL Vimetco<br /><br />
<br /><br />
The community members expressed willingness to loss their land used for agriculture and other purpose<br /><br />
to bauxite mining operations in their community. They indicated that this can only be possible provided<br /><br />
the following conditions are met by the company: increase surface rent and compensation; provide<br /><br />
training and employment opportunities for the community; provide improved and affordable medical<br /><br />
facilities and prompt and appropriate rehabilitation of the mined out areas for future use.<br /><br />
<br /><br />
2. Surface rent payments for mining land<br /><br />
The surface rents paid to community for mining land was viewed by all affected communities as too<br /><br />
small. While stakeholders generally clamour for the rate of surface rent payments to be increased, some<br /><br />
stakeholders believe that surface rents are disproportionately distributed amongst the different<br /><br />
stakeholder groups. Some argue that the percentage for the Paramount Chiefs is too large; others<br /><br />
believe Local Councils are getting the lion’s share. Some stakeholders would like the involvement of all<br /><br />
stakeholders in deciding the level of surface rent payments. In a focus group discussions or discussion<br /><br />
meetings, the communities expressed that most times these amounts are paid annually and untimely<br /><br />
and often the indirect payments are too bureaucratic. The distribution of surface payment rents across<br /><br />
the communities interviewed is as shown in the table below. Note that while the payment rates are<br /><br />
Environmental and Social Impact Assessment (ESIA)<br /><br />
<br /><br />
Page 166<br /><br />
<br /><br />
established by law, the distribution is determined in consultations among the GOSL (mainly the MLGCD),<br /><br />
Chiefs and Local Councils, although it is not clear how a final decision is made. There is concern that<br /><br />
decisions are made in a subjective and non-transparent manner for the distribution of surface rent<br /><br />
payment. The communities indicated that there is a need to re-negotiate the amount paid per annual<br /><br />
per acre of land and increase on the current amount paid and these monies are to be paid monthly, and<br /><br />
that direct payment should be made to the landowners themselves.<br /><br />
<br /><br />

<br /><br />

<br /><br />

<br /><br />
% of Total payment<br /><br />
<br /><br />
Land owners<br /><br />
<br /><br />

<br /><br />
Paramount Chiefs<br /><br />
<br /><br />

<br /><br />

Source: FGDs meetings 2012<br /><br />
<br /><br />
3. Relocation of villages<br /><br />
The entire affected villages/communities are unwilling to relocate to another community. They however<br /><br />
expressed dissatisfaction over the current status of relocated villages/communities. The participation of<br /><br />
affected people is limited not only because stakeholders are not actively involved in the discussions on<br /><br />
relocation but, more importantly, there is no assessment of the changes in lifestyles and capacities<br /><br />
needed to overcome successfully a major disruption of local families. Participants in a focus group<br /><br />
discussion indicate that in any future village relocation, the company should build modern structures<br /><br />
and provide all socio-cultural structures (such as place of worship, educational and health facilities etc.),<br /><br />
renegotiate compensation fees and provide relief services to the people in the relocated village(s) for<br /><br />
the first three consecutive years after relocation.<br /><br />
Environmental and Social Impact Assessment (ESIA)<br /><br />
<br /><br />
Page 167<br /><br />
<br /><br />
4. Compensation payment and method<br /><br />
The factors that should determine the level of compensation payment for crops destroyed due to<br /><br />
mining activities are:<br /><br />
<br /><br />
<br /><br />
Adopt a procedure to determine the cost of crops/assets involved. This should be done in close<br /><br />
consultation with landowners.<br /><br />
<br /><br />
<br /><br />
<br /><br />
Negotiate directly with the landowner and reach consensus on the amount.<br /><br />
<br /><br />
<br /><br />
<br /><br />
Amount of payment should be commensurate to the crop/asset destroyed.<br /><br />
<br /><br />
<br /><br />
<br /><br />
Develop a framework for compensation payment with the involvement of the local<br /><br />
communities<br /><br />
<br /><br />
Although ministry officials are involved in the crop compensation exercise, crop owners would like the<br /><br />
economic life of the crops to be considered in the evaluation.<br /><br />
<br /><br />
5. Economic and Community Development issues<br /><br />
So far the company has provided the following benefits as reported by the affected communities:<br /><br />
community Barry, good road network, secondary school in the chiefdom, and improved medical<br /><br />
facilities. The participants in the focus group meetings said that the community should address their<br /><br />
concerns; the most common response was to assist with improving community infrastructure and<br /><br />
facilities such as the road, utilities, and recreational facilities. Other recommendations that the company<br /><br />
should provide training for community members provide information on job opportunities and services<br /><br />
and adhere to the highest standards for environmental protection.<br /><br />
<br /><br />
6. Contribution of mining to national economy<br /><br />
The general perception of the bauxite mining activities in the chiefdoms was viewed as a curse than a<br /><br />
blessing. They said that the mining activity has not contributed meaningfully to community development<br /><br />
initiatives rather than the destruction of the natural environment. To reverse this trend the community<br /><br />
recommends a thorough review of concession agreements reached to reflect mutual benefits for all<br /><br />
relevant stakeholders in the mining process.<br /><br />
<br /><br />
Environmental and Social Impact Assessment (ESIA)<br /><br />
<br /><br />
Page 168<br /><br />
<br /><br />
7. Health and Environmental Sanitation Problems:<br /><br />
The communities expressed deplorable status of the health and sanitation hygiene condition of their<br /><br />
communities. The major environmental, health and sanitation concerns resulting from the mining in the<br /><br />
community includes:<br /><br />
<br /><br />
<br /><br />
Pollution<br /><br />
<br /><br />
<br /><br />
<br /><br />
Erosion<br /><br />
<br /><br />
<br /><br />
<br /><br />
Deforestation and soil degradation<br /><br />
<br /><br />
<br /><br />
<br /><br />
Noise<br /><br />
<br /><br />
<br /><br />
<br /><br />
Dust<br /><br />
<br /><br />
<br /><br />
<br /><br />
Health problems – malaria, diarrhoea, high blood pressure and problems associated with ageing.<br /><br />
<br /><br />
<br /><br />
<br /><br />
Problem of sanitation.<br /><br />
<br /><br />
To resolve these problems, the following measures were proposed:<br /><br />
<br /><br />
<br /><br />
Reduce dust emissions using appropriate measures<br /><br />
<br /><br />
<br /><br />
<br /><br />
Develop soil erosion prevention plan<br /><br />
<br /><br />
<br /><br />
<br /><br />
Rehabilitate mined out areas<br /><br />
<br /><br />
<br /><br />
<br /><br />
Provide improved and affordable medical facilities for communities<br /><br />
<br /><br />
<br /><br />
<br /><br />
Provision and maintenance of improved road network in the community<br /><br />
<br /><br />
8. Increase in crime rate<br /><br />
The most common crime in the communities identified by focus group participants are:<br /><br />
<br /><br />
<br /><br />
Thieving<br /><br />
<br /><br />
<br /><br />
<br /><br />
Prostitution<br /><br />
<br /><br />
<br /><br />
<br /><br />
Disregard for local authorities<br /><br />
<br /><br />
9. Issues related to safety and accidents<br /><br />
The major safety and accidents problems reported by the FGDs participants include:<br /><br />
<br /><br />
<br /><br />
Boulders rolling from haulers/dumper trucks<br /><br />
<br /><br />
<br /><br />
<br /><br />
The creation of dams/ponds that does not support aqua-culture due to high concentration of<br /><br />
harmful chemicals<br /><br />
<br /><br />
<br /><br />
<br /><br />
Persistence dust emissions thereby increasing the possibilities of frequent accidents. For<br /><br />
example two accidents reported occurred in Gbonge and Badja respectively<br /><br />
<br /><br />
Environmental and Social Impact Assessment (ESIA)<br /><br />
<br /><br />
Page 169<br /><br />
<br /><br />
With the establishment of Environment and Safety Management within the corporate environment,<br /><br />
health, occupational and safety issues are vigorously addressed. No serious incident of occupational<br /><br />
hazards and safety problems were reported resulting from the dredging operation at the moment.<br /><br />
<br /><br />
10. Community concerns and needs<br /><br />
The following common concerns were raised by considerable number of FGDs participants across the<br /><br />
concessionary zone:<br /><br />
<br /><br />
<br /><br />
The benefits received from the mining company is not commensurate to their needs<br /><br />
<br /><br />
<br /><br />
<br /><br />
Mining in the communities are done in a haphazard manner, especially in the Gbonge (Kpanda<br /><br />
Kemoh Chiefdom) and these was considered unacceptable as it is not economical and<br /><br />
environmentally friendly.<br /><br />
<br /><br />
<br /><br />
<br /><br />
Land owners are not involved in the negotiation process for their land;<br /><br />
<br /><br />
<br /><br />
<br /><br />
Road network in their chiefdoms are deplorable<br /><br />
<br /><br />
<br /><br />
<br /><br />
Lack of knowledge about the acreage assigned as concession zone for the mining operation<br /><br />
<br /><br />
<br /><br />
<br /><br />
Agricultural Development Fund is not beneficial for the farmers; rather it is benefiting only the<br /><br />
paramount chief and the Ministry of Agriculture, Forestry and Food Security Officials.<br /><br />
<br /><br />
<br /><br />
<br /><br />
Frequent delay in the payment of both surface rents and compensation for land and crop loss<br /><br />
<br /><br />
<br /><br />
<br /><br />
No positive impact on the socio-economic status of the affected communities – no alternative<br /><br />
livelihood opportunities are not provided by the company<br /><br />
<br /><br />
<br /><br />
<br /><br />
No proper rehabilitation of mined areas with economic trees<br /><br />
<br /><br />
